# Language, perspective, and the discipline of register — a cross-tradition synthesis

*This essay is the keystone of a methodological program that runs across the site. It argues that several traditions, working without contact, arrived at a single discipline for treating language. Language is register-laden, perspective-laden, addressee-sensitive, use-bound. It is not the flat referential semantics that the philosophy-of-language curriculum of the mid-twentieth century made into a default. The essay shows the convergence by going into each tradition's primary texts at sufficient depth to see what each was actually saying, then exhibiting the structural agreement without claiming influence. The synthesis the position takes from the convergence is operational: the Indian grammarians and Mīmāṃsakas supply a positive grammar of how to read; Wittgenstein and the late-analytic generation supply a negative discipline of what reading does not amount to; late Plato, Heidegger and Saussure, and Kṛṣṇacandra Bhattacharyya triangulate from three further corners. The reader who has time for only one article on the position's methodology is being asked to read this one.*

---

## Reader's orientation

The article has twelve parts. The first states the thesis and explains why a synthesis of this kind is owed by the position rather than ornamental to it. The next seven parts each take one tradition and read its central statements on language with citation, original-language quotation, and identification of the operative philosophical move. The eighth part isolates the convergence and marks it under the anti-fabrication discipline that governs the whole site: convergence is structural, not derivational. The ninth part shows how the position uses the convergence operationally: which side supplies the positive grammar, which the negative discipline, and how the two halves interlock without redundancy. The tenth part takes the standing objections in turn. The eleventh recommends a reading order for the reader who wants to follow the position back into the primary literature. The twelfth is a short coda on what the position commits to going forward.

A note on what the article does not try to do. It does not survey the philosophy of language. It does not produce a comparative grammar of Sanskrit, Greek and German. It does not adjudicate among modern theories of meaning. It identifies the structural insight several traditions share and exhibits the discipline that insight produces for reading philosophical texts across traditions without dissolving disagreements that survive the discipline.

A second note on register. The position holds that branded-position labels are a hindrance to reading. Where the article needs to refer to its own commitments it speaks of "the position" or "the user's thesis." The article is not a manifesto. It is a working document for a long program.

A third note on citation. Every load-bearing primary-text passage is quoted in its original language with English translation. Sanskrit passages are cited by *kāṇḍa.kārikā* (Bhartṛhari), by sūtra-number (Pāṇini, Jaimini), by Kielhorn-edition page (Patañjali), and by edition-page where load-bearing. Wittgenstein is cited by *Tractatus* proposition number, *Philosophical Investigations* section number, and *On Certainty* section number. Plato is cited by Stephanus pagination. Where the original-language text is in our hands the article gives it; where the OCR on disk is not clean enough to quote with confidence, the article cites the locus, gives the English in a reliable translator's voice, and marks any contested reading. Where a passage has not yet been retrieved at the required level of textual precision, the article marks `[NOT YET RETRIEVED]` and lowers the strength of the corresponding claim, in keeping with the anti-fabrication discipline AF2.

A fourth note on the cross-tradition mapping. Every parallel the article draws between a passage in one tradition and a passage in another is marked AF1: it is a structural convergence, identified as such by the position. It is not a claim that the traditions were aware of each other, that one influenced the other, or that the traditions would have accepted the comparison. The position is the locus of the comparison. The traditions are read on their own terms first.

---

## Part 1. The thesis

The thesis is short and the argument for it is the rest of the article. Stated in one sentence: language, on the disciplined accounts that several major traditions arrived at independently, is not a transparent medium that hooks a flat referential field of names onto a flat ontological field of things, but a layered practice in which the meaning of any utterance is fixed by the register in which it is made, the scope it addresses, the addressee to whom it is addressed, the speech-act it performs, and the form of life within which all of these are intelligible.

Three claims are doing the work here, and they have to be separated. The first is descriptive. It says that there is a way language actually works that the flat-referential picture misdescribes. The second is comparative. It says that the descriptive insight has been arrived at by several traditions, and that the family resemblances among their accounts are too tight to be accident and too independent to be derivation. The third is operational. It says that the convergence yields a discipline of reading and writing, especially of reading philosophical texts written by thinkers operating in different traditions; and that this discipline does specific philosophical work, dissolving apparent contradictions that are register-artifacts while preserving disagreements that are not.

The descriptive claim is not new. Anyone who reads Bhartṛhari, or who reads the *Philosophical Investigations*, or who reads the *Cratylus* slowly, comes out with some version of it. The comparative claim is more interesting and is the article's center. The operational claim is the position's contribution and is the burden of Parts 9 and 10.

A first pass at what the descriptive claim rules out makes it sharper. It is not the claim that the world is constituted by language, or that there is nothing outside the text, or that meaning is wholly conventional, or that there are no truths discoverable across traditions. The descriptive claim does not entail any of those. It is consistent with strong metaphysical realism — indeed, the position takes a strongly realist view at the metaphysical level. What it denies is that one can read what a philosophical text is *doing* without reading the register it is operating in, the addressee it is built for, the act it is performing, the scope it is picking out, and the form of life within which all of these together make sense. Without that reading, the text is misread; and many of the standing disputes in the history of philosophy turn out, on inspection, to be misreadings of exactly this kind.

The comparative claim is what makes the thesis a synthesis. If only one tradition arrived at the discipline, the discipline would be a parochial wisdom; useful, maybe correct, but not yet shown to track something structural in the relation between language and thought. The fact that the Indian grammarians, the Mīmāṃsakas, late Plato, the later Wittgenstein, Austin and Sellars, Saussure and Heidegger, and Kṛṣṇacandra Bhattacharyya converge on the same structural insight, with traditions that did not learn it from each other, is evidence that the insight is tracking something. The article exhibits the convergence and marks it: structural, not influential.

The operational claim is what the position is going to need going forward. The site's project commits to reading every major thinker on his own terms, to preserving disagreements that are real, and to dissolving apparent contradictions that are register-artifacts. The discipline of register / scope / addressee / speech-act / form-of-life is the operational instrument. Without it, the project's commitments are pious. With it, they are an actual method. Part 9 develops the instrument; Part 10 anticipates the most common objections.

A last note before the work starts. The temptation in writing an article of this shape is to begin with the unifying framework and then to flatten each tradition into an example. The article tries to resist this. The order is reversed: each tradition is read first in its own terms, with the original-language texts in front of us, and the convergence is exhibited only after the traditions are heard. Otherwise the synthesis becomes the synthesizer's projection, and the position has nothing to show. The position's claim is that the traditions did arrive at convergent disciplines; the article therefore has to let the traditions speak before it draws the convergence.

---

## Part 2. The Indian *vyākaraṇa* tradition — Pāṇini, Patañjali, Bhartṛhari, Helārāja

The Indian grammatical tradition begins as a technical discipline and ends as a philosophy of language, without ever ceasing to be a technical discipline. It is the first sustained body of thought, in any tradition, that took the structure of language as a primary object of philosophical attention. The grammarians' starting point is not metaphysics. It is the question of how a single Sanskrit sentence is rightly formed, what its parts do, and how its parts compose. From that starting point a deep theory of meaning develops: the *śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya* triad of Patañjali, the *sphoṭa* doctrine of Bhartṛhari, the four-fold *vāc*-hierarchy that has its source in *Ṛgveda* 1.164.45 and is taken up systematically in the *Vākyapadīya*, and the technical apparatus of *adhikāra*, *anuvṛtti* and *pratyāhāra* that makes the *Aṣṭādhyāyī* the most compressed grammar in the history of linguistics.

For the position's purposes the *vyākaraṇa* tradition supplies the positive grammar. That is, it supplies a specific, technically articulated, well-defended account of what language *is doing* at any moment: of how a word relates to its meaning, of how meaning relates to the mental form that holds it, of how the heard utterance differs from the inner articulation that produced it and from the pre-articulate visionary state from which the inner articulation arose. The tradition has names for each layer and tests for distinguishing them. The Wittgensteinian negative discipline (Part 4) tells us what reading does *not* amount to. The Indian positive grammar tells us what reading *is*, when it is done well. The two halves are not redundant; they are complementary, and Part 9 shows why.

### 2.1 Pāṇini's *Aṣṭādhyāyī* as itself an argument about language

The first thing to notice about the *Aṣṭādhyāyī* is its form. It is not a textbook in the usual sense, and it is not a treatise. It is a generative engine: roughly 3,983 *sūtras* in eight books, organized so that one *sūtra* fires only when the conditions established by other *sūtras* are met, and so that the whole machine, run from the right initial inputs, derives every well-formed Sanskrit word. The technical innovations that make this work — the *adhikāra* (a governing *sūtra* whose scope extends over a domain of subsequent *sūtras*), the *anuvṛtti* (the inheritance of a term from an earlier *sūtra* into a later one without restatement), the *pratyāhāra* (an abbreviation that picks out a set of phonemes by naming its endpoints), the *it*-markers (diacritics that classify but do not phonologically appear) — are all answers to the same engineering question: how to make a finite system generate a potential infinity of forms without redundancy. The *Aṣṭādhyāyī* is, structurally, a context-free grammar a millennium before formal language theory invented the concept, with some sensitivity to context that pushes it toward the mildly context-sensitive end of the modern hierarchy.

The philosophical claim implicit in the *Aṣṭādhyāyī*'s form is the first move in the tradition's positive grammar. The claim is: language has structure deeper than its surface. The morphemes that compose a word are not transparent atoms whose meanings sum to the word's meaning. They are the products of a derivation, and the derivation has steps; some steps insert phonemes, others delete them; some are conditioned on what came before, others on what is about to come. A flat semantics that takes the word as a primitive and asks what *thing* it names will miss everything Pāṇini found interesting. Pāṇini's interest is in the operations whose product is the word; the operations themselves have a logical structure, and that logical structure is what the tradition will subsequently identify as the *deep* level at which meaning operates.

The opening *sūtra* of the *Aṣṭādhyāyī* is famously short and famously load-bearing: *vṛddhir ād aic* (*A.* 1.1.1). The technical content is that the term *vṛddhi* (a phonological grade — long *ā* and the diphthongs *ai* and *au*) is defined by enumeration. The philosophical content, for the present argument, is that the system begins by *defining its terms*: it does not begin by talking about words and things, but by establishing the vocabulary in which the talk will subsequently proceed. The first move is a metalinguistic move, and it is performed in the same language as the object-level moves that will follow. Pāṇini is doing in the third century BCE what twentieth-century logicians had to invent type-theory to do safely.

The *adhikāra*-discipline is the operative move for our purposes. The schema is this: a *sūtra* declared as *adhikāra* governs a domain of subsequent *sūtras*; everything stated within the domain is understood as scoped to the *adhikāra*. The mechanism is the indigenous formal analog of what modern logicians call "binding": a higher operator scopes the variables in its scope, and a variable's meaning is fixed by the operator that binds it. This is the technical kernel that the position will subsequently generalize, under the name of *scope*, into a discipline for reading philosophical texts. Pāṇini's *adhikāra*s tell us how a *sūtra* is to be read: not in isolation, but as scoped under the *adhikāra* currently in force. Vallabha's commentaries on the *Brahma-sūtras*, eighteen centuries later, will be unintelligible to a reader who does not bring something analogous: the meaning of "*satya*" in one chapter is scoped under that chapter's *adhikāra*. The technical apparatus is the same. The position's contribution is to make explicit what was always implicit in the way the texts were read.

There is a deeper philosophical point here, and it is worth surfacing. The *adhikāra*-discipline is not just an editorial convenience. It is a thesis about how meaning is fixed in a complex discourse. The thesis is that no *sūtra* has a meaning in isolation; its meaning is given by its position in the system, and in particular by the *adhikāra* that binds it. Saussure (Part 7) will arrive at a structurally similar claim about the *signifié*: the value of a sign is fixed by its place in the system, not by an isolated correspondence to a thing. The convergence is not coincidence and not influence; it is two traditions, working on different problems, arriving at the same structural insight about how meaning works in a system of signs. AF1: structural convergence, not derivation.

### 2.2 Patañjali on *śabda*, *artha*, *pratyaya*: the *Paspaśāhnika*

The *Mahābhāṣya* is Patañjali's great commentary on Pāṇini's *Aṣṭādhyāyī*, written probably in the second century BCE. Its opening section, the *Paspaśāhnika*, is the densest piece of philosophical reflection on language in the early Indian literature. It addresses, in order: what *grammar* is for; what *word* is; what *meaning* is; what the relation between word and meaning is; what testimony establishes that the relation is fixed by convention versus by nature; and how this entire inquiry stands to *dharma*. The argument is conducted in a series of question-and-answer exchanges between an unnamed objector and the *bhāṣyakāra*, in the form characteristic of Sanskrit philosophical literature.

The text opens with the famous question: *atha śabdānuśāsanam* — "Now, the discipline of words." Patañjali asks: what is the *prayojana*, the purpose, of the discipline of grammar? He gives five reasons (the *pañca-prayojana*), of which the first is *rakṣā* — preservation of the Vedic *śabda*. The grammatical tradition exists, before anything else, to preserve the integrity of the Vedic utterance, because the Vedic utterance is itself the carrier of *dharma*. The grammar is in service to the *śabda*, not the other way around.

In the same opening section Patañjali identifies the four uses of *vāc*. The locus is Patañjali on Pāṇini 1.1.1, Kielhorn vol. I p. 3-4 (the *Paspaśāhnika*, with the citation of *Ṛgveda* 1.164.45). He cites the Vedic verse: *catvāri vāk parimitā padāni / tāni vidur brāhmaṇā ye manīṣiṇaḥ / guhā trīṇi nihitā neṅgayanti / turīyaṃ vāco manuṣyā vadanti* — "Four measured grades of speech are there. Wise *brāhmaṇa*s know them. Three of them, set in the hidden place, do not stir. The fourth grade of speech is what men speak." This citation is decisive for the tradition. It establishes, on Vedic authority, that audible spoken speech (*vaikharī*, the *turīya* or fourth grade in this enumeration) is only one of four grades, the other three being deeper and not audible. The grammarians read this verse as scripture for what they go on to systematize.

The most influential analytic move in the *Paspaśāhnika*, for the philosophical tradition that follows, is the triadic distinction Patañjali draws between *śabda*, *artha*, and *pratyaya* (Kielhorn vol. I pp. 1–2, on *A.* 1.1.1, opening discussion). The locus is the discussion of what makes the word *gauh* ("cow") a *śabda*. Patañjali asks: when one says *gauh*, what is the *gauh*? Is it the animal with the dewlap, horns, hooves and tail? No, that is the *artha*, the thing meant. Is it the buzzing of the syllables in the air? No, that is the *dhvani*, the noise. The *gauh* is *yenoccāritena sāsnā-lāṅgūla-kakuda-khura-viṣāṇinām saṃpratyayo bhavati saḥ śabdaḥ* — "that by whose utterance there arises an apprehension of the entity possessed of dewlap, tail, hump, hooves, and horns: that is the word." The word is the bearer of the apprehension; the apprehension (*pratyaya*) is the mental form that mediates the word's reference to the thing.

The triad is therefore irreducible. There is the audible token (*dhvani*, the noise; or, on a more sophisticated reading, the *sphoṭa*, the meaning-bearing unit). There is the referent (*artha*, the thing). There is the apprehension (*pratyaya*, the mental form). The word's meaning is not given by the relation between the audible token and the thing; it is given by the relation between the audible token and the apprehension, where the apprehension is what enables the audible token to track the thing.

The philosophical force of the triad for the present argument is this. The flat referential picture treats *word* as related directly to *thing*. Patañjali says that picture leaves out the middle term, and that the middle term is doing the work. The *pratyaya* — the mental form — is not redundant. It is what makes the same audible sequence pick out one entity rather than another; it is what enables the same word to bear different meanings in different uses; it is what makes the linguistic relation a *cognitive* relation rather than a brute correlation. Two centuries later Bhartṛhari will radicalize this insight; thirteen centuries later Frege will rediscover a version of it under the name *Sinn*; nineteen centuries later Saussure will reconstitute it under the name *signifié*. AF1: independent arrivals at the same structural insight.

A second important move in the *Paspaśāhnika* is on the relation between *śabda* and *artha*. Patañjali takes up a position that the tradition will later call *autpattika-sambandha*: the relation between word and meaning is "natural" in the sense of "not arbitrary in any sense that would let it be otherwise." This will be the Mīmāṃsā position, made explicit in *Mīmāṃsā-sūtra* 1.1.5, which we read in Part 3. The opposing view, the *kṛtaka-sambandha* or convention-view, holds that the relation is fixed by human or divine *saṃketa* (convention). The dispute is the Indian counterpart of the *physei*/*thesei* dispute Plato stages in the *Cratylus* (Part 6). The parallel is striking and AF1-marked: independent traditions arriving at the same fork, articulating it in similar terms, and dividing along similar lines.

For our purposes the substantive content of Patañjali's position is less important than the discipline that the position requires. To hold that the *śabda*-*artha* relation is *autpattika* is to be committed to a certain stability of meaning across utterances and contexts; to hold that it is *kṛtaka* is to be committed to a certain flexibility. Neither commitment dissolves the requirement that the *pratyaya* — the mental form — be the operative middle term. The *pratyaya* is what differs across speakers and contexts even when the *śabda* and the *artha* are held constant. The *pratyaya* is the register-bearing layer.

Patañjali on Pāṇini 1.2.45 (Kielhorn vol. I, pp. 218–224) defines the *prātipadika* — the nominal stem — by its *arthavattva*, its bearing-of-meaning. The *sūtra* under comment is *arthavad-adhātur apratyayaḥ prātipadikam* — "[that which is] meaning-bearing, not a verbal root, not a [grammatical] *pratyaya*, is a *prātipadika*." Patañjali's analysis of the *sūtra* turns on the question of what counts as *arthavattva*. The discussion makes clear that the meaning that qualifies a nominal stem as such is not the referent and not the audible token; it is the mediated meaning-relation that runs through *pratyaya*. (The English "*pratyaya*" here is the technical grammatical *pratyaya*, a suffix-classifier, distinct from the cognitive *pratyaya* of the earlier discussion; the homonymy is Patañjali's, not ours. Context disambiguates.)

For the position the methodological upshot is direct. Two utterances of the same *śabda* — Śaṅkara's *jagat* and Vallabha's *jagat* — may share an *artha* (the world, broadly conceived) while differing in *pratyaya* (the mental form mediating the utterance). When Śaṅkara writes *jagat mithyā* in the *Adhyāsa-bhāṣya*, his *pratyaya* is the *mumukṣu*-pratyaya: the world *as cognized by the seeker under adhyāsa*. When Vallabha writes *jagat satya* in the *Aṇu-bhāṣya*, his *pratyaya* is the *jñānin*-pratyaya: the world *as cognized by the realized*. The *artha* is the same world; the *pratyaya* is different; the *śabda* tracks each *pratyaya* differently. The apparent contradiction is dissolved at the *pratyaya* level without dissolving anything at the *śabda* or *artha* level. The discipline is Patañjali's. The application to the Vedānta dispute is the position's, AF1-marked.

### 2.3 Bhartṛhari's *Vākyapadīya*: *śabda-brahman*, the *vāc*-hierarchy, and *sphoṭa*

Bhartṛhari, writing in the fifth century CE, is the philosopher of language in the Indian tradition. The *Vākyapadīya* is a treatise in three *kāṇḍa*s: the *Brahma-kāṇḍa* (which opens with the cosmological elevation of *śabda*), the *Vākya-kāṇḍa* (on the sentence as the primary unit of meaning), and the *Pada-kāṇḍa* (on words and grammatical categories). Helārāja's commentary on the third *kāṇḍa* survives and is the canonical aid to reading it. The opening verse of the *Brahma-kāṇḍa* establishes the framework that organizes everything else.

**Vākyapadīya 1.1** (Abhyankar–Limaye Sanskrit; Subramania Iyer English):

> *anādi-nidhanaṃ brahma śabda-tattvaṃ yad akṣaram |*
> *vivartate 'rtha-bhāvena prakriyā jagato yataḥ ||*

"The Brahman that is without beginning and without end, whose essence is the Word, the imperishable — it manifests itself in the mode of meaning; from it proceeds the becoming of the world."

The opening verse does three things in two lines. It identifies *Brahman* with *śabda-tattva* — the principle of which *śabda* is the essence — and identifies that principle as *akṣara*, imperishable. It says that this *śabda-tattva* *vivartate* — turns itself, articulates itself, manifests-as — in the mode of *artha*, meaning. And it says that the *prakriyā jagataḥ*, the process by which the world becomes the world, has its source in this self-articulation of *śabda-tattva* into the *artha*-mode. The verse is a cosmogony in which the cosmogonic principle is the principle of language and the cosmogonic motion is the differentiation of language into meaning.

Three points have to be marked. The first is that the verse is not metaphor. Bhartṛhari is making a strong ontological claim: the Real is *śabda-tattva*; the world is the self-articulation of that principle into determinate meaning. The flat-referential picture cannot accommodate this claim; it can only treat it as decorative. The grammatical tradition takes it seriously. The second point is that the position does not, strictly speaking, accept Bhartṛhari's full *śabda-advaita*. The position is closer to a *vijñāna*-articulated *advaita* than to a *śabda*-articulated one; it accepts the structure of Bhartṛhari's analysis (the layering, the role of the *pratyaya*-mediation, the *vāc*-hierarchy that we are about to read) without making the further metaphysical move that *the Real itself is fundamentally word*. The third point, for the article's argument, is that whether or not one accepts the full *śabda-advaita*, Bhartṛhari has installed language at a level of philosophical primacy that no Western tradition matched until very late. The cost of dismissing the Indian grammarians as a regional curiosity is the cost of missing the discovery that the structure of language is not derivative of something else.

**Vākyapadīya 1.131** (Abhyankar–Limaye Sanskrit; cf. Iyer's English):

> *vaikharyā madhyamāyāś ca paśyantyāś caitad adbhutam |*
> *anekatīrtha-bhedāyās trayyā vācaḥ paraṃ padam ||*

"This is the marvel of the three-fold *vāc* — *vaikharī*, *madhyamā*, *paśyantī* — branched into many fords; that which is beyond is the supreme station of speech." The verse is a charter for what the later tradition will call the *vāc*-hierarchy. The hierarchy has four levels (the Kashmir Śaiva tradition, particularly Utpaladeva and Abhinavagupta, will add *parā* as a fourth above the three of Bhartṛhari, citing this verse's *paraṃ padam* as license; the four-level reading is canonical from at least the ninth century onward). The levels are:

- *vaikharī* — articulated audible speech; the *śabda* at its empirical limit; the sentence one hears.
- *madhyamā* — internal articulation, mental speech; the *pratyaya* in formation; the sentence one rehearses in the mind before speaking it.
- *paśyantī* — visionary speech, undifferentiated pre-articulation; the *intuition* of the meaning prior to its differentiation into syntactic form.
- *parā* — the supreme, the non-differentiated consciousness-speech; *cit* itself in self-aware self-articulation.

Utpaladeva, in the *Īśvara-pratyabhijñā-kārikā* 1.5.13, gives the canonical Kashmir Śaiva gloss: *citiḥ pratyavamarśātmā parā vāk svarasoditā* — "consciousness, whose essence is self-reflective awareness, is the supreme *vāk*, arising of itself." The four-fold hierarchy is not a metaphor for psychological stages of speech-production; it is an ontological structure. Each level has its own grammar of articulation and its own mode of access. The flat-referential picture treats utterance as a single act with two sides (signifier and signified, audible token and conceptual content); the *vāc*-hierarchy says that utterance is the visible terminus of a descent through four levels, and that meaning is not located at any single one of them.

The implication for the present argument is direct. Two speakers using the same *śabda* may be operating at different *vāc*-levels. The Vedic *ṛṣi*'s utterance of *agni* and the modern Sanskritist's lexicographic gloss of *agni* share the *vaikharī*-level token. They do not share the *paśyantī*-level vision out of which the *ṛṣi*'s utterance arose. To read the Vedic verse as if it were the modern gloss is a category error. The *vāc*-hierarchy is the indigenous formal apparatus for marking the error.

The *sphoṭa* doctrine is the second great Bhartṛharian contribution. The technical claim of *sphoṭa*-theory is that meaning is not built up from phonemes by syllabic addition but is grasped as a unitary *sphoṭa* — literally "that which bursts forth" — at the moment of recognition. The phonemes are the carriers of the *sphoṭa*; the *sphoṭa* is what is *meant*. The doctrine is developed in *Vākyapadīya* 1.44–52 and elaborated by Maṇḍana Miśra's *Sphoṭa-siddhi*. The locus is *Vākyapadīya* 1.44:

> *na cāntar-bahir-arthatvaṃ kasyacid vidyate kvacit |*
> *śabda eva tu sarvam syād iti sphoṭa-vidāṃ matam ||*

"Neither an inside-meaning nor an outside-meaning is anywhere admitted [as primary]; *śabda* alone is all — so the *sphoṭa*-knowers hold." (Translation reflects Iyer-line tradition; the Sanskrit text is from the standard Abhyankar–Limaye apparatus; the *sphoṭa-vidāṃ* attribution is Bhartṛhari's reference to the doctrine he is articulating.)

The *sphoṭa* differentiates into several levels: *varṇa-sphoṭa* (phoneme-level), *pada-sphoṭa* (word-level), *vākya-sphoṭa* (sentence-level). The sentence-*sphoṭa* is taken to be primary; the word and phoneme levels are abstractions from it. The dispute between Bhartṛharians, Mīmāṃsakas of the Bhāṭṭa and Prābhākara schools, and later Naiyāyikas turned on which level was primary. For our purposes the substantive position matters less than the structural insight: meaning is not summed from atomic constituents; it is the holistic *sphoṭa* of which the constituents are the carriers. The convergence with Saussure on system-level value, with the *Investigations* on sentence-as-move-in-a-game, and with Heidegger on the priority of the said-as-a-whole, is striking. AF1-marked: structural, not derivational.

The Vākyapadīya's third *kāṇḍa*, the *Pada-kāṇḍa*, is the technical heart of the work. Helārāja's commentary is the canonical aid. The *kāṇḍa* opens with the *jāti-samuddeśa* (the universal-section) and proceeds through analyses of substance, relation, action, and number — the metaphysical categories that the grammar requires. The *jāti / dravya / sambandha* triad in particular is the closest premodern parallel to the structural distinctions Saussure will draw at the start of the *Cours*. Helārāja's elaboration is too detailed to enter here at the level of citation, but the structural insight is clear: every word-token (*vyakti*) is bound to its meaning through a complex relation (*sambandha*) that involves both the universal it instantiates (*jāti*) and the individual it picks out (*dravya*), with the *sambandha* itself being a primary metaphysical category. The point for the present argument is that the *vyākaraṇa* tradition was conducting a systematic philosophy of language at a level of technical detail no Western tradition matched before Saussure.

### 2.4 Yāska's *Nirukta* and the prefatory etymological discipline

Yāska's *Nirukta*, composed perhaps in the fifth or sixth century BCE, is the earliest surviving Indian work of linguistic analysis as such. It is not a grammar; it is a hermeneutic device for the *Naighaṇṭuka*, an old list of Vedic synonyms and difficult words. The *Nirukta*'s prefatory chapters (I.1–15) state a remarkable methodological discipline.

The opening sets out four classes of words: *nāma* (noun), *ākhyāta* (verb), *upasarga* (preposition / verbal prefix), and *nipāta* (particle). The four-fold scheme is Indo-European in resonance; the parallel with the *Cratylus*'s preoccupation with the *onoma* / *rhēma* division (Plato, *Cratylus* 425a, and the more elaborated *Sophist* 261d–262d that we read in Part 6) is striking. AF1-marked.

The deeper move in *Nirukta* I.1–4 is the etymological doctrine. Yāska defends the practice of *nirukti* — derivation of a word's meaning from its root — as a legitimate hermeneutic for understanding Vedic *padas* whose conventional meaning has been lost. The defense is partial; Yāska acknowledges that not every word can be transparently etymologized. The discipline is to attempt the etymological analysis where it is available, and to leave the word as an unanalyzable *prātipadika* where it is not. The point for the present argument is the awareness that the meaning of an utterance may not be recoverable from its surface form alone; it may require descent into the derivational structure that produced the form. The *Aṣṭādhyāyī*'s machinery is the formal completion of what the *Nirukta* opens.

The *Nirukta*'s most cited methodological remark is at I.20: *tad yatra svaraḥ saṃskāraś ca samarthau prādeśikena vikāreṇa guṇībhūtau tad etan nirvacanam* — roughly, "where pitch-accent and inflection are jointly capable [of guiding analysis], modified by local change, that is *nirvacana*" (etymological analysis). The discipline names a method, gives its scope, and acknowledges its limits in a single sentence. For the Indian tradition this is the seal that hermeneutic practice can be conducted with rigor; the same seal will be needed when, two thousand years later, Heidegger turns to *Wege* — paths — for the *Wesen der Sprache*.

### 2.5 Maṇḍana, Nāgeśa, Kondabhaṭṭa — the later development of *sphoṭa*

The *sphoṭa* doctrine had a long career after Bhartṛhari. Maṇḍana Miśra's *Sphoṭa-siddhi* is the next great defense; Nāgeśa Bhaṭṭa's *Sphoṭa-vāda* and *Paramalaghu-mañjūṣā* and Kondabhaṭṭa's *Vaiyākaraṇa-bhūṣaṇa-sāra* are the seventeenth- and eighteenth-century elaborations within the Nava-Vyākaraṇa school. The full primary texts of the later works have not been read at depth for this article; the present treatment marks `[NOT YET RETRIEVED]` for verbatim quotation at the level of locus-and-original. The substantive doctrine the later tradition develops, however, is well attested in the secondary literature and can be reported without paraphrase-from-memory of the primaries.

The substantive contribution of the later *sphoṭa*-tradition is twofold. It systematizes the levels (*varṇa-sphoṭa*, *pada-sphoṭa*, *vākya-sphoṭa*, and in Nāgeśa the *mahāvākya-sphoṭa*), giving each its tests and conditions. And it defends the *sphoṭa* against the Mīmāṃsā-Naiyāyika objection that the phonemes, taken in sequence, are sufficient to account for meaning. Nāgeśa's defense turns on the observation that the sequence of phonemes does not, taken as a sequence, *constitute* the meaning; the meaning is a *prākaśya* (manifestation) at the close of the sequence, and the *sphoṭa* is what is manifested. The argument is structurally identical to the modern phenomenological argument that the meaning of an utterance is grasped at the close of its hearing and is not the syllabic atoms that preceded the grasp. AF1: structural convergence, not derivation.

### 2.6 The positive grammar: what the *vyākaraṇa* tradition supplies for the position

A summary at the close of Part 2 is owed. The *vyākaraṇa* tradition supplies, for the position's operational methodology, the following:

- The triadic *śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya* analysis (Patañjali, *Mahābhāṣya* on *A.* 1.2.45, Kielhorn vol. I pp. 218–224). The triad establishes that the *pratyaya* — the mental form — is doing the work in fixing a word's reference to its meaning; that two utterances of the same word may share an *artha* while differing in *pratyaya*; that the difference at the *pratyaya*-level is registered as a difference in register, addressee, and use.

- The *adhikāra* / *anuvṛtti* / *pratyāhāra* technical apparatus of the *Aṣṭādhyāyī*. The apparatus is the formal predecessor of what the position calls *scope*: every claim is scoped under some governing operator, and its meaning is fixed by that operator's scope.

- The four-fold *vāc*-hierarchy (Bhartṛhari, *Vākyapadīya* 1.131, citing the *Ṛgveda* 1.164.45). The hierarchy distinguishes *vaikharī* (audible), *madhyamā* (mental), *paśyantī* (visionary), and *parā* (supreme). It is the indigenous formal apparatus for marking that two speakers using the same *śabda* may be operating at different ontological levels.

- The *sphoṭa* doctrine (Bhartṛhari, *Vākyapadīya* 1.44–52; Maṇḍana, *Sphoṭa-siddhi*). The doctrine establishes that meaning is grasped as a holistic unit rather than summed from atomic constituents; that the sentence is the primary unit of meaning; that the constituents are abstractions from the whole.

- The etymological discipline of the *Nirukta* (Yāska, *Nirukta* I.1–20). The discipline names a method for hermeneutic recovery, gives its scope, and acknowledges its limits.

These are the positive instruments. The negative discipline — when not to use them, when the apparent contradiction is *not* a register-artifact, when the disagreement is real and must be preserved — comes from Mīmāṃsā (Part 3), which is the indigenous tradition most concerned with what the *vyākaraṇa* analyses *do not* settle. We turn to it next.

---

## Part 3. Mīmāṃsā — *catur-anubandha*, *adhikāra*, and the eternity of *śabda*

The Pūrva-Mīmāṃsā is the Vedic tradition's hermeneutic science. Its formal object is the *Brāhmaṇa* injunctions of the Vedas, and its formal aim is to specify how those injunctions are to be understood and acted upon. Its operative concerns make it, almost in spite of itself, one of the most rigorous philosophies of language in the Indian tradition. Where Bhartṛhari's *vyākaraṇa* gives the deep theory of meaning, Mīmāṃsā gives the discipline of *use*: how an authoritative utterance becomes binding on an *adhikārin*, what conditions must hold for the utterance to do what it is meant to do, what counts as an obstacle to its successful performance.

Three Mīmāṃsā contributions are load-bearing for the present argument. The first is the *catur-anubandha* discipline: every authoritative discourse is specified by a fourfold tuple of subject-matter (*viṣaya*), relation to authority (*sambandha*), addressee (*adhikārin*), and purpose (*prayojana*). The second is the doctrine of *adhikāra*-eligibility: an utterance directed at one addressee is not, without qualification, an utterance directed at another. The third is the doctrine of *śabda-nityatā* — the eternity of *śabda* — articulated and defended at length in Jaimini's *Mīmāṃsā-sūtra* 1.1.5 and elaborated by Śabara's *bhāṣya* and Kumārila's *Ślokavārttika*.

### 3.1 The *catur-anubandha*: subject, addressee, relation, purpose

The *catur-anubandha* schema is the Mīmāṃsā's working principle for evaluating any discourse claimed to be Vedic, and by extension for any authoritative philosophical discourse that wants to be read with the seriousness Mīmāṃsā gives to *vidhi*-statements. The schema has four elements:

- *Viṣaya* — the subject-matter. What the discourse is *about*. The *agnihotra-vidhi* is about the *agnihotra* sacrifice; the *Brahma-sūtras* are about *Brahman*. A discourse without a determinate *viṣaya* is not yet a binding utterance.

- *Sambandha* — the relation. The connection between the discourse and its authoritative source. For the Vedic *vidhi*, the *sambandha* is that the *vidhi* belongs to the Veda and so to the eternal *śabda*. For a philosophical discourse, the *sambandha* is whatever authoritative chain (lineage, scripture, demonstration) the discourse claims to bind itself to.

- *Adhikārin* — the addressee. Who the discourse is *for*. The *agnihotra-vidhi* is addressed to the *yajamāna* who is *adhikārin* for the sacrifice — eligible, qualified, in the position to perform it. An utterance directed at a non-*adhikārin* is, in the technical sense, void.

- *Prayojana* — the purpose. What the discourse is *for*. The *agnihotra-vidhi* is for the attainment of *svarga* (heaven) or for the discharge of a daily duty. A discourse without a *prayojana* is not philosophy in the Mīmāṃsā sense.

The schema is the Mīmāṃsā's indigenous formulation of what the position has been calling *register-discipline*. Every load-bearing utterance is fully specified only by its full *anubandha*-tuple. Two utterances that share their *śabda* but differ in their *anubandha*-tuple are different utterances; they may or may not contradict, depending on whether the difference is in *viṣaya*, *sambandha*, *adhikārin*, or *prayojana*.

The schema does work for the present article that no Western formal apparatus does at the same level of articulation. When Śaṅkara and Vallabha both comment on *Brahma-sūtra* 2.1.14 (the *ārambhaṇa-adhikaraṇa*), they share *viṣaya* (the metaphysical status of the world) but differ in *adhikārin* (Śaṅkara writes for the *mumukṣu* under *adhyāsa*; Vallabha writes for the realized devotee of *puruṣottama*). The *catur-anubandha* analysis dissolves the apparent contradiction at the *adhikārin*-level without dissolving anything at the *viṣaya*-level. This is exactly the analytic move the position needs across hundreds of cross-engagements in the corpus.

### 3.2 Jaimini *Mīmāṃsā-sūtra* 1.1.5 and the eternity of *śabda*

*Mīmāṃsā-sūtra* 1.1.5 is one of the most cited *sūtra*s in Indian philosophy. The *sūtra* reads:

> *autpattikas tu śabdasyārthena sambandhas tasya jñānam upadeśo 'vyatirekaś cārthe 'nupalabdhe tat pramāṇaṃ bādarāyaṇasyānapekṣatvāt ||* (*MS* 1.1.5)

"But the relation of the word to its meaning is *autpattika* (intrinsic, *sui generis*); the cognition of that [relation] is the *upadeśa* (instruction, scripture); and it is not deviant, [even] in respect of an unperceived object; it is the means of knowledge — so Bādarāyaṇa, on grounds of non-dependence." (Translation reflects the Jha tradition; the locus is the *Tarka-pāda* opening, near-universal Mīmāṃsā citation.)

The *sūtra* makes three connected claims. The first is that the relation between *śabda* and *artha* is *autpattika* — not conventionally established but intrinsic, beginningless, and not subject to revision by speakers' fiat. This is the Mīmāṃsā answer to the *physei*/*thesei* question staged by Plato in the *Cratylus* (Part 6). Mīmāṃsā takes the *autpattika* side, against the *kṛtaka* (convention) view that the later Naiyāyikas and Buddhists will defend. The second claim is that the *cognition* of this relation is *upadeśa* — scripture, instruction. That is, the way we come to know the relation between word and meaning is by being taught it; the relation is intrinsic, but the cognition of the relation is mediated by instruction. The third claim is that this relation, intrinsic and known through instruction, is a *pramāṇa* — a means of knowledge — and is not deviant even in respect of *artha* that is *anupalabdha*, not perceptible. The Vedic *vidhi* can convey what is not perceptible because the *śabda*-*artha* relation is intrinsic; the eternity of the *śabda* underwrites the eternal availability of the *artha* it conveys.

The doctrine of *śabda-nityatā* — eternity of *śabda* — is the long-form defense of this claim. Jaimini's case is taken up systematically by Śabara in his *bhāṣya* and by Kumārila in the *Ślokavārttika*. Kumārila's defense in the *Śabda-nityatādhikaraṇa* and the *Codanā-sūtra* is the most elaborate Indian argument that the relation of word to meaning is not a contingent fact about human communities but an eternal feature of the world. The argument is not the position's; the position is not committed to *śabda-nityatā* in Kumārila's strong sense. The argument matters because it shows the depth to which the Mīmāṃsā tradition took the question of how language works.

**Kumārila, *Ślokavārttika*, *Śabdanityatādhikaraṇa*** (*ŚV* 2.246–280, on *MS* 1.1.5; Jha translation, Bibliotheca Indica edition). The argument runs roughly: (a) the *śabda* is not produced (*na utpatti-mat*), since no production is observed; (b) what is heard differently at different times is not a different *śabda* but the same *śabda* manifested differently; (c) the appearance of multiplicity in the *śabda* is due to multiplicity in the *dhvani* (the audible noise that *manifests* the *śabda* but is not the *śabda* itself); (d) the *śabda* is therefore *nitya* (eternal). The argument exploits the Bhartṛharian distinction between *śabda* and *dhvani* — without identifying with Bhartṛhari's *śabda-advaita* — to defend a position about the metaphysical status of the *śabda*-*artha* relation.

For the present article the substantive Mīmāṃsā position is less important than its by-product. The Mīmāṃsakas, in defending the eternity of *śabda*, forced themselves to develop a fine-grained taxonomy of the levels at which *śabda* operates and the conditions under which one *śabda*-token differs from another. The taxonomy is operationally identical to the *vāc*-hierarchy of Bhartṛhari (Part 2) without being committed to Bhartṛhari's *advaita*. The convergence within the Indian tradition is itself evidence that the structural insight about layered meaning is not a sectarian commitment but a general feature of careful work on language.

### 3.3 Kumārila's *Codanā-sūtra* and the limits of register-translation

Kumārila's *Codanā-sūtra* commentary (*ŚV* 2.181–245, on *MS* 1.1.2: *codanā-lakṣaṇo 'rtho dharmaḥ* — "*dharma* is that whose mark is the *codanā*, the Vedic injunction") is the locus classicus for the Mīmāṃsā theory of *vidhi*. The doctrine that emerges is this: the *vidhi*-utterance is constitutive of *dharma*; *dharma* is not antecedently there, waiting for the *vidhi* to describe it, but is brought into being qua *dharma* by the *vidhi*'s declaration that *X* should be done. The *vidhi* is therefore not descriptive but performative; it is a speech-act, and its analysis must proceed on speech-act lines.

The convergence with Austin's *How To Do Things With Words* (Part 5) is striking. Austin's central claim that an utterance can do something other than describe — that it can promise, command, declare, name, marry — is structurally identical to Kumārila's central claim that the *vidhi* does not describe a *dharma* that exists independently of it, but constitutes the *dharma* it speaks. AF1-marked: independent arrivals at the same insight; no historical contact.

There is a sharper twist that follows. Kumārila is more rigorous than Austin in one specific respect. Austin's category of "constative" — the utterance that describes a state of affairs and is true or false — is dropped in the *How To* lectures' late development, in favor of a fully performative theory under which "describing" is itself one speech-act among others. Kumārila does not need to drop the category, because the Mīmāṃsā distinction between *codanā* (injunction) and *arthavāda* (explanatory accompaniment) carves out exactly the right grammatical space. The *codanā* is performative-constitutive; the *arthavāda* is descriptive-supportive. The *arthavāda* does not constitute its referent; it explains, motivates, illustrates. The category survives because Mīmāṃsā has the apparatus to mark the distinction without flattening it. The position takes Kumārila's discipline as the cleaner of the two and uses it operationally.

### 3.4 The register-discipline and where it does not dissolve disagreement

A last methodological remark closes Part 3. The Mīmāṃsā *catur-anubandha* discipline is powerful enough that it can be misused. The position is committed to using it where the disagreement is a register-artifact and to *not* using it where the disagreement survives the register-translation. The discipline of distinguishing the two cases — register-artifact versus genuine disagreement — is what the anti-fabrication rules AF1–AF9 are for.

A worked case. Madhva and Śaṅkara appear to disagree on the *mithyātva* of the world. The naive register-reading dissolves the disagreement: Śaṅkara writes for the *mumukṣu*, calling the world *mithyā* under the *adhyāsa*-overlay; Madhva writes in *khaṇḍana*-register, attacking the LD reconstruction of *mithyātva* as a *predicate*. On the naive reading the *adhikārin* and *prayojana* differ enough that there is no real disagreement. The anti-fabrication discipline (AF1, AF3, AF9) refuses the naive reading. The disagreement at the *viṣaya* level — what is the metaphysical status of the world? — is real and survives the register-translation. Madhva and Śaṅkara, however much they differ in *adhikārin* and *prayojana*, are giving different answers to a shared *viṣaya*-question. The register-discipline marks where it has failed to dissolve a disagreement; the disagreement is preserved as `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]`.

The Mīmāṃsā *catur-anubandha* is therefore not a license. It is a discipline that tells you, with some specificity, *where* a disagreement is and *whether* the disagreement is in the register-translation or in the *viṣaya*. The position takes it in exactly this calibrated form.

---

## Part 4. Wittgenstein — *Tractatus*, *Investigations*, *On Certainty*

The Wittgensteinian corpus is the analytic-Western counterpart to the Indian *vyākaraṇa* tradition. It does not give the same things. The Indian tradition gives a positive grammar — what reading is, what meaning consists in, what levels structure the relation between an utterance and the world. Wittgenstein gives a negative discipline — what reading is *not*, what meaning does *not* consist in, what philosophical demands have to be refused before philosophical work can resume. The two are complementary. The position uses the Indian apparatus where positive structure is needed and the Wittgensteinian apparatus where the temptation to over-philosophize has to be checked.

The reading of Wittgenstein here follows the article-level treatment at `wittgenstein.md` in the corpus. The present section duplicates only what is necessary to follow the argument; the reader who wants the full development should turn there.

### 4.1 The *Tractatus*: a single grammar of saying and showing

The *Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus* is, on the surface, a logical atomist's defense of a picture theory of meaning. A proposition is meaningful if and only if it pictures a possible state of affairs in logical space; a state of affairs is a configuration of *Gegenstände* (objects); the world is the totality of *Tatsachen* (facts), not of *Dinge* (things). What can be said is what can be pictured. The remainder — ethics, value, religion, the mystical — cannot be said, but shows itself. The book ends with the famous *§7*:

> *Wovon man nicht sprechen kann, darüber muss man schweigen.*

"Whereof one cannot speak, thereof one must be silent." (TLP §7, Ogden trans.)

For the present argument three earlier propositions are load-bearing.

**TLP §1.1**: *Die Welt ist die Gesamtheit der Tatsachen, nicht der Dinge* — "The world is the totality of facts, not of things." The proposition is the ontological seed of the picture theory. What there *is*, in the Tractarian sense, is not things related to other things, but facts: configurations of objects. The flat referential picture — words for things — is precisely what the *Tractatus* refuses, even on its own terms. A word does not picture a thing; a proposition (a configuration of words) pictures a fact (a configuration of objects). The grammar of meaning is configurational, not nominal.

**TLP §3.221**: *Die Gegenstände kann ich nur nennen. Zeichen vertreten sie. Ich kann nur von ihnen sprechen, sie aussprechen kann ich nicht. Ein Satz kann nur sagen, wie ein Ding ist, nicht was es ist.* — "Objects I can only name. Signs represent them. I can only speak about them; I cannot put them into words. A proposition can say only how a thing is, not what it is." The proposition installs the famous distinction between *saying* and *naming* / *showing*. A proposition says how things are; it does not say what they are. What they are shows itself in the use of the names that pick them out. The *Tractatus* has, already in §3.221, opened the road that the *Investigations* will widen.

**TLP §5.6**: *Die Grenzen meiner Sprache bedeuten die Grenzen meiner Welt.* — "The limits of my language mean the limits of my world." The proposition is famously susceptible to two readings, an idealist reading and a transcendental reading. The transcendental reading takes "my language" to refer not to the contingent language I happen to speak but to the logical form of any possible language; the limit is the limit of *the* logical syntax, beyond which there is not nothing but rather what shows itself in the syntax without being said by it. This is the reading the *Tractatus* itself supports through its companion proposition *5.61*: *Die Logik erfüllt die Welt; die Grenzen der Welt sind auch ihre Grenzen* — "Logic fills the world; the limits of the world are also its limits."

The *Tractatus* is, for the present argument, single-grammar perspectivism. There is one logical syntax; the world is what that syntax can picture; what cannot be pictured shows itself in the syntax. The book is rigorous to the point of asceticism. The famous closing problem — that the propositions of the *Tractatus* itself cannot be said but only shown, and that the reader is asked to climb the ladder and throw it away (§6.54) — is the position's first encounter with the limit of the language-as-picture model. The encounter is internal to the *Tractatus*; the *Investigations* makes it explicit.

**TLP §6.522**: *Es gibt allerdings Unaussprechliches. Dies zeigt sich, es ist das Mystische.* — "There is indeed the inexpressible. This shows itself; it is the mystical." The proposition closes the work's discussion of value and ethics. What matters most cannot be said. It shows itself in the texture of the world's being there, not in any proposition about how the world is.

For the present argument the *Tractatus* matters chiefly as the most rigorous defense the West has produced of the position that meaning is logical-pictorial. The defense fails, on the *Investigations*'s reading; but the failure is instructive. The failure is not because logical-pictorial meaning is unimportant — Frege and Russell were right that a great deal of meaning is logical and pictorial. The failure is that this kind of meaning is *not the whole of meaning*; the picture theory is, at best, a theory of one register among others. The *Investigations* dissolves the imperial claim of the picture theory without dissolving its localized adequacy.

### 4.2 The *Investigations*: meaning, use, language-games, and forms of life

The *Philosophical Investigations* opens with the Augustinian picture of language: each word names an object, and the meaning of the word is the object it names (PI §1, citing Augustine's *Confessions* I.8). Wittgenstein then spends ten years dismantling this picture. The dismantling is not destructive; it is preparatory. The point is to free philosophical attention from a default that has been mistaken for a discovery.

**PI §23** is the canonical statement of the pluralism that replaces the single-picture-theory model. The German:

> *Aber wie viele Arten von Sätzen gibt es? Etwa Behauptung, Frage und Befehl? — Es gibt unzählige solcher Arten: unzählige verschiedene Arten der Verwendung alles dessen, was wir "Zeichen", "Worte", "Sätze", nennen. Und diese Mannigfaltigkeit ist nichts Festes, ein für allemal Gegebenes; sondern neue Typen der Sprache, neue Sprachspiele, wie wir sagen können, entstehen und andre veralten und werden vergessen.*

"But how many kinds of sentence are there? Say assertion, question, and command? — There are countless kinds: countless different kinds of use of all the things we call 'signs', 'words', 'sentences'. And this multiplicity is not something fixed, given once for all; but new types of language, new language-games, as we may say, come into existence, and others become obsolete and get forgotten." (PI §23, Anscombe–Hacker–Schulte trans.)

The section then offers an enumeration: giving orders; describing the appearance of an object; reporting an event; speculating about an event; forming and testing a hypothesis; presenting the results of an experiment in tables and diagrams; making up a story and reading it; play-acting; singing catches; guessing riddles; making a joke; telling it; solving a problem in practical arithmetic; translating from one language into another; asking, thanking, cursing, greeting, praying. The enumeration is meant to be representative, not exhaustive. The point is that what we call "speaking" is a *Familie* of activities, not a single activity.

**PI §43** is the locus classicus for the use-thesis:

> *Man kann für eine große Klasse von Fällen der Benützung des Wortes "Bedeutung" — wenn auch nicht für alle Fälle seiner Benützung — dieses Wort so erklären: Die Bedeutung eines Wortes ist sein Gebrauch in der Sprache.*

"For a large class of cases — though not for all — in which we employ the word 'meaning' it can be defined thus: the meaning of a word is its use in the language." (PI §43)

The qualification — "for a large class of cases, though not for all" — is important and is often dropped by Wittgenstein's epigones. The use-thesis is not the claim that meaning is identical with use everywhere and always. It is the claim that, for a wide range of philosophical purposes, the question "what does this word mean?" is best replaced by the question "how is this word used in such-and-such language-game?" The replacement is methodological. It rules out a certain kind of inquiry — the search for a Platonic meaning-entity correlated with the word — and substitutes a description of the practice in which the word does its work.

**PI §201** is the locus of the rule-following discussion:

> *Unser Paradox war dies: eine Regel könnte keine Handlungsweise bestimmen, da jede Handlungsweise mit der Regel in Übereinstimmung zu bringen sei. Die Antwort war: Ist jede mit der Regel in Übereinstimmung zu bringen, dann auch zum Widerspruch. Daher gäbe es hier weder Übereinstimmung noch Widerspruch.*

"This was our paradox: no course of action could be determined by a rule, because any course of action can be made out to accord with the rule. The answer was: if any action can be made out to accord with the rule, then it can also be made out to conflict with it. And so there would be neither accord nor conflict." (PI §201)

Wittgenstein's resolution is not to find a different kind of meaning-entity that *does* determine the course of action. It is to point out that what makes a rule a rule, in practice, is the public practice of acting on it in agreement with others over time. The rule is constituted by the practice. There is no further meaning-fact behind the practice that the practice tracks; the practice is what the rule *is*. The argument is the central spine of the *Investigations*'s positive proposal: meaning is constituted in practice, and practice is not constituted by some further meaning-fact behind it.

**PI §242** closes the discussion with the form-of-life formula:

> *Zur Verständigung durch die Sprache gehört nicht nur eine Übereinstimmung in den Definitionen, sondern (so seltsam dies klingen mag) eine Übereinstimmung in den Urteilen.*

"If language is to be a means of communication there must be agreement not only in definitions but also (queer as this may sound) in judgments." (PI §242)

And, slightly earlier, *§241*:

> *"So sagst du also, daß die Übereinstimmung der Menschen entscheide, was richtig und was falsch ist?" — Richtig und falsch ist, was Menschen sagen; und in der Sprache stimmen die Menschen überein. Dies ist keine Übereinstimmung der Meinungen, sondern der Lebensform.*

"'So you are saying that human agreement decides what is true and what is false?' — What is true or false is what human beings say; and they agree in the language they use. That is not agreement in opinions, but in form of life." (PI §241)

The *Lebensform* — form of life — is the bedrock of the language-game. The agreement that makes communication possible is not agreement in propositions but agreement in the practices that make propositions intelligible. The *Lebensform* is the indigenous Wittgensteinian counterpart to the Mīmāṃsā *prayojana* — what the discourse is for, in the form of life that sustains it.

The convergence with the *catur-anubandha* schema is striking. The Mīmāṃsā says that a discourse is fully specified by its *viṣaya*, *sambandha*, *adhikārin*, and *prayojana*. The *Investigations* says that an utterance is intelligible only within a language-game, which is itself situated in a form of life. The *adhikārin* corresponds, roughly, to the practitioners of the language-game; the *prayojana* corresponds to what the language-game is *for*; the *sambandha* corresponds to the constitutive practices of the form of life; the *viṣaya* corresponds to the language-game's domain. AF1-marked: structural convergence, not derivation.

### 4.3 *On Certainty*: hinges, the world-picture, bedrock

The notes published as *Über Gewissheit* (*On Certainty*) were written in the last eighteen months of Wittgenstein's life, in response to G. E. Moore's "A Defence of Common Sense" and "Proof of an External World." Moore had argued that propositions like "Here is one hand" and "I have never been on the moon" are propositions we know with greater certainty than any philosophical argument that purports to undermine them. Wittgenstein's response is not that Moore is wrong but that Moore's claim is the wrong shape. The propositions Moore cites do not stand in the language-game of knowledge-claims as ordinary propositions stand; they *frame* the language-game. They are *hinges* on which the game turns; the game cannot question them without ceasing to be the game it is.

**OC §94**:

> *Ich aber gehe nicht durch Schauen ihrer Wahrheit nach. Ich habe ein Weltbild. Ist es wahr oder falsch? Vor allem ist es die Substratum allen meinen Forschens und Behauptens.*

"But I did not get my picture of the world by satisfying myself of its correctness; nor do I have it because I am satisfied of its correctness. No: it is the inherited background against which I distinguish between true and false." (OC §94, Paul–Anscombe trans.)

The *Weltbild* — world-picture — is the background against which true/false claims are made; it is not itself a true/false claim. To ask whether the *Weltbild* is true is to ask the wrong kind of question: not because the question is foolish, but because the *Weltbild* does not have the role that would let it be true or false in the ordinary sense. The role it has is to frame the practice within which truth and falsehood can be assigned.

**OC §341–§343**:

> *§341: Das heißt, die Fragen, die wir stellen, und unsere Zweifel beruhen darauf, daß gewisse Sätze vom Zweifel ausgenommen sind, gleichsam die Angeln, in denen jene sich bewegen.*

"The questions we raise and our doubts depend on the fact that some propositions are exempt from doubt, are as it were like hinges on which those turn."

> *§342: Das heißt, es gehört zur Logik unserer wissenschaftlichen Untersuchungen, daß Gewisses in der Tat nicht angezweifelt wird.*

"That is to say, it belongs to the logic of our scientific investigations that certain things are in deed not doubted."

> *§343: Es ist aber nicht so, daß wir eben nicht alles untersuchen können und uns daher notgedrungen mit Annahme begnügen müssen. Wenn ich will, daß die Tür sich drehe, müssen die Angeln feststehen.*

"But it is not that we just cannot investigate everything, and so are forced to be content with assumption. If I want the door to turn, the hinges must stay put."

The *Angeln* — hinges — are the practical conditions of the language-game's operation. Doubting a hinge is not deeper inquiry; it is exit from the language-game. The hinges are not propositions we are sure of; they are conditions of the game we play. The Mīmāṃsā analogue is the *sambandha* — the relation to authority — that grounds the *vidhi*-utterance. The discourse-game operates because the *sambandha* is in place; doubting the *sambandha* is exit from the discourse, not deeper engagement with it.

**OC §115**:

> *Wer an allem zweifeln wollte, würde auch nicht bis zum Zweifel kommen. Das Spiel des Zweifelns selbst setzt schon die Gewißheit voraus.*

"If you tried to doubt everything you would not get as far as doubting anything. The game of doubting itself presupposes certainty." (OC §115)

The proposition is the formal closure of the rule-following discussion in the *Investigations*. Doubt is intelligible only within a language-game that holds certain things fixed; the holding-fixed is constitutive, not provisional. The discipline of the *Investigations* and the discipline of *On Certainty* together give the position what it needs from Wittgenstein: a sustained, technically rigorous, philosophically deep account of why the demand for a meaning-fact behind the language-game, or for a foundation behind the form of life, is a demand the philosophy of language cannot satisfy and does not need to satisfy.

### 4.4 What Wittgenstein supplies and what he does not

A summary at the close of Part 4. Wittgenstein supplies the position's negative discipline. The discipline says:

- Do not ask for the meaning-entity behind the use; the use *is* the meaning, where "use" is broadly enough construed to include the practices that sustain it.
- Do not ask for the foundation behind the form of life; the form of life is the bedrock.
- Do not ask for the hinge to be justified by its truth; the hinge holds the game in place that makes truth-claims intelligible.
- Do not ask for the rule to determine the practice by some prior content; the practice is what makes the rule a rule.

These are not theses about meaning. They are methodological prohibitions on a certain kind of philosophical question. The questions are not absurd; they are confusions. Asking them is a misuse of the grammar of "meaning," "foundation," "rule," "certainty." The right response is not to answer them but to dissolve them.

What Wittgenstein does not supply is the positive grammar. The *Investigations* names language-games and forms of life; it does not give a *taxonomy* of them. The *Tractatus* gives a single logical grammar; the *Investigations* dissolves the single grammar without putting an analyzed structure in its place. This is deliberate. Wittgenstein's project is methodological and quietist; the project is not to *construct* a positive theory of language but to *clear* the philosophical demand for one.

The position takes the negative discipline and adds the positive grammar — *śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya*; *catur-anubandha*; *vāc*-hierarchy; *sphoṭa*; *adhikāra*-scope. The two halves are complementary, not redundant. Part 9 develops the operational synthesis. Before that, three more traditions have to be heard: the late-analytic accretion that extends Wittgenstein in the directions he himself did not extend, late Plato whose *Cratylus* and *Sophist* are the Western tradition's first serious attempt at a philosophy of language, the continental development from Saussure through Heidegger, and Kṛṣṇacandra Bhattacharyya, who supplies a register-theory of the absolute that no other tradition has at the same level of articulation.

---


## Part 5. Austin, Sellars, Quine, Davidson — the late-analytic accretion

The decade after the *Investigations* saw the analytic tradition extend Wittgenstein's negative discipline in directions Wittgenstein himself did not pursue. Austin developed a positive taxonomy of speech-acts where Wittgenstein had only gestured. Sellars dismantled the foundationalism that Wittgenstein had shown to be confused but had not historicized. Quine pressed the indeterminacy of translation in a way that brought the structural insight to the doorstep of behaviorism. Davidson reconstructed truth-conditional semantics under a charity principle that protected it from Quinean dissolution. The four developments are not a school, and they do not converge on a single position. For the present argument they share a methodological feature: each takes seriously a level of linguistic structure that the *Tractatus*'s picture theory had no room for, and each contributes a piece of the positive grammar that the *Investigations* itself does not provide.

### 5.1 Austin — locutionary, illocutionary, perlocutionary

J. L. Austin's *How To Do Things with Words* (William James Lectures, Harvard 1955; published 1962) opens with the observation that a great deal of what we say is not, in the standard logical-positivist sense, a description of how the world is. We promise, command, christen, marry, bequeath, name, threaten, apologize, congratulate. Each of these utterances *does* something — performs an act — rather than reporting that something is the case. The early lectures call these *performatives* and contrast them with *constatives* (statements that describe and are true or false). The later lectures of the series, on internal evidence, abandon the performative/constative distinction in favor of a more general analysis under which every utterance has locutionary, illocutionary, and perlocutionary aspects.

The locutionary act is the act of *saying* — uttering the sentence with its grammatical meaning. The illocutionary act is the act *in saying* — what the speaker is doing in producing the locutionary act: asserting, asking, warning, promising. The perlocutionary act is the act *by saying* — what is brought about by the production of the illocutionary act: convincing, persuading, alarming, amusing. Every utterance has all three aspects; the same utterance may have a determinate locution, a definite illocution, and a contingent perlocution depending on uptake. The taxonomy is, for the position, a sharpened version of what the Mīmāṃsā *catur-anubandha* tracked at the level of *prayojana* and *speech-act type*.

Austin's lectures gives an indicative list of illocutionary types (Lecture XII): *verdictives* (verdicts of judgment), *exercitives* (exercises of authority), *commissives* (commitments to action), *behabitives* (responses to behavior, like congratulating or condoling), *expositives* (clarifications, like granting, conceding, denying). The taxonomy is loose. What matters for the present argument is the structural insight that the meaning of an utterance is not given by its locutionary content alone. The same locutionary content can perform different illocutionary acts depending on context, addressee, conventions of uptake, and the form of life of the speakers. The convergence with Kumārila's distinction between *codanā* (injunction) and *arthavāda* (explanatory accompaniment), discussed in Part 3.3, is exact at the structural level. Mīmāṃsā says: the *vidhi* is performative-constitutive, the *arthavāda* is descriptive-supportive. Austin says: the illocutionary act is what the utterance *does*, not what it *describes*. AF1-marked: structural convergence, not derivation; no contact between the traditions.

The position takes Austin operationally as follows. When reading a passage in any tradition, the question is not only what the words mean (the locutionary level) but what the passage is *doing* (the illocutionary level) and what it is meant to *bring about* (the perlocutionary level). A passage of Śaṅkara's bhāṣya on *Bṛhadāraṇyaka* 2.3.6 (the *neti neti* passage) has a locutionary content (denying that any predicate applies to *Brahman*), an illocutionary force (instructing the *mumukṣu* to drop the predicative grasp), and a perlocutionary aim (the seeker's recognition of the witness as not-an-object). To read the passage at the locutionary level alone and to evaluate it as a metaphysical proposition is to misread it. The passage is a speech-act in *upadeśa*-register; its analysis requires the speech-act apparatus, in the form Austin gives and Mīmāṃsā gives more rigorously.

### 5.2 Sellars — the myth of the given

Wilfrid Sellars's *Empiricism and the Philosophy of Mind* (1956; published in *Minnesota Studies in the Philosophy of Science* I) is the analytic-Western dismantling of foundational empiricism. The argument is in several stages. The first stage shows that the empiricist's "given" — the supposed brute datum of sense that grounds empirical knowledge — is a myth. The brute datum, on inspection, is either an act of cognition (in which case it is already conceptually articulated and not the foundational ground the empiricist needs) or it is a physiological event with no semantic content (in which case it cannot ground anything semantic). The empiricist's "looks-talk" — the talk of things looking thus-and-so — is, on Sellars's analysis, parasitic on the prior talk of things being thus-and-so; the looks-locution is a derivative, semantic, inference-permitting construct, not a foundational, prior, direct given.

The second stage installs the positive replacement: knowledge is a matter of being *in the space of reasons*, of being able to give and receive reasons for one's claims. The space of reasons is conceptual, articulated, public, and historical. To know that p is to be entitled to assert that p in the practice of giving and receiving reasons; the entitlement is not given by a prior brute datum but by the practice in which one stands.

The position takes Sellars as a confirmation, from inside the analytic tradition, of what the *Investigations* and the Mīmāṃsā independently established. Meaning is constituted in practice; the practice is conceptual and articulated; the temptation to ground it in something prior to and outside of the practice is the temptation Wittgenstein dissolved and Sellars further historicized. Sellars's specific contribution is the diagnosis of *foundationalism* as the underlying error. The Indian tradition's analogue is the dispute over *svataḥ-prāmāṇya* — the self-validity of cognition — taken up by the Mīmāṃsakas (especially Kumārila) against the Buddhists and Naiyāyikas, who held *parataḥ-prāmāṇya*, that cognition's validity is established by external warrant. Kumārila's defense of *svataḥ-prāmāṇya* is structurally cognate to Sellars's claim that knowledge is constituted in the practice rather than grounded in something outside it. AF1-marked: structural convergence, not derivation.

### 5.3 Quine and Davidson — indeterminacy and charity

W. V. Quine's *Word and Object* (1960) and the earlier "Two Dogmas of Empiricism" (1951) push the structural insight in a different direction. The indeterminacy of radical translation thesis says that, given the totality of a speaker's verbal behavior, there are multiple incompatible translations of the speaker's language that are equally consistent with that behavior. The "gavagai" example is the canonical illustration: a field linguist observing a native speaker's response to a rabbit cannot, from observation alone, determine whether the speaker's "gavagai" means *rabbit*, *undetached rabbit-part*, *rabbithood*, *temporal slice of rabbit*, and so on. The translations are equally consistent with the verbal behavior and with each other; the choice among them is *underdetermined* by the observational data.

Quine's lesson, for the position, is that meaning is not a fact of the speaker's head that the linguist's behavior tracks. Meaning is a fact of the linguist's reconstruction, made under constraints of theoretical economy and behavioral fit. The reconstruction is non-unique. The flat referential picture — words for things — cannot account for the under-determination, because the under-determination is precisely what one cannot escape by adding more observational data. The under-determination is structural.

Donald Davidson's truth-conditional semantics (in the essays collected in *Inquiries into Truth and Interpretation* and *Subjective, Intersubjective, Objective*) reconstructs a robust theory of meaning under a principle of charity: the linguist assumes that the speaker is mostly right about the world and that the speaker's sentences have truth-conditions that the linguist can recover. The charity principle is methodological; it does not establish that the speaker is mostly right, but it specifies the conditions under which interpretation can proceed at all. The convergence with the Mīmāṃsā doctrine of *svataḥ-prāmāṇya* — the presumption of cognitive validity until specifically defeated — is exact at the structural level. AF1-marked.

The position takes Quine and Davidson together as the late-analytic completion of the negative-discipline arc. Meaning is not a fact of the speaker's head; meaning is not given by direct observation; meaning is a reconstruction made under methodological constraints. The reconstruction is non-unique but not therefore arbitrary; the constraints of charity, theoretical economy, and behavioral fit narrow the under-determination to a workable degree. This is, again, a version of what Wittgenstein dissolved philosophically and what Mīmāṃsā operationalized.

### 5.4 What the late-analytic tradition adds

The late-analytic tradition adds three things to the Wittgensteinian negative discipline. First, a taxonomy of speech-acts (Austin) that gives the discipline some positive structure where Wittgenstein had only gestured. Second, a diagnosis of foundationalism (Sellars) as the underlying error driving the demand for a meaning-entity. Third, a methodological account of how interpretation is possible under indeterminacy (Quine, Davidson). All three are useful to the position. None of them displaces the Indian positive grammar of *śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya* / *vāc*-hierarchy / *sphoṭa* / *catur-anubandha*. The late-analytic contributions are pieces of a positive grammar that, taken together, do not amount to the systematic positive theory the Indian tradition provides. The position uses each in its proper place and supplements with the Indian apparatus where the positive structure is required.

---

## Part 6. Plato — *Cratylus*, *Sophist*, *Seventh Letter*

The Greek tradition's first serious work on language is in Plato's *Cratylus*. The dialogue stages the *physei*/*thesei* dispute — whether names are by nature (*physei*) or by convention (*thesei*) — between Hermogenes (the conventionalist) and Cratylus (the naturalist), with Socrates in the role of provocateur. The dialogue ends in a guarded skepticism that has been read in radically different ways. The *Sophist* gives the first explicit syntactic theory in the Western tradition. The *Seventh Letter*, if authentic, contains Plato's most direct statement of the limits of writing as a vehicle for philosophical truth. Together the three texts constitute Plato's discipline of language: a recognition that language is layered, register-laden, and limited in ways the early Socratic dialogues had not made fully explicit.

### 6.1 *Cratylus*: the *physei*/*thesei* dispute and its irresolution

The *Cratylus* opens at 383a with Hermogenes reporting to Socrates that Cratylus has been teasing him: Cratylus says Hermogenes's very name is not really his name, because names are not assigned by human convention but by *nature*. Hermogenes confesses that he cannot persuade Cratylus to argue the matter and asks Socrates to take it up.

Hermogenes's position is the conventionalist's. He says (384d):

> *ἐγὼ γοῦν, ὦ Σώκρατες, καὶ διαλεγόμενος Κρατύλῳ καὶ ἄλλοις πολλοῖς, οὐ δύναμαι πεισθῆναι ὡς ἄλλη τις ὀρθότης ὀνόματος ἢ συνθήκη καὶ ὁμολογία.*

"For my part, Socrates, in discussion with Cratylus and many others, I cannot be persuaded that there is any other correctness of a name than convention and agreement." (*Cratylus* 384d, Burnet OCT; English follows Jowett with minor adjustments.)

Cratylus's position, in the opposite direction, is that the name expresses the nature of the thing named.
