Socrates initially appears to side with Cratylus, deploying a long series of etymologies that purport to show that Greek names express deep features of what they name. The etymologies (391d–427d) are an extended performance of *etymological play* — what looks like sober philological derivation but is, on inspection, often improvisational, ironic, or even comical. Socrates derives *theoi* (gods) from *thein* (to run, because the early Greeks thought the heavenly bodies were the gods, and they ran across the sky); *daimones* from *daēmones* (knowers, because the souls of the dead are wise); *psyche* from *anapsychein* (refreshes the body); *soma* from *sema* (tomb, since the soul is buried in the body) or from *sōzō* (preserves, since the body preserves the soul for the duration of its sentence). The etymologies are too many and too inconsistent to bear the weight of a serious naturalist thesis. The performance is, structurally, a *reductio*: the etymological method, taken seriously enough, yields contradictions and absurdities.

By 427d–428e, Socrates has begun to turn against the naturalism the etymologies were ostensibly supporting. At 429b he says to Cratylus:

> *τόδε δὲ ἴσως ἂν λέγοις, ὅτι, ὁπότε τις λόγῳ τι λέγει, λέγει τι ἢ οὔ; ... οὐ φῄσομεν, ὅταν τις λέγῃ, λέγειν τι;*

"This perhaps you would say, that whenever someone says something in speech, he says something or not? ... Shall we not say, when someone speaks, that he speaks of something?"

The exchange that follows (429b–430a) presses Cratylus on the question of whether a *wrong* name can be used to call someone — whether it is *possible to speak falsely*. Cratylus is committed, by his naturalism, to denying this; if names are by nature, a name applied to a wrong thing is not really a name. Socrates exposes the absurdity of the position. By the end of the dialogue (438d–440e) Socrates has retreated to a guarded skepticism. The original *physei* position cannot be sustained; the conventionalist *thesei* position is no better, since on it there is no fact of the matter about what a name *correctly* picks out. The dialogue does not resolve. It closes with Socrates urging the question be pursued further (440e):

> *ταῦτα μὲν οὖν, ὦ Κρατύλε, ἤδη ἐγὼ καὶ πάλαι θαυμάζων ὁμολογῶ ἐμαυτῷ· ὅμως δὲ ἐπισκεπτέον.*

"These things, Cratylus, I have been wondering about for some time and I admit them to myself; nevertheless they must be examined further." (*Cratylus* 440e.)

The reading the position takes of the *Cratylus* is this. The dialogue is not staging a debate that one side wins. It is staging a debate that *neither side can win*, in the form available to the two debaters, and the staging is itself the philosophical content. Names cannot be by nature in the strong sense Cratylus wants, because then false speech would be impossible. Names cannot be by convention in the strong sense Hermogenes wants, because then no name is more correct than any other, and philosophical inquiry cannot get a grip. The conclusion the dialogue performs but does not state is that the question is mis-framed; *naming* is not, fundamentally, the relation between *names* and *natures* or between *names* and *conventions*, but a more layered relation in which both naturalist and conventionalist elements have their place.

This is the structural insight the Indian *autpattika* / *kṛtaka* dispute (Patañjali on Pāṇini 1.1.1; Jaimini at *MS* 1.1.5) handles by a different route. The Mīmāṃsā takes the *autpattika* side, but its *autpattika* is not the strong naturalist position of Cratylus; it is the claim that the *śabda*-*artha* relation is intrinsic at a metaphysical level while remaining contingent at the level of individual speakers' usage. The Bhartṛharian *vāc*-hierarchy gives a related answer: the *vaikharī* level is conventionally articulated, the *paśyantī* level is not. Both Indian positions are subtler than the Cratylan dichotomy. The *Cratylus* itself, read carefully, *is* subtler than the Cratylan dichotomy; the dialogue is the staging of why the dichotomy itself is the problem.

The convergence between the *Cratylus* and the Indian *autpattika*-debate is AF1-marked: structural convergence at the level of the problem-statement, not derivation. The Greek and Indian traditions independently arrived at the same fork in the road and worked out different but structurally cognate responses.

### 6.2 *Sophist*: *logos* as combination of *onomata* and *rhēmata*

The *Sophist* (probably late Plato, around 360 BCE) contains the Western tradition's first explicit syntactic analysis. The Stranger from Elea, who has taken over from Socrates as principal speaker, is leading Theaetetus through the analysis of *false speech* — the very problem the *Cratylus* could not solve. The path runs through the famous *parricide* of Parmenides (*Sophist* 241d–242a), in which the Stranger argues that, against Parmenides's prohibition on speaking of what-is-not, *some* "is-not" must be admitted if false speech is to be intelligible. The analysis culminates at 261c–264b in the first explicit theory of the sentence.

The Stranger says (262a):

> *ὀνόματος καὶ ῥήματος συμπλοκὴν δεῖν τὸν λόγον ἀναγκαίως ἔχειν.*

"A *logos* must, by necessity, have the interweaving of name and verb." (*Sophist* 262a, Burnet OCT; English follows Cornford with adjustments.)

The Stranger continues (262b–c) to give the canonical examples. *Anthrōpos manthanei* — "a man learns" — is a *logos*; *anthrōpos*, alone, is not. *Manthanei* alone is not a *logos*. A *logos* requires the interweaving (*sumplokē*) of *onoma* (name) and *rhēma* (verb). The example is the seed of subject-predicate analysis in the Western tradition; every subsequent theory of the proposition — from Aristotle's *De Interpretatione* to Frege's concept-and-object analysis — is a development of the Stranger's *sumplokē*.

The structural insight is that the meaningful unit of language is not the name and not the verb but the *sumplokē* of name and verb. The unit is the sentence, not the word. The convergence with Bhartṛhari's *sphoṭa*-doctrine, on which the sentence (*vākya-sphoṭa*) is the primary meaningful unit and the words and phonemes are abstractions from the sentence, is exact at the structural level. AF1-marked. Plato (or the Eleatic Stranger) and Bhartṛhari, working a millennium apart and without contact, arrive at the same structural insight: meaning is sentence-level, not word-level; the word-level is an abstraction.

A second move in the *Sophist* is more subtle and is more important for the present argument. The Stranger, having established that *logos* requires the *sumplokē*, immediately points out (263a–c) that a *logos* is *of something* (*tinos*) and is *about something* (*peri tinos*). The *logos* "Theaetetus is sitting" is of Theaetetus and is about his sitting (or not sitting); the *logos* "Theaetetus is flying" is also of Theaetetus and is about his flying (or not flying). The structure of *of-ness* and *about-ness* is what makes the *logos* susceptible to truth and falsehood. False speech is intelligible because the *logos* can be of Theaetetus and about something that does not happen to be the case.

The analysis is, for the present argument, the Western tradition's first explicit recognition that an utterance has more structure than its surface words: it has an aboutness, a directedness, a being-of-something-and-about-something that the bare syntactic interweaving does not yet capture. The convergence with the Mīmāṃsā doctrine of *viṣaya* (subject-matter) is exact. AF1-marked. The *Sophist* establishes that a sentence is *of a viṣaya* — has a determinate subject-matter — and *about an artha* — has a determinate predication; the convergence with the *catur-anubandha*'s articulation of *viṣaya* is structural.

### 6.3 *Seventh Letter*: the limit of writing and the *exaiphnēs*

The *Seventh Letter* (Plato, *Epistle* VII; authenticity disputed but the consensus is for authenticity, with the famous epistemological digression at 341c–344d treated as Platonic by virtually all serious readers, including those who doubt the authorship of other passages of the letter). The digression is the most direct statement Plato makes anywhere about the limits of writing as a vehicle for philosophical truth.

The opening of the digression (341c–d):

> *οὔκουν ἐμόν γε περὶ αὐτῶν ἔστιν σύγγραμμα οὐδὲ μήποτε γένηται· ῥητὸν γὰρ οὐδαμῶς ἐστιν ὡς ἄλλα μαθήματα, ἀλλ' ἐκ πολλῆς συνουσίας γιγνομένης περὶ τὸ πρᾶγμα αὐτὸ καὶ τοῦ συζῆν ἐξαίφνης, οἷον ἀπὸ πυρὸς πηδήσαντος ἐξαφθὲν φῶς, ἐν τῇ ψυχῇ γενόμενον αὐτὸ ἑαυτὸ ἤδη τρέφει.*

"There is no writing of mine about these matters, nor will there ever be one. For this knowledge is not something that can be put into words like other sciences; but after long-continued intercourse between teacher and pupil, in joint pursuit of the subject, suddenly, like light flashing forth when a fire is kindled, it is born in the soul and straightway nourishes itself." (*Seventh Letter* 341c–d.)

The passage is rich and has been variously read. For the present argument three things matter. The first is the emphatic claim that the deepest philosophical content is *not* something that can be put into a written treatise. The second is the *exaiphnēs* — the suddenly, the at-once — which marks the cognition's character as not the result of a step-by-step argument but as a recognition that arrives. The third is the claim that, once the cognition arrives, *it nourishes itself*: it does not depend on further external support but is self-sustaining.

The structural convergence with the Bhartṛharian *vāc*-hierarchy is exact. Plato is saying that the deepest content cannot be expressed at the *vaikharī* (written, audible) level; it can be transmitted only through long *sunousia* (being-together) between teacher and pupil; and it arrives at a *paśyantī* level that the *vaikharī* and *madhyamā* levels cannot reach. The *exaiphnēs* is Plato's word for what Bhartṛhari calls *sphoṭa*: the meaning that *bursts forth* at the moment of recognition. AF1-marked: independent arrivals at the same structural insight.

The continuation of the digression (342a–344d) gives Plato's four-fold scheme: *onoma* (name), *logos* (definition), *eidōlon* (image), *epistēmē* (knowledge). The knowledge of the *pragma* (thing-itself) requires all four; none alone is sufficient; the *pragma* itself is fifth (*pemton*), accessible only through the joint operation of the four. The scheme is striking. It is the Western tradition's first attempt at a layered theory of access to philosophical truth, and the parallel with the Bhartṛharian *vāc*-hierarchy is again exact: each level supplements the others, no level alone is sufficient, and the *pragma* itself is at a level beyond all four.

The *Seventh Letter* is, for the present argument, the Western tradition's first explicit statement of a *register-discipline*. The discipline says: not every truth can be conveyed in every register; some truths require the *sunousia*-register, the long teacher-pupil intercourse that the written word cannot provide; the register-mismatch between writing and the deepest content is structural, not contingent. Plato's discipline is, structurally, the same as the Mīmāṃsā *adhikāra*-discipline: not every utterance is meant for every addressee; the discourse is fully specified only by its full *anubandha*-tuple.

### 6.4 What the late Plato supplies

The late Plato — the *Cratylus*'s staging of the *physei*/*thesei* dispute, the *Sophist*'s explicit syntactic theory, the *Seventh Letter*'s register-discipline — supplies the position with the Western tradition's first articulation of three structural insights that the Indian tradition arrived at independently: that the question of naming cannot be settled by the naturalist/conventionalist dichotomy; that the meaningful unit of language is the sentence, not the word; and that the deepest philosophical content has a register-discipline that limits what writing can do.

The convergences with the Indian tradition are AF1-marked throughout. The Greek and Indian traditions had no contact at the time these works were composed (the Indo-Greek interactions of later periods do not affect Plato's authorship or Bhartṛhari's; Patañjali postdates Plato by about three centuries, but had no access to him). The convergence is structural, not derivational. Two traditions, working on different problems with different vocabularies, arrived at the same insights about how language works.

---

## Part 7. Heidegger, Saussure, Derrida — the continental tradition on language

The continental tradition's twentieth-century work on language has three principal figures for the present argument. Ferdinand de Saussure, in the *Cours de linguistique générale* (1916, compiled posthumously from student notebooks), founded the structuralist analysis of language as a system of differential signs. Martin Heidegger, in the essays collected as *Unterwegs zur Sprache* (1959) — "On the Way to Language" — develops a meditation on *die Sprache spricht*, "language speaks," that radicalizes the priority of language over speaker. Jacques Derrida, in *De la grammatologie* (1967) and "La différance" (1968), develops the trace, *différance*, and the general critique of the metaphysics of presence. The three figures do not constitute a school. Each is doing something different with the structural insight; the present article needs each for a different reason.

### 7.1 Saussure — the arbitrariness of the sign and value-as-system

Saussure's *Cours de linguistique générale* opens (Part I, ch. I) with the foundational distinction between *signifiant* (signifier) and *signifié* (signified). The sign is the unity of the two; the *signifiant* is the sound-image (the acoustic pattern), the *signifié* is the concept. The relation between them is *arbitraire* — arbitrary — not in the sense that any speaker can change it, but in the sense that there is no natural reason why this *signifiant* tracks this *signifié* rather than another. The English word *tree*, the French *arbre*, the German *Baum*, the Sanskrit *vṛkṣa* all track the same *signifié* — the concept of a tree — through different *signifiants*. The arbitrariness is the foundation of the structural analysis.

The deeper move in the *Cours* is the doctrine of *value*. Saussure says (Part II, ch. IV): *dans la langue il n'y a que des différences*, "in language there are only differences." The value of a sign is determined not by what the sign positively *is* but by what the sign *is not* — by its differential position in the system of signs as a whole. The *signifiant* *tree* has its value because it is not *three*, not *true*, not *thee*; the *signifié* "tree" has its value because it is not "shrub," not "bush," not "plant." The system of signs is a system of differences, and the meaning of any sign is constituted by its place in the system.

The convergence with Bhartṛhari's *sphoṭa*-doctrine is, again, exact at the structural level. Bhartṛhari says the *sphoṭa* is the holistic unit of meaning; the phonemes and morphemes are abstractions from the *sphoṭa*. Saussure says the sign's value is constituted by its differential place in the system; the sign in isolation has no value. Both are versions of the same structural insight: meaning is constituted at the level of the system, not at the level of the isolated unit. AF1-marked: structural convergence, not derivation. Saussure had no access to Bhartṛhari.

The convergence with the Pāṇinian *adhikāra*-discipline is also exact at the structural level. Pāṇini's *adhikāra* establishes that no *sūtra* has a meaning in isolation; its meaning is given by its position in the system, and in particular by the *adhikāra* currently in force. Saussure's *valeur* establishes that no sign has a meaning in isolation; its meaning is given by its differential place in the system. The structural insight is the same. AF1-marked.

The position takes Saussure as the Western tradition's clearest exposition of the systemic character of meaning. The Indian tradition has the insight in different forms — Bhartṛhari's *sphoṭa*, Pāṇini's *adhikāra*, the *vāc*-hierarchy — but Saussure's explicit theorization of *value-as-difference* is more accessible to readers approaching the position from the Western tradition. The convergences with the Indian apparatus are exact and AF1-marked.

### 7.2 Heidegger — *die Sprache spricht*

Heidegger's *Unterwegs zur Sprache* collects six lectures and essays from the period 1950–1959. The most cited is the essay "Die Sprache" (1950), which contains the formulation:

> *Die Sprache spricht. Der Mensch spricht, indem er auf die Sprache antwortet.*

"Language speaks. Man speaks insofar as he answers to language." (*Unterwegs zur Sprache*, "Die Sprache," German edition; the locus is the opening pages of the essay, GA 12, p. 11; the precise verbatim German has not been retrieved at editorial precision and the English here follows Hofstadter's translation [*Poetry, Language, Thought*, 1971]. `[NOT YET RETRIEVED]` at the level of the German edition page.)

The structural claim is that language is not the speaker's instrument; the speaker is in language as in a medium that precedes him and that he answers to. The relation between speaker and language is reversed: where the flat-referential picture has the speaker as agent and language as tool, Heidegger has language as the primary agent and the speaker as respondent.

The convergence with Bhartṛhari is, once more, exact at the structural level. *Vākyapadīya* 1.1 says the Real itself is *śabda-tattva*; the world's *prakriyā* is the self-articulation of *śabda* into *artha*; the speaker is in *śabda* as the wave is in the ocean. Heidegger says: language speaks; the speaker answers. The structural insight is the same. AF1-marked: independent arrivals at the priority of language over speaker. Heidegger had access to the secondary literature on Bhartṛhari only through limited transmission; the resemblance is structural, not derivational.

Heidegger's longer essay "Das Wesen der Sprache" (1957–58 lectures) develops the meditation. The essay turns on the difficult formula *die Sprache der Sprache* — language of language — and the related *Geviert* (fourfold) of earth, sky, mortals, and divinities, which is the *Wesen* (essence, but better: being-as-presencing) of language. The technical content of these formulations is not load-bearing for the present argument; what matters is the structural priority Heidegger establishes for language over speech. Language is not what speakers do; language is what speakers participate in, and what they participate in is what makes speech possible.

The position takes Heidegger as the continental Western tradition's most explicit articulation of the priority of language over speaker. The position does not accept the full Heideggerian apparatus — the *Geviert*, the *Ereignis*, the *Wesen der Sprache* as a name for *Sein* — but accepts the structural insight that language is not the speaker's tool but the medium the speaker is in. The same insight is what Bhartṛhari developed in the *Brahma-kāṇḍa* of the *Vākyapadīya*. The two articulations are structurally cognate.

### 7.3 Derrida — *différance*, the trace, the critique of presence

Derrida's "La différance" (1968) and *De la grammatologie* (1967) take Saussure's differential analysis of the sign and radicalize it. Saussure had said: in language there are only differences; the value of a sign is constituted by its differential place in the system. Derrida says: the system itself is constituted by *différance* — a coined neologism that combines *différer* in its sense of *to differ* (spatial) and in its sense of *to defer* (temporal). The sign is constituted by its difference from other signs (Saussure's insight) and by its deferral from any final presence that would close the differential play. The *trace* is the mark of this constitutive *différance*: every sign is the trace of what it is not and of what it defers.

Derrida's "La différance" is taken from the philosophy-source file already in the corpus (`/home/eeshan/philosophy/sources/french/03_derrida_la_differance.md`). The key passage:

> *cette différence graphique (le 'a' au lieu du 'e'), cette différence marquée entre deux notations apparemment vocales, entre deux voyelles, reste purement graphique : elle s'écrit ou se lit, mais elle ne s'entend pas.*

"This graphic difference (the *a* instead of the *e*), this marked difference between two apparently vocal notations, between two vowels, remains purely graphic: it is written or it is read, but it is not heard." (Derrida, "La différance," in *Marges*, Minuit 1972, pp. 3–4.)

The *différance* is silent; it cannot be heard; it can only be written and read. The structural insight is that the constitution of meaning happens at a level that is not accessible to the speaker as speaker — at a level of *trace* and *deferral* that precedes any present speech-act and constitutes its possibility.

The convergence with Bhartṛhari is partial. Bhartṛhari has the *vāc*-hierarchy in which the audible *vaikharī* is the visible terminus of a descent through three deeper levels; the deeper levels are not audible but are real. Derrida has the *trace* and *différance* in which the audible sign is constituted by the silent graphic-temporal *différance*; the *différance* is not audible but is real (or, in Derrida's preferred formulation, is *neither real nor unreal*, since it precedes the metaphysics of presence on which the real/unreal distinction is built). The structural similarity is real; the philosophical commitments are very different. Bhartṛhari has a positive ontology of *śabda-brahman* in which the deeper levels of *vāc* are stages of the Real's self-articulation; Derrida has a negative or quasi-negative analysis in which the *différance* refuses ontological commitment.

The position takes Derrida's structural insight — that the constitution of meaning happens at a level not accessible to the speaker as speaker — without taking Derrida's anti-ontological commitments. The position is realist; the position holds that the deeper levels of *vāc* are stages of a Real that is more than a play of *différance*. Derrida's contribution to the position is therefore limited: it sharpens the negative discipline (against the metaphysics of presence as a flat referential model) but cannot supply the positive grammar (because *différance* is a refusal of positive grammar). AF1-marked: structural convergence with Bhartṛhari at the level of the layered analysis, divergence at the level of the ontological commitments.

### 7.4 What the continental tradition supplies

The continental tradition supplies, for the position, three things. Saussure supplies the cleanest Western articulation of the systemic character of meaning — *value-as-difference* — that converges structurally with Bhartṛhari's *sphoṭa* and Pāṇini's *adhikāra*. Heidegger supplies the priority of language over speaker — *die Sprache spricht* — that converges structurally with Bhartṛhari's *śabda-brahman*. Derrida supplies the constitution of meaning at a level not accessible to the speaker — *différance* and the trace — that converges structurally with the deeper levels of the *vāc*-hierarchy but diverges in ontological commitment. The three together constitute the continental contribution; the Indian apparatus provides the positive ontology that the continental tradition (especially in its Derridean wing) refuses.

---

## Part 8. Kṛṣṇacandra Bhattacharyya — subject-language and alternative absolutes

Kṛṣṇacandra Bhattacharyya (KCB, 1875–1949) is the modern Indian philosopher whose register-theory of philosophical discourse is the most articulated in the literature. The position takes KCB as supplying a piece of the operational synthesis that no other tradition supplies at the same level: a theory of *alternative absolutes* under which different philosophical traditions can hold different metaphysical positions, each *absolutely*, without contradiction. The theory is in *Studies in Philosophy* I (1956, the posthumous collection of the major essays) and *Studies in Philosophy* II (1958), and is anticipated in the earlier monograph *The Subject as Freedom* (1930) and in the lectures *Swaraj in Ideas* (1928).

### 8.1 *The Subject as Freedom* — the subject not as object

*The Subject as Freedom* (KCB 1930; reprinted in *Studies in Philosophy* II, 1958, pp. 369–500) is KCB's most sustained analysis of the subject-pole. The argument is, in outline, that the subject is not an object of any cognition. Whatever one introspects, one finds *as object*; the *subject* is what is doing the introspecting and is not itself in the field of objects introspected. KCB calls this the *subject as freedom*: freedom-from-object, where "object" is taken in the broadest sense to include any cognitive content, any positable item, any introspectable structure.

The thesis is, on the surface, close to the Vedāntic *neti neti* doctrine, especially as Śaṅkara reads *Bṛhadāraṇyaka* 2.3.6. KCB himself is alert to the resemblance, and the *Subject as Freedom* contains long passages of engagement with the Vedāntic tradition. The difference, on KCB's reading, is that the Vedāntic doctrine is *soteriological* — the seeker is told to drop the *aham*-pratyaya in pursuit of liberation — while KCB's analysis is *phenomenological*: the subject is found, by careful introspective attention, to be not-an-object, regardless of soteriological aim.

The reading the position takes of *The Subject as Freedom* is that KCB has isolated, with phenomenological rigor, the structure that the Vedāntic tradition operates with soteriologically. The structures are continuous; KCB does not, in *The Subject as Freedom*, take the Vedāntic soteriological move (the realized subject *is* Brahman). KCB stops at the phenomenological claim. The position's view is that the phenomenological claim and the soteriological claim are not in tension; they are register-distinct. KCB is doing in phenomenological register what Śaṅkara does in soteriological register; the two are speaking of the same structure under different *prayojana*.

### 8.2 *Studies in Philosophy* I — *The Jaina Theory of Anekānta* and the alternative-truths thesis

The essay *The Jaina Theory of Anekānta* (in *Studies in Philosophy* I, pp. 331–346; first published 1925) is KCB's most influential single essay. It opens:

> "The Jaina theory of anekānta or the manifoldness of truth is a form of realism which not only asserts a plurality of determinate truths but also takes each truth to be an indetermination of alternative truths." (*Studies in Philosophy* I, p. 331.)

The opening sentence is loaded. *Manifoldness of truth* — there is more than one truth. *A form of realism* — not relativism, not perspectivism-as-projection; each truth is real. *Plurality of determinate truths* — the truths are determinate; they have specific content; they are not vague gestures. *Each truth is an indetermination of alternative truths* — each determinate truth, taken individually, admits further determination in alternative directions. The structure is recursive: at every level of resolution, the manifoldness reappears.

KCB places the *anekānta* doctrine against three rivals. Against monism (especially Hegel's, but also a certain reading of Śaṅkara's), KCB argues that the appearance of plurality cannot be reduced to a single truth without losing the determinacy that the plurality of truths preserves. Against relativism, KCB argues that the plurality of truths is not subjective; the truths are real, and each is determinate. Against perspectivism in Nietzsche's sense — perspectives as projections of drives — KCB argues that the multiplicity is in the object, not in the viewer's drives.

The most cited passage in the essay is the closing formulation (p. 343):

> "These modes of truth are not merely many truths but alternative truths. What is presented and cannot be got rid of has to be accepted as truth even though it is not definitely thinkable or is thinkable in alternative definite modes." (*Studies in Philosophy* I, p. 343.)

The phrase *alternative truths* — distinct from *many truths* — is the heart of KCB's reading. "Many truths" admits a relativist reading: each true *for* a viewer, with no fact of the matter as to which is true *simpliciter*. "Alternative truths" refuses the relativist reading: the truths are alternative because each is structurally distinct, irreducible to the others, and *real*. The realism is in the second sentence: what is presented and cannot be got rid of must be accepted as true, even if it is not definitely thinkable, or is thinkable only in alternative definite modes.

The structural convergence with the Mīmāṃsā *catur-anubandha* discipline is striking. The Mīmāṃsā says that a discourse is fully specified by its full *anubandha*-tuple; two discourses with the same *viṣaya* but different *adhikārin* and *prayojana* are different discourses, and their truths are alternatives in the *anekānta* sense, not competitors. KCB has theorized at the metaphilosophical level what the Mīmāṃsā operationalized at the textual level. The convergence is internal to the Indian tradition; KCB is, in this respect, doing something that has Jain roots and Mīmāṃsā parallels.

### 8.3 *Studies in Philosophy* II — alternative absolutes

The essays *The Concept of Philosophy* (1936; *Studies* II pp. 119–128) and *The Concept of the Absolute and its Alternative Forms* (1934; *Studies* II pp. 125–143) develop the alternative-absolutes thesis. KCB argues that the absolute can be conceived in three structurally distinct ways: as *knowing*, as *willing*, as *feeling*. Each is a determinate concept of the absolute; each is internally coherent; each excludes the others; no Hegelian totalization can sublate the three into a single super-absolute. The three are *alternative absolutes*, in the same structural sense in which the Jain *anekānta* gives alternative truths.

The substantive content is that the three absolutes are tied to three modes of the subject. The knowing-absolute is correlated with the subject as knower; the willing-absolute with the subject as agent; the feeling-absolute with the subject as enjoyer. The three modes of the subject (taken from the Sāṅkhya-Yoga tradition's analysis of *citta*-vrttis, and from the Vedāntic *sat-cit-ānanda* triad) are real and irreducible; the corresponding absolutes are real and irreducible.

The structural convergence with Leibniz's monadology — each monad expresses the whole from its own perspective; the unity of the Real is not a higher perspective but the condition of compossibility of all the perspectives — is striking. AF1-marked: KCB and Leibniz arrive at structurally cognate positions on the multiplicity of absolutes from very different starting points (Indian *darśana* analysis and the *Monadologie*'s metaphysics of substance). The position takes KCB's articulation as the more general — KCB is explicitly a register-thesis, while Leibniz's monadological perspectivism is more nearly an ontology — and uses KCB's framework as the operational instrument for cross-tradition comparison.

### 8.4 *Swaraj in Ideas* — and the register-thesis applied politically

*Swaraj in Ideas* (1928 lecture; reprinted in *Studies* II) extends the alternative-absolutes thesis to cultural and political life. The argument is that the colonial encounter has subjugated Indian philosophical discourse to Western categories in a way that distorts both traditions. The remedy is not to retreat into traditionalism or to assimilate to Western frames, but to recover the Indian categories *as alternative absolutes* — alongside and irreducible to the Western. The cultural-political application is striking. KCB is saying that *Swaraj* — self-rule — in the realm of ideas requires the same discipline of alternative truths that the Jain *anekānta* established for metaphysics.

For the present article *Swaraj in Ideas* matters chiefly as evidence that KCB's register-thesis is not a parochial Indianist commitment. The thesis applies generally: wherever the *anubandha*-tuple of a discourse is suppressed (and the colonial encounter is the paradigm case of such suppression), the discourse is distorted. The recovery requires explicit articulation of the suppressed *anubandha*. This is, again, what the position takes operationally from KCB.

### 8.5 What KCB supplies

KCB supplies, for the position, three things. *The Subject as Freedom* supplies the phenomenological isolation of the subject-pole that is the structural correlate of the Vedāntic *neti neti*; the convergence is AF1-marked. *The Jaina Theory of Anekānta* supplies the alternative-truths thesis that grounds the position's plural realism. *The Concept of the Absolute and its Alternative Forms* supplies the alternative-absolutes thesis that grounds the position's refusal of Hegelian totalization. *Swaraj in Ideas* supplies the cultural-political application that motivates the entire project.

KCB does not, however, develop the positive grammar at the level of detail the Indian *vyākaraṇa* and Mīmāṃsā traditions do. He is a metaphilosopher and a phenomenologist; he is not a grammarian. The position therefore takes KCB's register-thesis at the metaphilosophical level and supplements it with the *śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya* / *vāc*-hierarchy / *catur-anubandha* apparatus that gives the thesis operational content. The two together — KCB's metaphilosophical thesis plus the Indian grammatical-Mīmāṃsā apparatus — are the position's complete positive grammar.


---

## Part 9. The convergence — what the seven traditions agree on, marked AF1

This part takes inventory. Seven traditions have been read — the Indian *vyākaraṇa*, the Indian Mīmāṃsā, the late Wittgenstein, the late-analytic accretion of Austin–Sellars–Quine–Davidson, late Plato, the continental development of Saussure–Heidegger–Derrida, and Kṛṣṇacandra Bhattacharyya — and the convergences among them have been marked AF1 throughout. The present part exhibits the convergences systematically and identifies what each tradition contributes that the others do not.

The convergence has six points, ordered from most uncontroversial to most contested.

**(C1) Meaning is sentence-level, not word-level.** The flat-referential picture treats words as the primary meaning-bearing units; sentences are then constructed by composition. Every tradition the article has read denies this. Bhartṛhari's *vākya-sphoṭa* (Part 2.3) takes the sentence as the primary sphoṭa and the word as an abstraction from it. Plato's *Sophist* at 262a (Part 6.2) establishes that a *logos* is the *sumplokē* of *onoma* and *rhēma*; neither alone is a *logos*. Wittgenstein's *Investigations* §43 (Part 4.2) qualifies the use-thesis with "for a large class of cases" and §22's preceding discussion makes clear that the meaningful unit is the sentence-as-move-in-a-language-game, not the word in isolation. Saussure's *valeur* doctrine (Part 7.1) makes the sign's value differential and systemic; the isolated sign has no value. Austin's locutionary-illocutionary-perlocutionary triad (Part 5.1) operates at the speech-act level, which is at-or-above sentence-level. The Mīmāṃsā *vākyādhikaraṇa* dispute (Part 3; *MS* 1.4) between the *anvitābhidhāna* and *abhihitānvaya* schools is internal to the tradition but presupposes that the sentence-level is where the question of meaning is settled. The convergence is exact at the structural level. AF1: independent arrivals.

**(C2) Meaning requires a mediating mental form, not just a direct word-thing relation.** The flat-referential picture treats meaning as a binary relation between word and thing. Every tradition the article has read insists on a triadic structure. Patañjali's *śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya* (Part 2.2) is the most explicit statement: the *pratyaya* — the mental form — is the operative middle term. Bhartṛhari's *vāc*-hierarchy (Part 2.3) refines this into a four-level analysis: *vaikharī*, *madhyamā*, *paśyantī*, *parā*. Frege's *Sinn*/*Bedeutung* distinction (1892) is the Western tradition's late rediscovery of the structural insight. Saussure's *signifiant* / *signifié* (Part 7.1) is another instance. Plato's *Seventh Letter* (Part 6.3) gives the four-fold *onoma* / *logos* / *eidōlon* / *epistēmē* scheme that supplies a similar mediating structure. The convergence is at the structural level: every careful theory of meaning requires more than a binary word-thing relation. AF1.

**(C3) Meaning is layered.** Beyond the triadic mediation, every tradition's mature account distinguishes levels at which meaning operates. Bhartṛhari's *vāc*-hierarchy is the most explicit. Plato's *Seventh Letter* four-fold scheme is the Western analogue. The *Cratylus*'s closing skepticism (Part 6.1) gestures at a level of "*sounousia*"-recognition that the dialogue cannot itself articulate but acknowledges. The Wittgensteinian distinction between the locutionary content (PI §43) and the language-game (PI §23) and the form of life (PI §241–242) is a stratification, though not labeled as such. *On Certainty*'s hinge-doctrine (OC §341–§343) adds another layer. Sellars's distinction between the brute given and the conceptually articulated knowledge (Part 5.2) is yet another. Mīmāṃsā distinguishes the *vidhi* (the injunction) from the *arthavāda* (the explanatory accompaniment); Patañjali distinguishes the four uses of *vāc* (*upadeśa*, *vyavahāra*, *stuti*, *parihāra*). The convergence is that meaning operates at levels that have to be distinguished; the flat-referential model treats the levels as if they collapsed into one. AF1.

**(C4) Meaning is use-bound, addressee-sensitive, scope-bound.** The strongest convergence is on the relational structure of meaning. The Wittgensteinian *Sprachspiel* (PI §23) installs the use-thesis: meaning is constituted in the practice of use within a language-game. The Mīmāṃsā *catur-anubandha* (Part 3.1) makes the addressee (*adhikārin*) and the purpose (*prayojana*) constitutive of the discourse. Pāṇini's *adhikāra* (Part 2.1) makes scope constitutive of the *sūtra*'s meaning. Austin's illocutionary act (Part 5.1) makes the act-in-saying constitutive of what is said. KCB's alternative-truths thesis (Part 8.2) makes addressee-sensitivity constitutive of the truth-claim. Saussure's *valeur* (Part 7.1) makes systemic position constitutive of the sign's value. Even Plato, in the *Cratylus*'s closing skepticism and the *Seventh Letter*'s emphasis on *sunousia* (Parts 6.1, 6.3), is acknowledging that the meaning of the philosophical content is bound to the practice of teacher-pupil transmission. The convergence is that meaning is not the static relation of word to thing but the dynamic relation of utterance to use, addressee, scope, and form of life. AF1.

**(C5) The deepest meaning cannot be put into writing as such.** This convergence is more contested but is striking when it appears. Plato's *Seventh Letter* (Part 6.3) is the explicit Western statement: there is no writing of mine on these matters, nor will there ever be; the cognition arrives suddenly through long *sunousia*. Wittgenstein's *Tractatus* §6.522 and §7 (Part 4.1) is the analytic-tradition counterpart: there is the inexpressible, which shows itself; what cannot be said must be remained silent about. Bhartṛhari's *paśyantī* level (Part 2.3) and Patañjali's *guhā trīṇi nihitā* (the three grades hidden in the cave; *Mahābhāṣya* citation of *RV* 1.164.45) name the same structure: there are levels of *vāc* that are not articulated at the audible level, and the audible level cannot, by itself, transmit them. Heidegger's *die Sprache spricht* (Part 7.2) is a related claim: the speaker does not, by speaking, command language; language speaks, and the speaker answers. The convergence is on the structural insight that some philosophical content is beyond what writing-as-such can convey. AF1.

**(C6) Plurality of perspective/register is irreducible at the level of the absolute.** The most metaphysically substantive convergence. KCB's alternative-absolutes thesis (Part 8.3) is the most explicit articulation: three structurally distinct concepts of the absolute — knowing, willing, feeling — none reducible to the others. Leibniz's *Monadologie* §57 (cited in the corpus at `leibniz.md` Part II): "And as the same city, viewed from different sides, appears entirely different, and is, as it were, multiplied perspectivally, so it happens that, by the infinite multitude of simple substances, there are, as it were, so many different universes, which are nevertheless only the perspectives of one single universe according to the different points of view of each monad." Nietzsche's *je mehr Augen* at GM III.12: more eyes, different eyes; objectivity is the multiplicity, not its sublation. The Jain *naya*-doctrine: seven *nayas* (*naigama*, *saṃgraha*, *vyavahāra*, *ṛjusūtra*, *śabda*, *samabhirūḍha*, *evambhūta*) each giving a determinate standpoint on the real; the seven-fold predication (*saptabhaṅgī-naya*) systematizing the qualifications. The Bhartṛharian *vāc*-hierarchy: four levels at which the Real articulates, each real, none reducible. The convergence at this level is the most contested, because Hegel and other monists explicitly refuse it. But the convergence among those who do not refuse it is striking and is AF1-marked.

The convergence on these six points is the article's central evidentiary claim. The points are independently arrived at by traditions that did not have contact with each other at the time the convergent positions were developed. The traditions used different vocabularies and worked on different problems. The structural agreement at this depth is not accident. The position takes it as evidence that the structural insight is tracking something real in the relation between language, thought, and the world.

### 9.1 What each tradition contributes that the others do not

The convergences are not redundancies. Each tradition contributes something specific that the others do not.

The *vyākaraṇa* tradition contributes the most systematic positive grammar: the triadic *śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya* analysis, the *vāc*-hierarchy, the *sphoṭa* doctrine, the *adhikāra* / *anuvṛtti* / *pratyāhāra* technical apparatus. No other tradition has positive structure at this level of technical detail.

The Mīmāṃsā tradition contributes the *catur-anubandha* discipline as the operational instrument for analyzing any authoritative discourse: *viṣaya*, *sambandha*, *adhikārin*, *prayojana*. The discipline is sharper than Wittgenstein's language-game / form-of-life formulation because it has four explicit dimensions where Wittgenstein has two implicit ones.

The Wittgensteinian tradition contributes the negative discipline: the prohibition on the search for a meaning-entity behind the use, on the demand for a foundation behind the form of life, on the question of whether the rule determines the practice antecedently to the practice. The negative discipline is what protects the positive grammar from being misread as a metaphysics of meaning.

The late-analytic accretion contributes three pieces: Austin's speech-act taxonomy (sharper than the Mīmāṃsā at the locutionary-illocutionary distinction, though less sharp at the *codanā*/*arthavāda* distinction); Sellars's diagnosis of foundationalism (the analytic-Western form of what the Indian tradition handles through *svataḥ-prāmāṇya*); Quine–Davidson's interpretation-theory (the methodological account of how cross-language understanding is possible under indeterminacy).

Plato contributes the Western tradition's first explicit syntactic theory (*Sophist* 262a), the first staging of the *physei*/*thesei* dispute (*Cratylus*), and the first explicit register-discipline (*Seventh Letter* 341c–344d). The *Sophist*'s contribution is technical and durable; the *Cratylus* is a staging that does not resolve but exhibits why the dichotomy is mis-framed; the *Seventh Letter* is a metaphilosophical statement of limits that no later Western tradition matched in compression.

Saussure contributes the cleanest Western articulation of *value-as-difference* — the systemic constitution of the sign. The Indian tradition has the insight in different forms; Saussure's explicit theorization makes it accessible to Western readers and provides the bridge to the structuralist work of the twentieth century.

Heidegger contributes the priority-of-language thesis — *die Sprache spricht* — in a Western philosophical idiom. The thesis is structurally close to Bhartṛhari's *śabda-tattva*, but Heidegger's version comes without the explicit positive ontology of *śabda-brahman*. The Heideggerian version is less metaphysically committed and so available to readers who reject the strong *śabda-advaita*.

Derrida contributes the radicalization of Saussure into *différance* — the constitution of meaning at a level below any present speech-act. The contribution is double-edged: it sharpens the structural insight but refuses the ontological commitment the position needs. Derrida is useful for the negative discipline; he is not available for the positive grammar.

KCB contributes the metaphilosophical articulation of plural realism: alternative truths (not merely many truths), alternative absolutes (not merely many absolutes), the *Swaraj in Ideas* application that grounds the entire register-thesis at a cultural-political level. KCB is the most explicit articulation, in any tradition known to the position, of the metaphilosophical commitments that the discipline of register requires.

The seven traditions together constitute a complete instrument. The Indian apparatus supplies the positive grammar; Wittgenstein supplies the negative discipline; the late-analytic accretion supplies pieces of the positive grammar at the speech-act and interpretation-theory levels; Plato supplies the Western tradition's first register-discipline; Saussure–Heidegger–Derrida supply the continental development of structural and priority-of-language theses; KCB supplies the metaphilosophical articulation of plural realism. The position uses each tradition for what it contributes and does not pretend that any one tradition is sufficient.

### 9.2 The AF1 marking is not a hedge — it is a discipline

A note on the AF1 marking is owed at this point. AF1 — never assert influence where there is independent arrival — is not a methodological hedge. It is a substantive philosophical commitment. The position holds that the structural convergences among these traditions are evidence that the insights are tracking something real. If the convergences were derivational — if Heidegger had read Bhartṛhari and copied the *vāc*-hierarchy into *die Sprache spricht*, if Wittgenstein had read the *Investigations* off the Mīmāṃsā *catur-anubandha* — the convergence would tell us nothing about whether the insights are tracking something real. The convergence would tell us only that one tradition learned from another.

That the convergences are independent — Bhartṛhari did not learn from Plato, Wittgenstein did not learn from Mīmāṃsā, KCB articulated the alternative-absolutes thesis without copying Leibniz — is what makes the convergence evidence. Independent traditions, with no contact and different vocabularies, arriving at the same structural insight, is evidence that the insight is tracking the structure of how language and thought actually relate. The AF1 marking preserves this evidentiary force; the relinquishing of the AF1 commitment, which a casual reader might dismiss as pedantic, would relinquish the very thing that makes the convergence philosophically interesting.

This is why the position is so insistent on the AF1 discipline. The convergence at the structural level is the article's central evidence for its thesis. The discipline of marking the convergences as convergences, rather than as influences, is what preserves the evidence.

### 9.3 Where the convergence breaks — preserved disagreement

A complementary discipline is owed: where the convergence breaks, the position is committed to preserving the disagreement rather than dissolving it. Three cases are worth surfacing as illustrative.

**Hegel on Bhartṛhari (or Hegel on the Indian tradition generally).** Hegel's totalization at the level of the absolute — the *Aufhebung* of every plurality into the higher synthesis of *absolutes Wissen* — is structurally incompatible with the alternative-absolutes thesis of KCB and the alternative-truths thesis of the Jain *anekānta*. The disagreement is not register-translatable. Hegel is operating in M-register with strong LD; KCB is operating in M-register with strong E and Mh; the *viṣaya* is the same (the structure of the absolute when plurality is in play); the *adhikārin* is the same (the philosophically reflective reader); the disagreement survives all register-translations. It is a `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` in the sense AF9 names.

The position takes KCB's side on this disagreement, but the AF9 discipline requires that the position name the disagreement as a disagreement rather than dissolve it by register-trick. Hegel's totalization is internally coherent and is not a confusion; it is a competing philosophical position that the position rejects on substantive grounds. The grounds are stated, the alternative is named, the disagreement is preserved.

**Bhartṛhari on Derrida (or *vāc*-hierarchy on *différance*).** Bhartṛhari's *vāc*-hierarchy is a positive ontology: the levels of *vāc* are stages of the Real's self-articulation. Derrida's *différance* refuses positive ontology: the *différance* is *neither real nor unreal*, since it precedes the metaphysics of presence on which the real/unreal distinction is built. The structural similarity at the level of *layered analysis below the audible* is real; the ontological commitments are incompatible. The position takes Bhartṛhari's side, but the AF3 discipline requires that the position name the disagreement as a disagreement. Derrida's anti-ontology is not a confusion; it is a competing position that the position rejects on substantive grounds.

**Nietzsche's perspectivism on KCB's alternative-absolutes (or biological-drives versus ontological-multiplicity).** Nietzsche's *je mehr Augen* makes the plurality of perspectives biological-naturalistic: perspectives are positions of drives (*Triebe*), each drive a kind of *Herrschsucht*. KCB's alternative-absolutes makes the plurality ontological: the alternative truths are *in the object*, not in the viewer's drives. The structural convergence at the level of anti-Hegelian polemic is real (both refuse the higher synthesis); the substantive disagreement at the level of the *ground* of the multiplicity is real. The position takes KCB's side. The AF3 discipline preserves the disagreement.

These three cases are illustrative; the corpus has many more. The discipline is that the convergence is exhibited where it is real, and the disagreement is preserved where it is real. The methodology is not relativism. It is the discipline of reading each tradition on its own terms first, then comparing only after the terms are explicit. Where the comparison yields convergence, the convergence is marked AF1. Where the comparison yields disagreement, the disagreement is marked `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` AF9.

---

## Part 10. The position's operational synthesis

The position takes the convergence (Part 9) and the apparatus of each tradition (Parts 2–8) and assembles them into an operational instrument. The instrument is what the position uses to read texts, to write articles, to evaluate cross-engagements, to preserve and dissolve disagreements appropriately. The present part exhibits the instrument.

### 10.1 The operational tuple

Every load-bearing utterance in the corpus is to be specified by the following tuple, derived from the seven traditions read in Parts 2–8:

- **Register** (the kind of claim being made): from the eighteen-item taxonomy in `SCOPE_REGISTER_FRAMEWORK.md` §1 — M (metaphysical), E (epistemological), Φ (phenomenological), S (soteriological), P (pedagogical), T (teleological), Eth (ethical), A (aesthetic), PS (political–social), Mh (methodological), SL (semantic–linguistic), C (cosmological), LD (logical–dialectical), RN (ritual–normative), HG (historical–genealogical), AD (aesthetic–devotional). Every utterance carries one primary register and zero-or-more secondary registers. The taxonomy is the position's, derived from the convergence of Mīmāṃsā speech-act analysis, Austin's illocutionary taxonomy, Wittgenstein's language-game enumeration, and the indigenous register-distinctions in the Vedic and Vedāntic literature.

- **Scope-inside** (what the abstraction picks out): the specific items, conditions, or relations the utterance is about. Pāṇini's *adhikāra* is the technical apparatus; the position's *scope-inside* is the generalization.

- **Scope-outside** (what the utterance is silent on): the specific items, conditions, or relations the utterance does not address. Many apparent contradictions in the literature are between utterances whose *scope-outside* the reader has misread as *scope-inside*.

- **Addressee** (who the utterance is for): *mumukṣu*, *jñānin*, *bhakta*, polemicist, philosophically reflective reader, scientist, student. The Mīmāṃsā *adhikārin* is the technical apparatus; the position's *addressee* is the generalization.

- **Speech-act type** (what the utterance does): assertion (*pratijñā*), instruction (*upadeśa*), refutation (*khaṇḍana*), hymn (*stotra*), interrogation (*praśna*), provisional positing (*adhyāropa*), withdrawal (*apavāda*), declaration, performative-constitutive (Kumārila's *codanā*; Austin's illocutionary). The Indian tradition's taxonomy and Austin's together give the operational vocabulary.

- **Discourse-game** (the language-game and form of life within which the utterance is intelligible): the indigenous practice that provides the utterance's grammar, success-conditions, and standards of evaluation. Wittgenstein's *Sprachspiel* and *Lebensform* are the technical apparatus; the position generalizes.

- **Sanskrit-grammar layer** (where applicable): the *śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya* triad, the *vāc*-level, the *sphoṭa* level. Bhartṛhari and Patañjali supply the apparatus.

Every load-bearing utterance in the corpus is meant to be fully specified by this tuple. Two utterances that share all tuple-elements are directly comparable. Two utterances that differ in any tuple-element require register-translation before comparison; the translation must be made explicit, not implicit.

### 10.2 The positive grammar (Indian) and the negative discipline (Wittgensteinian)

The position's operational claim, central to Parts 2 and 4, is that the Indian apparatus supplies the *positive grammar* and the Wittgensteinian apparatus supplies the *negative discipline*. The two halves are complementary, not redundant.

The positive grammar tells us what reading *is*, when it is done well. To read a passage of Bhartṛhari's *Vākyapadīya*, we identify the *śabda* (the audible-textual token), the *artha* (the referent), the *pratyaya* (the mental form mediating the utterance to the reader), the *vāc*-level (audible, mental, visionary, supreme), the *sphoṭa* (the holistic meaning-unit at the sentence-level or higher), the *catur-anubandha* (*viṣaya*, *sambandha*, *adhikārin*, *prayojana*). The reading is constituted by specifying these. The reading is wrong if any of them are misspecified. The positive grammar is the apparatus for getting them right.

The negative discipline tells us what reading *is not*. To read Bhartṛhari's *anādi-nidhanaṃ brahma śabda-tattvaṃ* as a literal claim that the Real is composed of audible noises is to misread. To read Śaṅkara's *jagat mithyā* as a Kantian transcendental-idealist claim is to misread. To read Plato's *Cratylus* as defending the naturalist position because the etymologies cover the most pages is to misread. The negative discipline says: do not extract a flat-referential reading and then evaluate the passage by the flat-referential standards. The grammar that determines what the utterance is doing is local to the discourse-game.

The combination — positive grammar plus negative discipline — gives the position what it needs. Without the negative discipline, the positive grammar can be over-applied: the reader can mechanically specify the tuple and miss the substantive philosophical move the utterance is making. Without the positive grammar, the negative discipline becomes quietist: the reader can refuse the flat-referential reading without supplying any positive structure to replace it. The two halves together give the reading both substantive structure and disciplinary protection.

### 10.3 The redundancy worry — answered

A standing objection to the synthesis is the *redundancy worry*: isn't Wittgenstein enough? The *Investigations* has the use-thesis, the language-game, the form of life, the rule-following discussion. The Mīmāṃsā *catur-anubandha* is a sharper version of the same thing; what does the Indian apparatus add?

The answer has three parts. First, the Indian apparatus *is* sharper, in the literal sense that the *catur-anubandha* names four explicit dimensions where Wittgenstein has two implicit ones. Sharpness is not redundancy.

Second, the Indian apparatus has *technical detail* at the level of *śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya*, *vāc*-hierarchy, *sphoṭa* that Wittgenstein does not have. The technical detail does specific philosophical work in cases where Wittgenstein's broad-strokes language-game / form-of-life schema is too coarse. The Vedānta disputes between Śaṅkara, Rāmānuja, Madhva, Vallabha, Caitanya, Bhāskara, Nimbārka, Vijñānabhikṣu cannot be analyzed at the level of "different language-games"; the analysis requires the technical detail of *śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya*, the *vāc*-level, the *catur-anubandha*.

Third, the Indian apparatus comes with a *substantive positive ontology* (Bhartṛhari's *śabda-tattva*, the Mīmāṃsā's *autpattika-sambandha*, the alternative-absolutes thesis) that Wittgenstein refuses on quietist grounds. The position's commitments are realist; the position needs the positive ontology. Wittgenstein's quietism is a methodological stance, not a substantive doctrine; the position can use the negative discipline of the quietism without sharing the substantive refusal of metaphysics.

The redundancy worry therefore misses. The Indian apparatus and the Wittgensteinian apparatus are doing different things at different levels of structure with different substantive commitments. They are not redundant. The position uses both, in their respective roles, and gains substance from the combination that neither could supply alone.

### 10.4 The translation worry — answered

A second standing objection is the *translation worry*: granting the convergence, how can the position claim that "*adhikārin*" and "form of life" or that "*śabda-tattva*" and "*die Sprache spricht*" are tracking the same insight? Aren't these untranslatable concepts in radically different conceptual schemes?

The position's response is two-part. The first part is that the position is *not* claiming that the concepts are interchangeable in their full philosophical context. *Adhikārin* in the *catur-anubandha* of Jaimini carries Vedic-Brahmanical resonances and is bound to *adhikāra* in the technical sense of ritual-eligibility; *form of life* in PI §242 carries Wittgensteinian-quietist resonances and is bound to the language-game / *Lebensform* discipline. The two concepts are not interchangeable.

The second part is that the position *is* claiming that the *structural roles* the two concepts play in their respective theories are cognate. *Adhikārin* is the addressee-pole of an utterance, the eligible recipient whose epistemic state and practical context determines what the utterance can do. *Form of life* is the practice-bedrock of a language-game, the agreement-in-practice that makes the game intelligible. The two are not the same concept; they are concepts that play structurally analogous roles in theories that share the structural insight that meaning is addressee-bound and practice-bound. The cognation is at the level of role, not at the level of content. The translation is structural, not lexical.

This is also the response to anyone who objects that comparing *paśyantī* to Plato's *exaiphnēs* or to Heidegger's *die Sprache spricht* is "fluffy." The comparison is at the level of structural role within a layered theory of language: each names a level below the audible at which the constitution of meaning happens. The substantive theories are different in their ontological commitments; the structural roles are cognate. The cognation is the locus of the AF1 marking. The disagreement at the level of substantive ontology is preserved.

### 10.5 The relativism worry — answered

A third standing objection is the *relativism worry*: if meaning is use-bound, register-bound, addressee-sensitive, scope-bound, isn't everything relative? Isn't the position committed to a soft relativism in which any utterance is "true in its register" and there are no shared truths across registers?

The position's response is that the worry conflates two distinct claims. The first is that *the meaning of an utterance* is bound to its register, addressee, scope, etc. The second is that *the truth of an utterance* is bound to its register, addressee, scope, etc. The first is the position's commitment; the second is not. Meaning is constitutive of the utterance — what the utterance *says* depends on its register. Truth is what the utterance is *evaluated against* — what makes the utterance true depends on whether what it says is the case. The two are distinct.

The Jain *anekānta* is, again, the cleanest articulation in any tradition. The *naya*-doctrine says that each *naya* is a determinate standpoint with its own determinate claim. The *syādvāda* says that the claims are *qualified* — *syāt asti, syāt nāsti, syāt avaktavyam* — by their standpoint. But the claims are *true* under the standpoint; the qualification is not a relativization. KCB's reading (Part 8.2): "alternative truths" — not "alternatively true." The truths are alternative because each is structurally distinct; each is also *true*. KCB's realism in the second sentence at *Studies* I p. 343 ("what is presented and cannot be got rid of has to be accepted as truth") is the position's realism.

The position therefore takes plural realism, not relativism. There are real truths, plural and irreducible. Each is bound to its register, addressee, scope, etc. The boundedness specifies what the truth *is* and what it is *not*; it does not undercut the truth-claim. The discipline of register is in service of getting the truth-claim right, not in service of undercutting it.

### 10.6 The eclecticism worry — answered

A fourth standing objection is the *eclecticism worry*: the position takes Bhartṛhari's positive grammar and Wittgenstein's negative discipline and Plato's register-thesis and Saussure's value-doctrine and KCB's alternative-absolutes — isn't this a hodgepodge? Doesn't a serious philosophical position commit to one tradition's framework rather than picking and choosing from several?

The position's response is that the worry mis-states what serious philosophical commitment requires. To commit to one tradition's framework, on a deep reading, is precisely to commit to claims that the tradition's framework cannot, by itself, defend. Bhartṛhari's positive grammar without the Wittgensteinian negative discipline is susceptible to over-application — the *vāc*-hierarchy becomes a license to dissolve every disagreement into "different levels." Wittgenstein's negative discipline without the Bhartṛharian positive grammar becomes quietism — every philosophical question is a confusion. Each tradition needs the others to be defensibly applied.

The eclecticism worry assumes that traditions are sealed-off systems whose components cannot be combined without loss of coherence. The convergence (Part 9) is evidence against this assumption. The traditions agree on the structural insights; they differ on the substantive ontological commitments. The position takes the structural insights from each tradition (which converge) and makes its own substantive ontological commitments (which are the position's, defended on substantive grounds). This is not eclecticism. It is the standard procedure of any philosophical project that takes the prior literature seriously: read each tradition for what it sees, identify the convergences and the substantive commitments separately, and build the position on the basis of the structural insights and the position's own substantive choices.

### 10.7 The influence worry — answered

A fifth standing objection is the *influence worry*: if the traditions converge on the same structural insights, isn't that suspicious? Aren't the convergences likely to be a projection by the synthesizer who is reading later traditions back into earlier ones?

The position's response is that the AF1 discipline is precisely the response to this worry. Every cross-tradition mapping is marked AF1: structural convergence, not influence. The marking is not a hedge; it is the substantive philosophical commitment. The convergences are exhibited at the level of structural role within each tradition's own framework. The mapping between *adhikārin* and *form of life* is at the level of the role each concept plays in its own theory; the mapping is not a claim that the concepts are interchangeable or that one tradition learned from the other. The structural cognation is evidence-bearing precisely because it is not influence-driven.

There is a stronger version of the influence worry that deserves separate response. The version: even if the traditions did not have contact, the synthesizer is reading them and is projecting onto them a single framework. The convergence is in the synthesizer's projection, not in the traditions. The response: each tradition has been read in this article at sufficient depth to surface the structural insights *within* the tradition's own articulation. Bhartṛhari's *vāc*-hierarchy is in *Vākyapadīya* 1.131; the four-level analysis is Bhartṛhari's, not the position's projection. Patañjali's *śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya* is in *Mahābhāṣya* on *A.* 1.2.45; the triad is Patañjali's, not the position's projection. Wittgenstein's language-game / form-of-life is in PI §23 and §241–242; the discipline is Wittgenstein's, not the position's projection. The convergences are in the texts. The synthesizer's contribution is to identify them and to mark them; the convergences themselves are not projections.

### 10.8 What the position commits to operationally

The position commits, operationally, to the following discipline:

- Every load-bearing utterance in the corpus is to be specified by the operational tuple of §10.1.
- Every cross-engagement is to be analyzed by the *catur-anubandha* discipline, marking *viṣaya*, *sambandha*, *adhikārin*, *prayojana* explicitly.
- Every cross-tradition mapping is to be marked AF1: structural convergence, not influence.
- Every disagreement that survives the register-translation is to be marked `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` AF9 and preserved rather than dissolved.
- Every passage of primary text is to be cited by locus and quoted in original language where load-bearing; English gloss is required; paraphrase-from-memory is forbidden (AF2).
- Where a passage has not yet been retrieved at the required textual precision, the corresponding claim is to be marked `[NOT YET RETRIEVED]` and the strength of the claim is to be reduced accordingly.

These are not abstract commitments. They are the operational discipline that produces the corpus's reading of every major thinker and every major cross-engagement. The discipline is the position's contribution to the philosophy of the site.

---

## Part 11. Objections and replies

This part takes the standing objections to the synthesis and replies to each. Several of the objections have been partially answered in Parts 9 and 10; the present part assembles the full set of replies.

### 11.1 The translation worry

*Objection.* The position claims that *adhikārin*, *form of life*, *Lebensform*, the addressee-pole, the constitutive practice — these are all "doing the same work." But the concepts are bound to their philosophical traditions in ways that make cross-tradition translation suspect. *Adhikārin* is bound to Vedic-Brahmanical *adhikāra* and to the entire Mīmāṃsā apparatus of ritual-eligibility; *form of life* is bound to Wittgenstein's quietism and to the *Investigations*'s anti-foundationalism. The claim that they are doing the same work is a projection.

*Reply.* The objection mis-states the claim. The position is not claiming that the concepts are interchangeable in their full philosophical context. The position is claiming that the structural roles the concepts play in their respective theories are cognate. *Adhikārin* is the addressee-pole of an utterance; *form of life* is the practice-bedrock of a language-game. The two are not the same concept; they play structurally analogous roles in theories that share the structural insight that meaning is addressee-bound and practice-bound. The cognation is at the level of role, not at the level of content. The position's commitment to AF1 throughout the article is precisely the marking of this structural cognation as such — convergence, not derivation; structural, not lexical.

A deeper version of the objection: even if the structural roles are cognate, the substantive philosophical work the roles do depends on the surrounding theory, and the surrounding theories are different. The Mīmāṃsā's *adhikārin* operates within a theory in which the *vidhi*-utterance constitutes *dharma*; Wittgenstein's *Lebensform* operates within a theory in which there is no *vidhi*-utterance and no *dharma*. The roles are structurally analogous, but the work the roles do is different.

The reply to the deeper version: yes, the work the roles do is different. The position is committed to using the *catur-anubandha* discipline operationally and the *Lebensform* concept methodologically. The Mīmāṃsā gives the corpus the operational categories for analyzing authoritative discourse; Wittgenstein gives the corpus the methodological vigilance against searching for meaning-entities behind the use. The two contributions are different and are used differently. The position is not claiming that the Indian apparatus and the Wittgensteinian apparatus are doing the same work. The position is claiming that they are doing complementary work at structurally cognate levels.

### 11.2 The relativism worry

*Objection.* If meaning is use-bound, register-bound, addressee-sensitive, scope-bound, then nothing is true *simpliciter*; everything is true *in its register*; there are no shared truths across registers. The position is committed to soft relativism.

*Reply.* The objection conflates meaning and truth. The position's commitment is that meaning is register-bound, addressee-bound, etc. The position's commitment is not that truth is register-bound in the relativist sense. The Jain *anekānta* is the clearest articulation: each *naya* gives a determinate claim; the claim is *true* under the standpoint; the standpoint specifies what the claim *is*, not whether the claim is true. KCB's "alternative truths, not merely many truths" is the same point. The plural realism takes truths to be real, plural, and irreducible to each other; relativism would take truths to be unreal, conventional, or merely-for-a-viewer.

A deeper version of the objection: granted that the position is committed to plural realism, isn't plural realism itself a problematic position? Aren't truths supposed to be unified at some final level — Hegelian *Aufhebung*, monistic Advaita, or whatever — rather than irreducibly plural?

The reply to the deeper version: the unification at the final level is what the position rejects, on substantive philosophical grounds. The grounds are (a) the substantive disagreement between Hegelian totalization and the alternative-absolutes thesis (KCB *Studies* II pp. 125–143), which the position takes on KCB's side; (b) the substantive disagreement between Advaita Vedānta's monism and the various *bhedābheda* and *dvaita* positions, which the position takes on a *bhedābheda*-friendly side without committing to any single school; (c) the substantive disagreement between Nietzsche's biological-drives perspectivism and Leibniz's monadological perspectivism, which the position resolves in favor of the Leibnizian structural picture without the biological grounding. These are substantive philosophical commitments, defended on their own grounds. The position is committed to them. The plural realism that follows is the position's substantive commitment.

### 11.3 The redundancy worry

*Objection.* The *Investigations* has the use-thesis, the language-game, the form of life, the rule-following discussion. The Mīmāṃsā *catur-anubandha* is a sharper version of the same thing. What does the Indian apparatus add?

*Reply.* The reply was given in Part 10.3. To rehearse: (a) the Indian apparatus *is* sharper, in the literal sense; sharpness is not redundancy; (b) the Indian apparatus has technical detail (*śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya*, *vāc*-hierarchy, *sphoṭa*) that Wittgenstein does not have, and the technical detail does specific philosophical work in cases where Wittgenstein's schema is too coarse; (c) the Indian apparatus comes with a substantive positive ontology (Bhartṛhari's *śabda-tattva*, the Mīmāṃsā's *autpattika-sambandha*) that Wittgenstein refuses; the position's realism needs the positive ontology.

A deeper version of the objection: even granting (a)–(c), isn't the resulting framework simply the *Investigations* dressed up in Sanskrit vocabulary? Isn't the substantive content the same?

The reply to the deeper version: no. The substantive content is different in three respects. First, the *Investigations* is quietist about metaphysics; the Indian apparatus is metaphysically realist. The two are not the same. Second, the *Investigations* does not give a positive grammar; the Indian apparatus does. The two are not the same. Third, the *Investigations* operates with a single-tier analysis (use within language-game within form of life); the Indian apparatus is multi-tier (*vaikharī* / *madhyamā* / *paśyantī* / *parā*; *śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya*; *catur-anubandha*'s four dimensions). The two are not the same. The convergence at the structural level (Part 9) is real; the substantive contents are different.

### 11.4 The influence worry

*Objection.* The convergences among the seven traditions look like a synthesizer's projection. The traditions are being read through the position's framework, and the framework's lenses are producing the convergences.

*Reply.* The convergences are exhibited at the level of structural role within each tradition's own articulation. Bhartṛhari's *vāc*-hierarchy is in *Vākyapadīya* 1.131; Patañjali's *śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya* is in the *Paspaśāhnika* (*Mahābhāṣya* on *A.* 1.1.1, Kielhorn vol. I pp. 1–4); Wittgenstein's language-game and form of life are in PI §23 and §241–242; Plato's *sumplokē* analysis is in *Sophist* 261c–264b; Saussure's *valeur* doctrine is in the *Cours* Part II ch. IV; KCB's alternative-absolutes thesis is in *Studies* II pp. 125–143. The convergences are in the texts; the position identifies them and marks them. The convergence is not a projection of the position's framework; it is a feature of the texts that the position has surfaced.

A deeper version of the objection: even granting that the convergences are in the texts, the position has *selected* the texts and the passages that support the convergence and has ignored passages that would undermine it. The convergence is in the position's selection bias.

The reply to the deeper version: the position has read the *whole* of each tradition's central work on language (Bhartṛhari's *Vākyapadīya*, Patañjali's *Paspaśāhnika*, Wittgenstein's *Tractatus* and *Investigations* and *On Certainty*, Plato's *Cratylus* and *Sophist* and *Seventh Letter*, Saussure's *Cours*, KCB's *Studies in Philosophy* I and II) and has identified the convergences across the full body of each work. The convergences are not at the level of cherry-picked passages; they are at the level of the structural commitments of each work. Where passages would undermine the convergence (e.g., Hegel's totalization at the absolute level, Derrida's anti-ontology), the position has named the divergence explicitly as a `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` (Part 9.3). The selection bias objection is therefore a charge that the position acknowledges: yes, the position is selecting; the selection is in service of the substantive philosophical claim, and the disagreements are preserved where they survive the discipline. This is what serious philosophical work requires.

### 11.5 The eclecticism worry

*Objection.* The position takes positive grammar from one tradition, negative discipline from another, register-thesis from a third, value-doctrine from a fourth, alternative-absolutes from a fifth. The result is a hodgepodge that no tradition would recognize as its own.

*Reply.* The reply was given in Part 10.6. To rehearse: serious philosophical commitment does not require commitment to one tradition's framework; it requires defensible substantive commitments. The traditions converge on the structural insights; they differ on the substantive ontological commitments. The position takes the structural insights from each tradition (which converge) and makes its own substantive ontological commitments (which are defended on their own grounds). This is not eclecticism; it is the standard procedure of any serious philosophical project.

A deeper version: the eclecticism worry, in its sharpest form, says that the position's substantive commitments are not defensibly grounded. The substantive commitments — plural realism, alternative-absolutes, the rejection of Hegelian totalization, the realist refusal of Derridean anti-ontology — are claimed by the position to follow from the structural insights, but the structural insights do not, by themselves, entail the substantive commitments. The substantive commitments are imported separately and are not adequately defended.

The reply to the deeper version: the substantive commitments are defended elsewhere in the corpus. The plural realism is defended in `kc-bhattacharyya.md` and `kcb-kantian-perspectivism.md`. The rejection of Hegelian totalization is defended in `hegel.md` and `nietzsche.md`. The realist commitment in metaphysics is defended in `aurobindo.md` and the corpus's main statement of the position's metaphysics. The present article does not attempt to defend the substantive commitments; it identifies the convergences at the structural level and notes where the position's substantive commitments depart from the substantive commitments of one or another tradition. The full defense is in the corpus.

### 11.6 The translation-of-Sanskrit worry

*Objection.* The Indian apparatus relies on Sanskrit technical vocabulary — *śabda*, *artha*, *pratyaya*, *vāc*, *sphoṭa*, *catur-anubandha*, *adhikārin*, *prayojana*.
