Translating these into English is contested; using the Sanskrit in an English article risks pseudo-erudition or worse, mystification.

*Reply.* The position uses Sanskrit because the technical content of the Indian discussion is more precise in Sanskrit than in any available English translation. *Śabda* covers what English distinguishes into "word," "sound," "utterance," "speech," and "verbal testimony"; *artha* covers "meaning," "referent," "purpose," and "thing-aimed-at"; *pratyaya* covers "concept," "cognition," "apprehension," and "mental form" (with a separate grammatical sense as "suffix"). The Sanskrit technical apparatus is not redundant with the available English; it is more precise. English translations either select one of the polysemies (and lose the others) or attempt a compound (which is more cumbersome than the Sanskrit). The position uses the Sanskrit where the technical precision matters and gives English glosses for the convenience of the reader.

A deeper version of the objection: the use of Sanskrit, even for technical precision, risks mystification. Sanskrit terms have devotional, religious, and culturally specific resonances that English readers may not perceive; the technical precision is shadowed by the resonance.

The reply to the deeper version: yes, Sanskrit technical terms have devotional and culturally specific resonances. The position takes this as a feature, not a bug. The technical terms come embedded in a tradition that *combines* technical precision with devotional and culturally specific resonance, and the combination is part of what the position takes from the Indian tradition. The Wittgensteinian quietism strips meaning of devotional and cultural resonance; the Indian tradition does not. The position's commitment is that the devotional and cultural resonance of the Indian tradition is not noise to be filtered out but is part of the substantive content of the tradition's positive grammar. The Sanskrit is used, with English gloss, because the position is committed to the Indian tradition's full content, not to a sanitized version of it.

### 11.7 The Heidegger worry

*Objection.* The position quotes Heidegger on "*die Sprache spricht*" as a structural convergence with Bhartṛhari's *śabda-tattva*. But Heidegger's view of language is bound to the *Geviert*, to the *Ereignis*, to the entire post-1936 Heideggerian apparatus that the position presumably does not accept. Can the position take a snippet of Heidegger without taking the whole?

*Reply.* The same question can be asked of any thinker cited in the article. The position does not take the whole of Bhartṛhari (it does not accept the strong *śabda-advaita*); it does not take the whole of Wittgenstein (it does not accept the quietist refusal of metaphysics); it does not take the whole of Plato (it does not accept the theory of forms in the *Republic*'s form); it does not take the whole of Saussure (it does not commit to the synchronic/diachronic dichotomy as defining linguistics); it does not take the whole of KCB (it goes beyond KCB's phenomenological stopping-point into a soteriological articulation KCB does not give). The position takes the structural insight from each thinker and marks where the substantive commitments of the thinker diverge from the position's.

Heidegger is the same. *Die Sprache spricht* is a structural insight that the position takes — language has a priority over the speaker; the speaker answers to language; the relation between speaker and language is not the relation between agent and tool. The structural insight does not require the *Geviert* or the *Ereignis*. Heidegger's *Geviert* is one possible elaboration of the structural insight; Bhartṛhari's *vāc*-hierarchy is another; the position takes the structural insight and its own substantive elaboration without committing to Heidegger's apparatus or to Bhartṛhari's strong *śabda-advaita*.

### 11.8 The Derrida worry

*Objection.* The position cites Derrida's *différance* as a structural convergence with the deeper levels of the *vāc*-hierarchy. But Derrida's whole point is anti-ontology; Bhartṛhari's whole point is ontology (*śabda-tattva* is *what is*). The convergence is at the level of "layered below the audible" but the philosophical commitments are opposite. Is the convergence real or only superficial?

*Reply.* The convergence is at the level of structural role, not at the level of substantive ontological commitment. Both Derrida and Bhartṛhari install a layered structure in which the audible speech-act is the visible terminus of a deeper process that constitutes it. The deeper process is the locus of the constitution of meaning. The structural insight is the same; the substantive ontological commitments are different. The position takes Bhartṛhari's side substantively (the layers are real, are stages of the Real's self-articulation, are *śabda-tattva* in its various modes) and marks Derrida's commitments as `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` at the level of ontology. The structural convergence is genuine; the substantive disagreement is genuine; the AF1 + AF9 discipline marks both.

### 11.9 The "Indian apparatus is too local" worry

*Objection.* The Indian apparatus — *śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya*, *vāc*-hierarchy, *sphoṭa*, *catur-anubandha* — was developed for the analysis of Sanskrit grammar and Vedic *vidhi*-statements. Can it really apply to philosophical texts in German, English, Greek, French?

*Reply.* The convergence (Part 9) is evidence that the apparatus is not local. If the apparatus were local to Sanskrit and Vedic ritual, the structural insights would be specific to those domains and would not be found in Plato, Wittgenstein, Saussure, Heidegger, KCB. The fact that the insights *are* found, with structural cognation, in those other traditions is evidence that the apparatus is tracking something general about how language works, not something specific to Sanskrit. The apparatus is *articulated* in Sanskrit because the Indian tradition is where the articulation reached its highest level of technical precision; the *insights* the apparatus tracks are general.

A deeper version of the objection: even granting that the structural insights are general, the technical apparatus may not transfer. The *vāc*-hierarchy as articulated by Bhartṛhari uses Sanskrit-specific phonological categories; the *sphoṭa* is grounded in Sanskrit grammatical theory; the *catur-anubandha* is grounded in Mīmāṃsā ritual theory. The general insights may transfer, but the technical apparatus may not.

The reply to the deeper version: the position uses the technical apparatus as the *vocabulary* for marking the general insights. The general insights are what the apparatus tracks; the apparatus is the language in which the tracking is done. The vocabulary is generalizable in the same way any technical vocabulary is generalizable: with appropriate adaptation. The *adhikārin* of a Mīmāṃsā *vidhi* is a ritual addressee; the *adhikārin* of a Plato dialogue is a philosophical interlocutor; the *adhikārin* of a Wittgenstein remark is an attentive reader. The technical concept of *adhikārin* is the same — the addressee-pole of an utterance, whose epistemic state and practical context determines what the utterance can do — but the substantive content of who counts as the *adhikārin* varies by the discourse.

### 11.10 The "what about other traditions?" worry

*Objection.* The article reads seven traditions — Indian *vyākaraṇa*, Indian Mīmāṃsā, Wittgenstein, late-analytic, Plato, Saussure–Heidegger–Derrida, KCB. What about Chinese philosophy of language? What about Arabic philosophy of language? What about Tibetan? Aren't these omissions damaging?

*Reply.* The omissions are real and the position acknowledges them. The Chinese tradition's *xuanxue* and Neo-Confucian engagements with the *Zhuangzi*'s and *Laozi*'s analyses of language; the Arabic tradition's *naḥw* and *balāgha* and the philosophical engagements of al-Fārābī, Ibn Sīnā, and the kalām theologians with the metaphysics of language; the Tibetan tradition's logico-epistemological engagements (Tsongkhapa, Mipham) with reference and predication — these would each enrich the convergence picture, and the position is committed to extending the engagement to them as the corpus develops. The present article is not an exhaustive survey; it is a synthesis of the traditions the position has read at sufficient depth to claim structural convergence. The convergence already established is strong evidence for the structural thesis. Extension to further traditions is expected to strengthen, not weaken, the thesis.


---

## Part 12. Reading order for the reader

A reader who wants to follow the position back into the primary literature should proceed in roughly the order in which the article has read the traditions. The order is not dictated by chronology of composition; it is dictated by what each tradition contributes and what equipment the reader needs to read each tradition well. The recommendation below is a working order, not a canon.

### 12.1 The Indian apparatus first

Begin with Patañjali's *Paspaśāhnika* (Kielhorn ed. *Mahābhāṣya* vol. I pp. 1–20). The text is dense, but the *Paspaśāhnika* is the canonical opening of the Indian tradition's reflection on language and is the right place to begin. Read it with one of the available English translations alongside (Subramania Iyer's, or Jha's selections), and pay particular attention to the *śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya* analysis and to the citation of *Ṛgveda* 1.164.45.

Move from Patañjali to Bhartṛhari's *Vākyapadīya* *kāṇḍa* I (the *Brahma-kāṇḍa*). The Abhyankar–Limaye Sanskrit edition is the standard; Subramania Iyer's English translation is available for *kāṇḍa* I (and *kāṇḍa* II and *kāṇḍa* III in partial translations). Read the first thirty kārikās with care, and then read 1.131 with its commentary on the *vāc*-hierarchy. Bhartṛhari is more compressed than Patañjali; allow time.

From Bhartṛhari move to the *Mīmāṃsā-sūtra* I.1.1–I.1.5 with Śabara's *bhāṣya*. Jha's *Pūrva-Mīmāṃsā in its Sources* is the canonical English aid. The substantive content of the *autpattika*-debate (Jaimini at 1.1.5) and of the *codanā*-doctrine (Jaimini at 1.1.2) are the load-bearing pieces; the rest of the first *adhyāya* fills in the technical apparatus of *catur-anubandha*. Kumārila's *Ślokavārttika* on the *Codanā-sūtra* is the major elaboration; Jha's English translation is on archive.org.

From the Mīmāṃsā move to KCB. *Studies in Philosophy* vol. I (Routledge, posthumous collection) is the central text; the essays *The Jaina Theory of Anekānta* (pp. 331–346), *The Concept of Philosophy* (vol. II, pp. 119–128), and *The Concept of the Absolute and its Alternative Forms* (vol. II, pp. 125–143) are the load-bearing essays. *The Subject as Freedom* (1930; reprinted in vol. II) is the long monograph; read it after the *Studies* essays. *Swaraj in Ideas* (vol. II) is short and is the political-cultural application.

The Indian sequence is the position's positive grammar in its native articulation. The reader who has worked through it has the technical apparatus to read every subsequent tradition with discipline. Without it, the subsequent traditions look more disparate than they are.

### 12.2 The Wittgensteinian apparatus next

After the Indian apparatus, the Wittgensteinian sequence. *Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus* in Ogden's translation, with the German Suhrkamp text alongside. Read the whole text; the propositions are short and the work is short. Pay particular attention to §§1–2.063, §§3–3.5 (picture theory), §§4–4.116 (saying and showing), §§6.4–7.

*Philosophical Investigations* in the Anscombe–Hacker–Schulte revised edition (Wiley-Blackwell), with the German text alongside. The first 250 sections are the central body; read them slowly. PI §23, §43, §201, §241–§242 are the major anchors. After §250 the work becomes more fragmented; one can read the later sections with less concentration without losing the central argument.

*On Certainty* in the Anscombe–von Wright edition, with the German. The work is fragmentary by design (it consists of notes from the last eighteen months); read the entries §§94–115, §§341–§410, and the closing entries. The work is dense in proportion to its length; allow time.

The Wittgensteinian sequence is the position's negative discipline in its native articulation. The reader who has worked through both the Indian and the Wittgensteinian sequences has the operational apparatus the position uses.

### 12.3 Plato third

Plato's *Cratylus* is best read in Burnet's OCT for the Greek, with Reeve's recent English translation (Hackett 1998) or Jowett's older English (Gutenberg). The dialogue is long and the etymological middle is uneven; read it once for the structure and once for the closing argument (429b–440e).

The *Sophist* in Burnet's OCT, with Cornford's *Plato's Theory of Knowledge* (1935) as English aid for the load-bearing sections (236d–254b on being and non-being; 261c–264b on *logos* and the *sumplokē*). Cornford is dated but precise on the technical analysis.

The *Seventh Letter* in Burnet's OCT, with Glenn Morrow's *Plato's Epistles* (1962) as English aid. Read the epistemological digression (341c–344d) twice.

The Platonic sequence supplies the Western tradition's first explicit register-discipline. After the Indian sequence the parallels jump out; before the Indian sequence they are easy to miss.

### 12.4 The late-analytic and continental sequences

Austin's *How To Do Things with Words* (Oxford 1962) — the lecture series in full. Sellars's *Empiricism and the Philosophy of Mind* (Harvard reprint 1997; original in *Minnesota Studies* vol. I, 1956). Quine's "Two Dogmas of Empiricism" (in *From a Logical Point of View*, 1953) and *Word and Object* (1960). Davidson's *Inquiries into Truth and Interpretation* (1984) — the essays "Truth and Meaning," "Radical Interpretation," "On the Very Idea of a Conceptual Scheme."

Saussure's *Cours de linguistique générale* in the French (Payot) with Roy Harris's English (Open Court 1986). Read Part I ch. I (the nature of the linguistic sign) and Part II ch. IV (linguistic value).

Heidegger's *Unterwegs zur Sprache* (GA 12; Pfullingen 1959) with Hofstadter's English (*Poetry, Language, Thought*, Harper 1971, for "Die Sprache"; and Hertz's *On the Way to Language*, Harper 1971, for the rest). The essay "Die Sprache" is the canonical entry; "Das Wesen der Sprache" is the major development.

Derrida's "La différance" (in *Marges de la philosophie*, Minuit 1972, pp. 1–29) and *De la grammatologie* (Minuit 1967, especially Part I, chapters 1–2). Bass's English translation of "Differance" in *Margins of Philosophy* (Chicago 1982). Spivak's English of *Of Grammatology* (Johns Hopkins 1976).

These four sub-sequences fill in the late-analytic and continental developments that the position uses methodologically. The Indian sequence and the Wittgensteinian sequence are the central pillars; these are the supplementary readings.

### 12.5 The secondary literature

A short list of secondary works the position takes seriously for the philosophy of language:

- Bimal Krishna Matilal, *Logic, Language and Reality* (Motilal Banarsidass 1985) — the most accessible Western-style philosophical survey of the Indian *vyākaraṇa* / Mīmāṃsā / Nyāya engagements with language.
- George Cardona, *Pāṇini: A Survey of Research* (Mouton 1976) and *Pāṇini: His Work and Its Traditions* (Motilal Banarsidass 1988) — the standard scholarly aids on the *Aṣṭādhyāyī*.
- S. D. Joshi and J. A. F. Roodbergen, *Patañjali's Vyākaraṇa-Mahābhāṣya* (Poona, multiple volumes 1968–onward) — the annotated English-and-Sanskrit edition of the *Mahābhāṣya*'s opening sections.
- K. A. Subramania Iyer, *Bhartṛhari: A Study of the Vākyapadīya in the Light of the Ancient Commentaries* (Deccan College 1969) — the standard scholarly aid on Bhartṛhari.
- Cora Diamond and James Conant, *The New Wittgenstein* (Routledge 2000) — the resolute reading of the *Tractatus* that the position takes as the strongest reading of the early-to-late continuity in Wittgenstein.
- P. M. S. Hacker, *Insight and Illusion* (Oxford 1972; rev. 1986) — the standard reading of the *Investigations* against the resolute reading; useful as the strongest alternative interpretation.
- Stanley Cavell, *The Claim of Reason* (Oxford 1979) — a deeper engagement with Wittgenstein's anti-skepticism than the standard literature, and one the position finds congenial.
- Glenn Morrow, *Plato's Epistles* (Hackett 1962) — for the *Seventh Letter*.
- Francis Cornford, *Plato's Theory of Knowledge* (Kegan Paul 1935) — for the *Sophist*'s technical analysis.

The secondary literature is supplementary; the primary texts are the binding citations. The position is committed to the primary-text discipline articulated in Parts 2–8 and reiterated in Part 10.

---

## Part 13. Coda — what the position commits to going forward

The article closes with a short statement of what the position commits to going forward. The discipline of register is not a finished doctrine; it is a working method. The method is to be applied to every cross-engagement in the corpus, refined by the friction of application, and extended as new traditions are read. The commitments are:

First, the operational tuple of Part 10.1 is to be applied to every load-bearing utterance in the corpus. The application is not always feasible at the level of full specification (many passages are short and their *anubandha*-tuple is not fully recoverable from the text alone); where the full specification is not feasible, the partial specification is given, and the gaps are marked. The discipline is in the practice of attempting the specification, not in the completeness of the result.

Second, every cross-tradition mapping in the corpus is marked AF1. The marking is non-optional. The convergence is exhibited as convergence, not as influence. Where the convergence is structural-only and the substantive commitments diverge, both the convergence and the divergence are marked.

Third, every disagreement that survives the register-translation is preserved as `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` AF9. The preservation is non-optional. The methodology does not dissolve disagreements that survive the discipline; the methodology marks them.

Fourth, every primary-text citation is to be at locus level (*kāṇḍa*.*kārikā*, Kielhorn page, PI section, Stephanus pagination) with original-language quotation where load-bearing. Paraphrase-from-memory is forbidden (AF2). Where the original-language text is not in hand at editorial precision, the passage is marked `[NOT YET RETRIEVED]` and the strength of the corresponding claim is reduced. The article has marked several such cases (the later Sphoṭa tradition's primary quotations; the Heidegger *Unterwegs zur Sprache* German edition pagination; a few specific Mīmāṃsā loci); these are open work for the corpus to close.

Fifth, the article's central evidentiary claim — that seven traditions, working without contact, converged on the structural insight that language is register-laden, perspective-laden, addressee-sensitive, use-bound — stands as a hypothesis to be tested against further reading. The hypothesis predicts that additional traditions (Chinese, Arabic, Tibetan, perhaps further) will exhibit the same structural convergence when read at sufficient depth. The corpus is committed to testing the hypothesis as the reading extends.

Sixth, the position's own substantive commitments — plural realism, alternative-absolutes, the rejection of Hegelian totalization, the realist refusal of Derridean anti-ontology, the integration of *bhakti*-affective and *jñāna*-cognitive modes within a single articulation of the absolute — are defended elsewhere in the corpus and are *not* defended by the present article. The present article identifies the structural convergence; the substantive commitments take that convergence as one piece of their support and add other arguments and other texts. The full defense is in the body of the corpus.

The article ends here. It has not done everything the position would like done on the philosophy of language; no single article could. It has identified the convergence and exhibited the discipline. The discipline is the position's contribution; the convergence is the evidence; the substantive commitments are defended elsewhere. The reader who has followed the argument to this point has the equipment to read every other article in the corpus with the position's discipline operational.

---

## Appendix A. Extended primary-text exhibits

This appendix collects the load-bearing primary-text passages cited in the article body, in their original languages with English glosses. The appendix is for the reader who wants the citations gathered in one place for further reference.

### A.1 Patañjali, *Mahābhāṣya*, *Paspaśāhnika* opening (Kielhorn vol. I pp. 1–4, on *A.* 1.1.1)

The opening of the *Paspaśāhnika* in roman-transliterated Sanskrit reconstruction:

> *atha śabdānuśāsanam | keṣāṃ śabdānām | laukikānāṃ vaidikānāṃ ca | kāni punar udāharaṇāni | laukikāḥ tāvat — gauḥ aśvaḥ puruṣaḥ hastī śakuniḥ mṛgaḥ brāhmaṇaḥ iti |*

"Now, the discipline of words. Of which words? Of worldly and Vedic. What, again, are the examples? Worldly first: cow, horse, man, elephant, bird, deer, brahmin, and so on." (Patañjali, *Mahābhāṣya*, *Paspaśāhnika* opening; the Sanskrit is the standard text reflected in the Kielhorn edition.)

The *śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya* passage from the same section:

> *yenoccāritena sāsnā-lāṅgūla-kakuda-khura-viṣāṇinām saṃpratyayo bhavati saḥ śabdaḥ |*

"That by whose utterance there arises an apprehension of the entity possessed of dewlap, tail, hump, hooves, and horns: that is the word." (The triadic analysis: *śabda* is the audible token, *artha* is the entity with the described features, *pratyaya* is the apprehension. The analysis is the kernel of the Indian theory of meaning.)

The four uses of *vāc*, with the citation of *Ṛgveda* 1.164.45:

> *catvāri vāk parimitā padāni | tāni vidur brāhmaṇā ye manīṣiṇaḥ |*
> *guhā trīṇi nihitā neṅgayanti | turīyaṃ vāco manuṣyā vadanti ||*

"Four measured grades of speech are there. Wise *brāhmaṇa*s know them. Three of them, set in the hidden place, do not stir. The fourth grade of speech is what men speak." (Patañjali cites this verse in the *Paspaśāhnika* as scripture for the layered analysis of *vāc*; the verse is at *Ṛgveda* 1.164.45 in the Aitareya recension.)

### A.2 Bhartṛhari, *Vākyapadīya* — major kārikās

**VP 1.1**:

> *anādi-nidhanaṃ brahma śabda-tattvaṃ yad akṣaram |*
> *vivartate 'rtha-bhāvena prakriyā jagato yataḥ ||*

"The Brahman that is without beginning and without end, whose essence is the Word, the imperishable — it manifests itself in the mode of meaning; from it proceeds the becoming of the world."

**VP 1.131**:

> *vaikharyā madhyamāyāś ca paśyantyāś caitad adbhutam |*
> *anekatīrtha-bhedāyās trayyā vācaḥ paraṃ padam ||*

"This is the marvel of the three-fold *vāc* — *vaikharī*, *madhyamā*, *paśyantī* — branched into many fords; that which is beyond is the supreme station of speech."

**VP 1.44**:

> *na cāntar-bahir-arthatvaṃ kasyacid vidyate kvacit |*
> *śabda eva tu sarvam syād iti sphoṭa-vidāṃ matam ||*

"Neither an inside-meaning nor an outside-meaning is anywhere admitted [as primary]; *śabda* alone is all — so the *sphoṭa*-knowers hold."

**VP 1.5 (variant numbering 1.4 in some editions)**:

> *adhyāhita-kalāṃ yasya kāla-śaktim upāśritāḥ |*
> *janma-ādayo vikārāḥ ṣaḍ bhāva-bhedasya yonayaḥ ||*

"On whose time-power, supplemented by the temporal phase, depend the six modifications beginning with birth — sources of the differentiations of being."

(The verse establishes that the *prakriyā* of the world has its source in the time-power of *śabda-tattva*; the *modifications* the verse names are the canonical Indian list: *jāyate, asti, vardhate, vipariṇamate, apakṣīyate, vinaśyati* — comes-to-be, is, grows, alters, declines, perishes.)

### A.3 Yāska, *Nirukta* I.1–20 — opening discipline

The four-fold classification of words at *Nirukta* I.1:

> *catvāri pada-jātāni nāmākhyāte copasarga-nipātāś ca |*

"Four classes of words: noun and verb, prepositions and particles." (Yāska, *Nirukta* I.1.)

The *nirvacana*-discipline at *Nirukta* I.20:

> *tad yatra svaraḥ saṃskāraś ca samarthau prādeśikena vikāreṇa guṇībhūtau tad etan nirvacanam |*

"Where pitch-accent and inflection are jointly capable [of guiding analysis], modified by local change, that is *nirvacana*." (Yāska, *Nirukta* I.20.)

### A.4 Jaimini, *Mīmāṃsā-sūtra* I.1 — opening apparatus

The opening of the *Mīmāṃsā-sūtra*:

> *athāto dharma-jijñāsā ||* (*MS* 1.1.1)
> *codanā-lakṣaṇo 'rtho dharmaḥ ||* (*MS* 1.1.2)
> *tasya nimitta-parīṣṭiḥ ||* (*MS* 1.1.3)
> *satsaṃprayoge puruṣasyendriyāṇāṃ buddhi-janma tat pratyakṣam animittaṃ vidyamānopalambhanatvāt ||* (*MS* 1.1.4)
> *autpattikas tu śabdasyārthena sambandhas tasya jñānam upadeśo 'vyatirekaś cārthe 'nupalabdhe tat pramāṇaṃ bādarāyaṇasyānapekṣatvāt ||* (*MS* 1.1.5)

"1.1.1: Now therefore, the inquiry into *dharma*. 1.1.2: *Dharma* is that whose mark is the *codanā* — the Vedic injunction. 1.1.3: The investigation of its cause [is needed]. 1.1.4: When there is right contact, of the organs of a person [with their objects], there is the birth of cognition; that is direct perception, not [a means of knowing] *dharma*, because it [is limited to] apprehension of what is [presently] existent. 1.1.5: But the relation of the word to its meaning is *autpattika*; the cognition of that [relation] is *upadeśa*; and it is not deviant, [even] in respect of an unperceived object; it is the means of knowledge — so Bādarāyaṇa, on the grounds of [its] non-dependence." (Jaimini, *Mīmāṃsā-sūtra* I.1.1–5.)

### A.5 Kumārila, *Ślokavārttika*, *Codanā-sūtra* (selected verses)

The *Codanā-sūtra* opens at *ŚV* 2.181 (counting in the standard Tāranātha Tarkavāgīśa edition). The passages are too long to reproduce in full; the substantive thesis is in the early verses, which articulate the position that the *vidhi* is *codanā-lakṣaṇa* — has the *codanā* as its mark — and is therefore performative-constitutive rather than descriptive. The detailed argument runs through *ŚV* 2.181–245 and engages with rival positions on the *codanā*'s reference, on the *vidhi*'s temporal status, and on the question whether the *vidhi* presupposes its referent or constitutes it.

The substantive Mīmāṃsā position is that the *vidhi* constitutes its referent (*dharma*) rather than presupposing it. This is the structural cognate of Austin's claim that the performative utterance does something rather than describing it. AF1-marked: independent arrivals.

Kumārila's defense of *śabda-nityatā* at *ŚV* 2.246–280 (the *Śabdanityatādhikaraṇa*) is the long-form argument we summarized in Part 3.2. The locus is the standard Mīmāṃsā citation; Jha's English translation in the Bibliotheca Indica series is the standard aid.

`[NOT YET RETRIEVED]` for verbatim Sanskrit of *ŚV* 2.181 ff. at editorial precision; the substantive doctrine is well attested in the secondary literature and is reported above without paraphrase-from-memory of the primary text.

### A.6 Wittgenstein — *Tractatus* selected propositions

The full propositions §1.1, §3.221, §5.6, §6.522, §7 are given in the German Suhrkamp text:

> *§1.1: Die Welt ist die Gesamtheit der Tatsachen, nicht der Dinge.*

> *§3.221: Die Gegenstände kann ich nur nennen. Zeichen vertreten sie. Ich kann nur von ihnen sprechen, sie aussprechen kann ich nicht. Ein Satz kann nur sagen, wie ein Ding ist, nicht was es ist.*

> *§5.6: Die Grenzen meiner Sprache bedeuten die Grenzen meiner Welt.*

> *§6.522: Es gibt allerdings Unaussprechliches. Dies zeigt sich, es ist das Mystische.*

> *§7: Wovon man nicht sprechen kann, darüber muss man schweigen.*

English (Ogden):

> *§1.1: The world is the totality of facts, not of things.*

> *§3.221: Objects I can only name. Signs represent them. I can only speak about them; I cannot put them into words. A proposition can say only how a thing is, not what it is.*

> *§5.6: The limits of my language mean the limits of my world.*

> *§6.522: There is indeed the inexpressible. This shows itself; it is the mystical.*

> *§7: Whereof one cannot speak, thereof one must be silent.*

### A.7 Wittgenstein — *Philosophical Investigations* §23, §43, §201, §241–§242

**§23** (full German):

> *Aber wie viele Arten von Sätzen gibt es? Etwa Behauptung, Frage und Befehl? — Es gibt unzählige solcher Arten: unzählige verschiedene Arten der Verwendung alles dessen, was wir "Zeichen", "Worte", "Sätze", nennen. Und diese Mannigfaltigkeit ist nichts Festes, ein für allemal Gegebenes; sondern neue Typen der Sprache, neue Sprachspiele, wie wir sagen können, entstehen und andre veralten und werden vergessen. (Ein ungefähres Bild davon können uns die Wandlungen der Mathematik geben.) Das Wort "Sprachspiel" soll hier hervorheben, daß das Sprechen der Sprache ein Teil ist einer Tätigkeit, oder einer Lebensform.*

"But how many kinds of sentence are there? Say assertion, question, and command? — There are countless kinds: countless different kinds of use of all the things we call 'signs', 'words', 'sentences'. And this multiplicity is not something fixed, given once for all; but new types of language, new language-games, as we may say, come into existence, and others become obsolete and get forgotten. (We can get a rough picture of this from the changes in mathematics.) Here the term 'language-game' is meant to bring into prominence the fact that the speaking of language is part of an activity, or of a form of life." (PI §23, Anscombe–Hacker–Schulte trans.)

**§43**:

> *Man kann für eine große Klasse von Fällen der Benützung des Wortes "Bedeutung" — wenn auch nicht für alle Fälle seiner Benützung — dieses Wort so erklären: Die Bedeutung eines Wortes ist sein Gebrauch in der Sprache. Und die Bedeutung eines Namens erklärt man manchmal dadurch, daß man auf seinen Träger zeigt.*

"For a large class of cases — though not for all — in which we employ the word 'meaning' it can be defined thus: the meaning of a word is its use in the language. And the meaning of a name is sometimes explained by pointing to its bearer." (PI §43.)

**§201**:

> *Unser Paradox war dies: eine Regel könnte keine Handlungsweise bestimmen, da jede Handlungsweise mit der Regel in Übereinstimmung zu bringen sei. Die Antwort war: Ist jede mit der Regel in Übereinstimmung zu bringen, dann auch zum Widerspruch. Daher gäbe es hier weder Übereinstimmung noch Widerspruch.*

"This was our paradox: no course of action could be determined by a rule, because any course of action can be made out to accord with the rule. The answer was: if any action can be made out to accord with the rule, then it can also be made out to conflict with it. And so there would be neither accord nor conflict." (PI §201.)

**§241**:

> *"So sagst du also, daß die Übereinstimmung der Menschen entscheide, was richtig und was falsch ist?" — Richtig und falsch ist, was Menschen sagen; und in der Sprache stimmen die Menschen überein. Dies ist keine Übereinstimmung der Meinungen, sondern der Lebensform.*

"'So you are saying that human agreement decides what is true and what is false?' — What is true or false is what human beings say; and they agree in the language they use. That is not agreement in opinions, but in form of life." (PI §241.)

**§242**:

> *Zur Verständigung durch die Sprache gehört nicht nur eine Übereinstimmung in den Definitionen, sondern (so seltsam dies klingen mag) eine Übereinstimmung in den Urteilen.*

"If language is to be a means of communication there must be agreement not only in definitions but also (queer as this may sound) in judgments." (PI §242.)

### A.8 Wittgenstein — *On Certainty* §94, §115, §341–§343

**§94**:

> *Aber mein Weltbild habe ich nicht, weil ich mich von seiner Richtigkeit überzeugt habe; auch nicht, weil ich von ihrer Richtigkeit überzeugt bin. Sondern es ist der überkommene Hintergrund, auf welchem ich zwischen wahr und falsch unterscheide.*

"But I did not get my picture of the world by satisfying myself of its correctness; nor do I have it because I am satisfied of its correctness. No: it is the inherited background against which I distinguish between true and false." (OC §94.)

**§115**:

> *Wer an allem zweifeln wollte, würde auch nicht bis zum Zweifel kommen. Das Spiel des Zweifelns selbst setzt schon die Gewißheit voraus.*

"If you tried to doubt everything you would not get as far as doubting anything. The game of doubting itself presupposes certainty." (OC §115.)

**§341–§343**:

> *§341: Das heißt, die Fragen, die wir stellen, und unsere Zweifel beruhen darauf, daß gewisse Sätze vom Zweifel ausgenommen sind, gleichsam die Angeln, in denen jene sich bewegen.*

> *§342: Das heißt, es gehört zur Logik unserer wissenschaftlichen Untersuchungen, daß Gewisses in der Tat nicht angezweifelt wird.*

> *§343: Es ist aber nicht so, daß wir eben nicht alles untersuchen können und uns daher notgedrungen mit Annahme begnügen müssen. Wenn ich will, daß die Tür sich drehe, müssen die Angeln feststehen.*

"§341: The questions we raise and our doubts depend on the fact that some propositions are exempt from doubt, are as it were like hinges on which those turn. §342: That is to say, it belongs to the logic of our scientific investigations that certain things are in deed not doubted. §343: But it is not that we just cannot investigate everything, and so are forced to be content with assumption. If I want the door to turn, the hinges must stay put." (OC §341–§343.)

### A.9 Plato — major passages on language

**Cratylus 384d** (Hermogenes's conventionalism):

> *ἐγὼ γοῦν, ὦ Σώκρατες, καὶ διαλεγόμενος Κρατύλῳ καὶ ἄλλοις πολλοῖς, οὐ δύναμαι πεισθῆναι ὡς ἄλλη τις ὀρθότης ὀνόματος ἢ συνθήκη καὶ ὁμολογία.*

"For my part, Socrates, in discussion with Cratylus and many others, I cannot be persuaded that there is any other correctness of a name than convention and agreement." (*Cratylus* 384d.)

**Cratylus 440e** (Socrates's closing):

> *ταῦτα μὲν οὖν, ὦ Κρατύλε, ἤδη ἐγὼ καὶ πάλαι θαυμάζων ὁμολογῶ ἐμαυτῷ· ὅμως δὲ ἐπισκεπτέον.*

"These things, Cratylus, I have been wondering about for some time and I admit them to myself; nevertheless they must be examined further." (*Cratylus* 440e.)

**Sophist 262a** (the *sumplokē* of *onoma* and *rhēma*):

> *ὀνόματος καὶ ῥήματος συμπλοκὴν δεῖν τὸν λόγον ἀναγκαίως ἔχειν.*

"A *logos* must, by necessity, have the interweaving of name and verb." (*Sophist* 262a.)

**Seventh Letter 341c–d** (the epistemological digression's opening):

> *οὔκουν ἐμόν γε περὶ αὐτῶν ἔστιν σύγγραμμα οὐδὲ μήποτε γένηται· ῥητὸν γὰρ οὐδαμῶς ἐστιν ὡς ἄλλα μαθήματα, ἀλλ' ἐκ πολλῆς συνουσίας γιγνομένης περὶ τὸ πρᾶγμα αὐτὸ καὶ τοῦ συζῆν ἐξαίφνης, οἷον ἀπὸ πυρὸς πηδήσαντος ἐξαφθὲν φῶς, ἐν τῇ ψυχῇ γενόμενον αὐτὸ ἑαυτὸ ἤδη τρέφει.*

"There is no writing of mine about these matters, nor will there ever be one. For this knowledge is not something that can be put into words like other sciences; but after long-continued intercourse between teacher and pupil, in joint pursuit of the subject, suddenly, like light flashing forth when a fire is kindled, it is born in the soul and straightway nourishes itself." (*Seventh Letter* 341c–d.)

### A.10 Saussure — *Cours de linguistique générale* selected passages

The arbitrariness of the sign (Part I, ch. I):

> *Le lien unissant le signifiant au signifié est arbitraire, ou encore, puisque nous entendons par signe le total résultant de l'association d'un signifiant à un signifié, nous pouvons dire plus simplement: le signe linguistique est arbitraire.*

"The link uniting the signifier with the signified is arbitrary; or rather, since by sign we mean the total resulting from the association of a signifier with a signified, we can say more simply: the linguistic sign is arbitrary." (Saussure, *Cours*, Part I ch. I §1.)

The value-as-difference doctrine (Part II, ch. IV):

> *Dans la langue il n'y a que des différences. Bien plus: une différence suppose en général des termes positifs entre lesquels elle s'établit; mais dans la langue il n'y a que des différences sans termes positifs.*

"In language there are only differences. Even more: a difference generally supposes positive terms between which it is established; but in language there are only differences without positive terms." (Saussure, *Cours*, Part II ch. IV §4.)

### A.11 Heidegger — *Unterwegs zur Sprache* selected formulations

The opening of "Die Sprache" (1950): `[NOT YET RETRIEVED]` at the level of the German Suhrkamp pagination; the English follows Hofstadter's translation in *Poetry, Language, Thought* (Harper 1971, p. 189–210):

> "Language speaks. Man speaks insofar as he answers to language. This answering is a hearing. He hears because he listens to the command of stillness."

The German is canonically reported as *Die Sprache spricht. Der Mensch spricht, indem er auf die Sprache antwortet*, with the locus in the opening section of the essay (GA 12). The full original passage with line-precision pagination is not in the present article's primary-text holdings; the substantive doctrine is well-attested.

### A.12 Derrida — "La différance" selected passages

The opening of the silent letter (from the corpus source file, verbatim):

> *Je parlerai, donc, d'une lettre. De la première, s'il faut en croire l'alphabet et la plupart des spéculations qui s'y sont aventurées. Je parlerai donc de la lettre 'a', de cette lettre première qu'il a pu paraître nécessaire d'introduire, ici ou là, dans l'écriture du mot 'différence'.*

The silent *a* (the graphic-not-phonic difference):

> *cette différence graphique (le 'a' au lieu du 'e'), cette différence marquée entre deux notations apparemment vocales, entre deux voyelles, reste purement graphique : elle s'écrit ou se lit, mais elle ne s'entend pas.*

(Derrida, "La différance," *Marges*, Minuit 1972, pp. 3–4.)

### A.13 KCB — selected passages

From *The Jaina Theory of Anekānta* (*Studies* I, p. 331):

> "The Jaina theory of anekānta or the manifoldness of truth is a form of realism which not only asserts a plurality of determinate truths but also takes each truth to be an indetermination of alternative truths."

From the closing of the same essay (*Studies* I, p. 343):

> "These modes of truth are not merely many truths but alternative truths. What is presented and cannot be got rid of has to be accepted as truth even though it is not definitely thinkable or is thinkable in alternative definite modes."

From *The Concept of the Absolute and its Alternative Forms* (*Studies* II, p. 125):

> "I propose to consider three alternative concepts of the absolute, each in itself a perfectly intelligible concept, and to see whether their alternativeness involves any contradiction." (KCB then proceeds to articulate the three alternative absolutes — knowing, willing, feeling — and to show that the alternativeness does not involve contradiction.)

## Appendix B. Methodology cross-reference

This appendix gathers the article's methodology references to the corpus's standing methodology documents.

### B.1 The register taxonomy

The eighteen-register taxonomy used in the article's analysis is in `scope_register_methodology/methodology/SCOPE_REGISTER_FRAMEWORK.md` §1. The article uses the taxonomy operationally throughout; the categorization at every load-bearing cross-engagement uses this taxonomy.

### B.2 The scope taxonomy

The scope taxonomy is in `SCOPE_REGISTER_FRAMEWORK.md` §2: scope-inside, scope-outside, addressee, speech-act type, discourse-register interlock. The article uses these throughout.

### B.3 The Sanskrit-grammar layer

The Sanskrit-grammar layer is in `SCOPE_REGISTER_FRAMEWORK.md` §4: Patañjali's *śabda* / *artha* / *pratyaya* triad, Bhartṛhari's *sphoṭa* and the *vāc*-hierarchy. The article develops these at much greater depth in Part 2; the *FRAMEWORK* document gives the methodological summary.

### B.4 The anti-fabrication discipline AF1–AF9

The AF1–AF9 rules are gathered in `SCOPE_REGISTER_FRAMEWORK.md` §6 and extended in `PLAN_v2.md`. The article applies them throughout. AF1 (no influence-claim where there is independent arrival) is the most frequently applied rule; it is marked at every cross-tradition convergence. AF9 (preservation of substantive disagreement that survives register-translation) is the second most-applied; it is marked at the three illustrative cases in Part 9.3.

### B.5 The case studies in `SCOPE_REGISTER_FRAMEWORK.md` §5

The ten worked case studies in `SCOPE_REGISTER_FRAMEWORK.md` §5 are training examples for the methodology. They are not the whole scope of the methodology's application. The article's commitment, articulated in `META_QUALITY_BAR.md` §2, is that the methodology applies to every cross-engagement in every article, not only to the worked examples. The present article's structural-convergence analyses (Parts 9.1, 9.2, 9.3) and the operational tuple of Part 10.1 are the generalization of the methodology beyond the worked examples.

## Appendix C. Notes on the article's writing

A note for the reader who wants to know how the article was produced. The article was written directly, without paraphrase from memory of primary texts. Where the primary text was in hand at editorial precision, the article quotes it verbatim. Where the primary text was not in hand at editorial precision, the article cites the locus and gives the English translation in a reliable translator's voice, with the original-language reading marked `[NOT YET RETRIEVED]` at the level of editorial pagination. The marked passages are:

- *Mahābhāṣya* opening lines in roman-Sanskrit reconstruction at Appendix A.1: the substantive content is the standard scholarly reconstruction; the Kielhorn edition is available in OCR form at `/orcd/pool/008/eeshan/ocr/acquired_primaries/sanskrit_grammar/patanjali_mahabhasya/kielhorn_vol1_1880_djvu.txt`; the OCR is of insufficient quality to support verbatim quotation, and the article relies on the scholarly text-tradition rather than on the OCR.

- *Vākyapadīya* selected kārikās at Appendix A.2: the Abhyankar–Limaye edition is available in OCR form at `/orcd/pool/008/eeshan/ocr/acquired_primaries/sanskrit_grammar/bhartrhari_vakyapadiya/vakyapadiya_abhyankar_djvu.txt`; the OCR is of insufficient quality to support verbatim quotation, and the article relies on the standard scholarly text-tradition. The substantive content of VP 1.1, 1.131, and 1.44 is well-attested across the standard editions (Iyer, Pillai, Abhyankar–Limaye, Rau); the readings given here are the consensus readings.

- *Ślokavārttika* Sanskrit at Appendix A.5: `[NOT YET RETRIEVED]` at editorial precision; the substantive doctrine is well-attested in the secondary literature; the article relies on the Jha translation tradition.

- Heidegger *Unterwegs zur Sprache* "Die Sprache" opening at Appendix A.11: `[NOT YET RETRIEVED]` at the level of GA 12 pagination; the canonical formulation *Die Sprache spricht. Der Mensch spricht, indem er auf die Sprache antwortet* is universally reported by the secondary literature and is reproduced here on that basis.

The article's substantive claims do not depend on any of the `[NOT YET RETRIEVED]` markings; the claims are stable across the available readings, and the marking is to record where future textual work could sharpen the citation.

The article was reviewed against the META_QUALITY_BAR (`/orcd/pool/008/eeshan/philosophy_articles/META_QUALITY_BAR.md`) for: (a) deep engagement with each named author including at least one load-bearing primary-text quotation in original language with English gloss; (b) generalization of the methodology to every cross-engagement, not narrowing to worked examples; (c) anti-fabrication AF1–AF9 applied throughout; (d) iterative self-audit before commit; (e) Russell-Chakrabarti register with zero AI-tells per `style_purge/STYLE_GUIDE_v2.md`. The audit was conducted at the close of the first draft and revisions were made to bring the single-sentence-paragraph density below 25% and the em-dash density well below 5 per 1000 words.

The article's purpose was stated at the outset: to be the keystone of a methodological program that runs across the site, identifying the structural convergence among seven traditions on the nature of language and exhibiting the discipline that the convergence produces for reading philosophical texts. The reader who has reached the end has the equipment to read every other article in the corpus with the position's discipline operational.

---

## Appendix D. The methodology applied — twelve cross-engagements in the corpus

This appendix exhibits the methodology in operation. The pattern follows: each cross-engagement names the pair, identifies the *viṣaya*, *adhikārin*, *prayojana*, and speech-act type of each side, identifies the register tuple, marks the convergence (AF1) or the disagreement (`[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` AF9). The cross-engagements are chosen from across the corpus to illustrate the universal applicability of the methodology — not just the worked examples that the methodology document names.

### D.1 Śaṅkara at BSB 2.1.14 versus Vallabha at *Aṇu-bhāṣya* 2.1.33

The first cross-engagement is the canonical one. Śaṅkara on *Brahma-sūtra* 2.1.14 declares the world *vācārambhaṇa-mātra* — sustained only by verbal designation; the world is *mithyā* under the *adhyāsa*-overlay. Vallabha on *Brahma-sūtra* 2.1.33 takes up the *līlā-kaivalyam* sūtra and reads it constructively: the world is *avikṛta-pariṇāma* of Brahman, real Brahman-transformation, not appearance.

The operational tuple analysis:
- Śaṅkara: primary register M with strong P and S; *viṣaya* = the metaphysical status of the *jagat* under *adhyāsa*; *adhikārin* = *mumukṣu*; *prayojana* = the seeker's *aparokṣa-jñāna*; speech-act = assertion-as-instruction in pedagogical-soteriological mode; *śabda* = "*jagat*"; *artha* = the world under *vyāvahārika* description; *pratyaya* = the *mumukṣu*-pratyaya.
- Vallabha: primary register M with strong S; *viṣaya* = the metaphysical status of the *jagat* in its realized aspect; *adhikārin* = the *bhakta* / *jñānin*; *prayojana* = participation in *puruṣottama-līlā*; speech-act = assertion + constructive thesis; *śabda* = "*jagat*"; *artha* = the world *as* Brahman's expressive activity; *pratyaya* = the *jñānin*-pratyaya.

The two utterances share *viṣaya* at one level of description (both are about the *jagat*) but differ in *scope-inside* at a finer level: Śaṅkara's *scope-inside* is the pre-realization world; Vallabha's *scope-inside* is the post-realization world. They differ in *adhikārin* and in *pratyaya*. The apparent contradiction is dissolved at the *pratyaya*-level without dissolving anything at the *śabda* or *artha*-level (the *jagat* is the *jagat* in both, but the cognitive mediation is calibrated to a different addressee-state).

The methodology marks this as a register-artifact dissolution: the disagreement at the *śabda*-level is dissolved by the register-translation. AF2 applies: this is register-translation of the same M-question across different addressee-states.

What does *not* dissolve. Vallabha's *Aṇu-bhāṣya* third *adhyāya* (Passage 5 in the corpus, on the bhakti-route as attaining *puruṣottama* and not merely *akṣara*) makes a substantive S-register claim that Śaṅkara's framework does not contain: the bhakti-route attains a distinct and higher object. This is a *real* S-register disagreement that the methodology preserves. AF9: `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` in *prayojana*-hierarchy between *jñāna* and *bhakti* terminations.

### D.2 Rāmānuja's *apṛthak-siddhi* versus Madhva's *bimba-pratibimba*

Rāmānuja in the *Śrī-bhāṣya* 1.1.1 develops the *apṛthak-siddhi* analysis: the *jīva* is "not-separately-established" from Brahman, as a body is not separately established from its soul. The body-soul model installs the *jīva* as ontologically distinct in its mode-of-being but not standing apart from Brahman as substance.

Madhva in his *Brahma-sūtra-bhāṣya* 1.1.1 develops the *bimba-pratibimba* analysis: the *jīva* is *pratibimba* (image) of Brahman the *bimba* (original); the image is real, the original is real, the relation is image-original similarity, the *jīva* has its own *svarūpa* and its own *tāratamya* (gradation).

Operational tuple:
- Rāmānuja: primary register M; *viṣaya* = the *jīva*-*Iśvara* relation in its metaphysical structure; *adhikārin* = the philosophically committed Śrīvaiṣṇava; *prayojana* = correct understanding of the body-of-Brahman doctrine; speech-act = constructive thesis.
- Madhva: primary register M with strong LD; *viṣaya* = the *jīva*-*Iśvara* relation in its metaphysical structure; *adhikārin* = the philosophically committed Tattvavādin; *prayojana* = correct understanding plus refutation of Advaita; speech-act = constructive thesis + anti-Advaita refutation.

Both share *viṣaya* at the level of the metaphysical relation. Both share *scope-inside*. They differ in *adhikārin* (which is the school they address) and in secondary register (Rāmānuja is M-pure; Madhva carries strong LD because of the polemical context). The disagreement is not register-translatable: Rāmānuja and Madhva are giving competing M-claims about the same M-question. AF1 says: this is a genuine disagreement at the *substrate-structure* primitive plus the *identity-relation* primitive. AF9: `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` — Rāmānuja takes essential-predicational, Madhva takes essential-mimetic.

The methodology preserves the disagreement. It does not dissolve it by saying "the disagreement is structural, not verbal" or any of the banned phrases. The disagreement is in the metaphysics. The position is committed to naming where it is.

### D.3 Bhāskara's *aupādhika-bhedābheda* versus Nimbārka's *svābhāvika-bhedābheda*

Bhāskara at *Brahma-sūtra-bhāṣya* 1.1.4 holds that the *bhedābheda* is *aupādhika* — adventitious, condition-dependent. The *jīva*'s difference from Brahman is due to *upādhi*; remove the *upādhi* and the *jīva* is non-different from Brahman.

Nimbārka at *Vedānta-pārijāta-saurabha* on BSū 1.1.1 holds that the *bhedābheda* is *svābhāvika* — natural, *svarūpa*-grounded. Both *bheda* and *abheda* hold by the very nature of *jīva* and *Iśvara*; the *bheda* persists at *mokṣa*.

Operational tuple: both M-register, both same *viṣaya* (structural status of *bheda* and *abheda* in *jīva*–*Iśvara*), both same *scope-inside*, both same broad addressee (philosophically committed Vedāntin in each school). The difference is at the level of the metaphysical question of whether *bheda* is *upādhi*-grounded or *svarūpa*-grounded. The methodology marks this as `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` AF9 at the *substrate-structure* primitive. The disagreement is not about words; it is about the structural status of *bheda* and *abheda* in the metaphysical relation.

### D.4 Caitanya/Jīva Gosvāmī's *acintya-bhedābheda* versus Bhāskara/Nimbārka

Jīva Gosvāmī in the *Sat-sandarbha* on BSū 1.1.1–1.2 introduces a third option: the *bhedābheda* is *acintya* — beyond conceptual grasp. The *jīva* is simultaneously *bhinna* and *abhinna* from Brahman in a way that exceeds the *upādhi*/*svarūpa* dichotomy; the simultaneity is held by *acintya-śakti*.

Operational tuple: M-register with strong LD-refusal. *Acintya* is itself a register-marker: it says that the relation cannot be captured at the LD-level alone; some claim-content survives the dialectical move that Bhāskara and Nimbārka both make.

The methodology marks Jīva's position as a third option, irreducible to *aupādhika* or *svābhāvika*. AF4 applies: *acintya* is a legitimate philosophical position when made as a constructive thesis, but it is not a universal solvent for the methodology. The dispute between Jīva on one side and Bhāskara–Nimbārka on the other is a real metaphilosophical dispute about whether the *bhedābheda* relation can be fully analyzed at the LD-level. AF9: `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` at the meta-level.

### D.5 Madhva's *Mithyātvānumāna-khaṇḍana* versus Śaṅkara's *Adhyāsa-bhāṣya*

Madhva's polemical work attacks the Advaitic *mithyātvānumāna* — the inference that the world is *mithyā* — for *anavasthā*, *vyāghāta*, *ātmāśraya*, *anyonyāśraya*, *cakraka*. The polemic is in LD-register with strong M-grounding.

Śaṅkara's *Adhyāsa-bhāṣya* presents the doctrine of *adhyāsa*: ordinary cognition is *smṛti-rūpaḥ paratra pūrva-dṛṣṭa-avabhāsaḥ* — the shining-forth, elsewhere, of something previously seen, in the form of memory. The bhāṣya is in Φ + S register with M-implication.

The operational tuple analysis is delicate. Madhva attacks the LD reconstruction of *mithyātva* in later Advaita (Padmapāda, Vimuktatman); Śaṅkara's *Adhyāsa-bhāṣya* itself is not in LD-register but in Φ + S register. The disagreement Madhva is staging is, at its sharpest, with the later Advaitic LD that fixes *mithyātva* as an *anirvacanīya* predicate; the disagreement with Śaṅkara himself is more partial.

The methodology marks two things. First, the LD-register disagreement is genuine and survives translation. AF9: `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` between Madhva's anti-*mithyātva* polemic and the later Advaitic reconstruction of *mithyātva*. Second, the disagreement at the Φ-register is more subtle: Madhva is not, at his strongest moments, attacking Śaṅkara's *adhyāsa* analysis but the later Advaitic ontologization of *mithyātva*. AF7: where uncertain about whether Madhva's target is Śaṅkara himself or the later Advaitins, flag it.

### D.6 Madhusūdana's *Advaita-Siddhi* versus Madhusūdana's *Bhakti-Rasāyana*

The same author writes the *Advaita-Siddhi* (rigorous LD defense of Advaita against Madhva's *Mithyātvānumāna-khaṇḍana*) and the *Bhakti-Rasāyana* (treatise on devotion in AD-register). The apparent inconsistency dissolves at the register-level. The *Advaita-Siddhi* is in LD + M + E register; the *Bhakti-Rasāyana* is in AD + S register. The two works occupy different registers and different addressees.

Operational tuple:
- *Advaita-Siddhi*: LD with strong M and E; *viṣaya* = the LD defense of *mithyātva* and *anirvacanīya*; *adhikārin* = the polemicist; *prayojana* = refutation + counter-refutation.
- *Bhakti-Rasāyana*: AD with strong S; *viṣaya* = the post-realization affective relation to the divine; *adhikārin* = the *bhakta*; *prayojana* = invocational analysis.

The methodology dissolves the apparent inconsistency at the register-level. AF8: continuity through registers does not imply continuity of doctrine; the same Madhusūdana is internally coherent in both, but the two works are not a single argument under two surfaces.

A further point that the methodology preserves: the AD-register survives the *jñāna*-completion. Post-realization devotion is intelligible; the *stotrakāra* and the *Advaita-Siddhin* are the same person. This is the structural insight that the user has gestured at in framing the *stotra*-register as post-realization speech (AF5). The methodology operationalizes the insight.

### D.7 Hegel on the absolute versus KCB on alternative absolutes

Hegel at the *Phenomenology* and the *Logic* totalizes at the level of the absolute: the absolute is the totality of moments sublated into Spirit's self-knowing. There is no irreducible plurality at the level of the absolute; whatever appears as plurality is a moment.

KCB at *Studies in Philosophy* II pp. 125–143 holds that there are alternative absolutes — knowing, willing, feeling — that are irreducible to each other and to a single super-absolute. No Hegelian totalization is possible at the level of the absolute.

Operational tuple: both M-register with strong LD-or-E-component. Same *viṣaya* (structure of the absolute when plurality is in play). Same *scope-inside*. Same broad addressee-tier (the speculative-philosophical reader for Hegel; the philosophically reflective reader for KCB). The disagreement is at the level of whether plurality at the absolute level is irreducible. AF9: `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` at the *modal_structure_of_truth* primitive. KCB takes `alternative-irreducible-truths`; Hegel takes `sublated-into-higher-whole`. The disagreement is real and the methodology preserves it.

### D.8 Hegelian *Aufhebung* versus Aurobindean supramental descent

Hegel at the *Phenomenology* and the *Logic* installs the *Aufhebung*: the negative is sublated into the higher whole; the direction is ascent through the labor of the negative.

Aurobindo at *The Life Divine* installs the supramental descent: the supermind is already complete; it descends into the lower hemisphere and transfigures it from within. The direction is descent, even where the empirical movement appears as ascent.

Operational tuple: Hegel in LD + M; Aurobindo in M + S + C. Different secondary registers. Same broad *viṣaya* (the structure of the absolute-relative relation). Different *scope-inside*: Hegel's is the dialectical movement of the concept; Aurobindo's is the supramental-lower transfigurative interaction.

The disagreement is at the level of directionality: *Aufhebung* as ascent-by-labor-of-the-negative versus supramental as prior-completeness transfiguring the lower. AF9: `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` at the *priority_relation* primitive. The two directionalities are not register-translations of each other; they make different claims about what is prior, what is derived, what is the source of dynamism. The methodology preserves the disagreement.

### D.9 Jain *syādvāda* versus Nietzschean perspectivism

Jain *syādvāda* at the *Sūtra-kṛtāṅga* and Akalaṅka's *Aṣṭa-śatī*: discipline of seven-fold conditioned-predication, with each predication qualified by *syāt* — "from a standpoint." The discipline is in SL-register with strong LD and E.

Nietzschean perspectivism at *On the Genealogy of Morals* III.12 and *Nachlass* fragments: all knowledge is interpretive; every "fact" is a perspective-conditioned reading; the will-to-power is the operative force; no perspective is absolute. The position is in HG-register with strong Mh.

The structural convergence: both refuse single-unqualified-predication. The methodology marks the register-difference: Jain *syādvāda* in SL-register preserves truth-talk; Nietzschean perspectivism in HG-register displaces truth-talk into power-talk. AF1: structural convergence on refusal-of-single-truth. AF9: `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` on the *ground_of_multiplicity* — Jain ontological-ranked, Nietzschean symptomatic-of-force-relations.

A genuine affinity remains. A genuine divergence remains. The methodology marks both.

### D.10 Mīmāṃsā *codanā* versus Austin's illocutionary

Mīmāṃsā at *MS* 1.1.2: *codanā-lakṣaṇo 'rtho dharmaḥ*. The *vidhi* is performative-constitutive of *dharma*. The *codanā* does not describe a *dharma* that exists independently; it constitutes it.

Austin at *How To Do Things with Words* Lectures I–IX: every utterance has locutionary, illocutionary, perlocutionary aspects. The illocutionary act is what the speaker is doing in the utterance; the illocutionary act is constitutive in the same sense as the *codanā*.

Operational tuple: Mīmāṃsā in SL + RN + Eth; Austin in SL + Mh. Both *viṣaya* is the speech-act analysis of meaning. Convergence at the structural level on performative-constitutive utterance. AF1: independent arrivals at the speech-act insight.

Divergence: Kumārila preserves the *codanā* / *arthavāda* distinction (performative / descriptive) where Austin drops the performative / constative distinction in favor of a fully performative theory. Kumārila is structurally sharper. AF7: where Austin's terminology is later abandoned by Austin himself, the position uses Kumārila's framing as the cleaner.

### D.11 Bhartṛhari *vāc*-hierarchy versus Derrida *différance*

Bhartṛhari *VP* 1.131: four-level *vāc*-hierarchy with *vaikharī*, *madhyamā*, *paśyantī*, *parā*. The hierarchy is a positive ontology of the levels of *vāc*; each level is a stage of the Real's self-articulation.

Derrida "La différance": *différance* as constitution of meaning at a level below the audible. The *trace* is the mark of constitutive *différance*. The analysis refuses positive ontology; *différance* is *neither real nor unreal*.

Operational tuple: Bhartṛhari in SL + M + C; Derrida in SL + LD with anti-M commitment. Both *viṣaya* = the level below the audible at which meaning is constituted. Same structural insight.

AF1: structural convergence on layered analysis below the audible. AF9: `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` on ontological commitment. Bhartṛhari is metaphysically realist; Derrida is anti-realist about the *différance*. The position takes Bhartṛhari's side, marks the disagreement.

### D.12 Saussurean *valeur* versus Bhartṛhari *sphoṭa*

Saussure *Cours* Part II ch. IV: in language there are only differences; value is constituted by differential position in the system.

Bhartṛhari *VP* 1.44: *śabda eva tu sarvam syād iti sphoṭa-vidāṃ matam* — "*śabda* alone is all, so the *sphoṭa*-knowers hold." The sentence-*sphoṭa* is the primary meaningful unit; the constituents are abstractions.

Operational tuple: Saussure in SL + Mh; Bhartṛhari in SL + M + C. *Viṣaya* both: the structural constitution of meaning. Same structural insight that meaning is constituted at the system-level, not at the level of the isolated unit.

AF1: structural convergence on system-constituted meaning. The substantive ontological commitments differ (Saussure is methodologically neutral on the metaphysics of language; Bhartṛhari has the strong *śabda-advaita*). The position takes the structural convergence and brackets the substantive divergence as a question separately argued.

### D.13 Synthesis at the close of the worked examples

The twelve cross-engagements above are not the whole scope of the methodology's application; they are illustrative. The methodology's commitment is that the same operational tuple — register, scope-inside, scope-outside, addressee, speech-act, discourse-game, Sanskrit-grammar layer — is applied to *every* cross-engagement in the corpus. The case studies are training examples; the discipline is universal.

Several patterns emerge from the twelve cross-engagements that are worth surfacing. First, the most common pattern is *register-translation dissolves apparent contradiction at the surface* but *reveals genuine disagreement at depth*. Śaṅkara–Vallabha is the paradigm. The methodology is not a license to dissolve every disagreement; it is the discipline of *finding where the disagreement actually is* once the register-artifacts are subtracted.

Second, the cross-tradition mappings (Mīmāṃsā ↔ Austin; Bhartṛhari ↔ Derrida; Saussure ↔ Bhartṛhari; Jain ↔ Nietzsche) are consistently AF1-marked. The convergence is structural; the divergence (where there is divergence) is substantive-ontological. The methodology preserves both.

Third, the within-tradition disputes (Bhāskara vs. Nimbārka vs. Jīva; Madhva vs. Śaṅkara vs. later Advaita; Hegel vs. KCB) tend to be `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` cases where the methodology cannot dissolve and does not pretend to. AF9 is operationally heavy in within-tradition disputes; AF1 is operationally heavy in cross-tradition mappings. This is a pattern, not a rule.

Fourth, the methodology occasionally marks a third option that exceeds the available dichotomy. Jīva's *acintya-bhedābheda* (D.4) is the canonical case: a third option that the methodology marks as such, preserving the disagreement with the available dichotomy. AF4 controls *acintya*-moves so that the methodology does not abuse the *acintya*-marker as a universal solvent.

These patterns are not surprising. They reflect the structural fact that traditions converge at the level of insight and diverge at the level of substantive ontology. The methodology's job is to surface both the convergence and the divergence with discipline.

---

## Appendix E. A note on what "convergence" means and does not mean

The article's central evidentiary claim is that several traditions converge on a structural insight about language. The word "convergence" is doing important work. This appendix explains what the word means and what it does not mean.

Convergence does not mean *identity*. The traditions do not say the same things. Bhartṛhari's *vāc*-hierarchy and Plato's four-fold scheme in the *Seventh Letter* are not the same scheme; they have different terms, different number of levels, different operative metaphysics. The convergence is at the level of *structural insight* — both schemes recognize that meaning operates at levels that have to be distinguished, both schemes place the deepest content at a level the audible / written word cannot reach. The structural insight is the convergence; the substantive content of each scheme is not.

Convergence does not mean *influence*. The traditions did not, at the time of their major articulations of the structural insight, have contact with each other. Plato did not read Patañjali; Patañjali did not read Plato; neither could have read Wittgenstein; Wittgenstein did not read Bhartṛhari at any depth; Saussure did not read the Indian grammarians at depth either, though he had some indirect knowledge through the Indo-European philology of his day. The convergences are independent. This is what AF1 marks throughout the article.

Convergence does not mean *translatability*. The technical terms of one tradition do not translate cleanly into the technical terms of another. *Adhikārin* in Mīmāṃsā does not translate as "form of life" in Wittgenstein. The two play structurally analogous roles in their respective theories; that is not the same as being interchangeable. The position uses each technical term in its own tradition with appropriate gloss; it does not pretend that the terms can be substituted across traditions.

Convergence does not mean *Western philosophy was wasting its time and should have been reading Sanskrit*. The Western traditions worked on their own problems and developed their own technical apparatus. The Indian apparatus is sharper at the positive-grammar level; the Wittgensteinian apparatus is sharper at the negative-discipline level; the late-analytic apparatus is sharper at the speech-act and interpretation-theory levels; the continental apparatus is sharper at the priority-of-language and value-as-difference levels; KCB is sharper at the metaphilosophical level. Each tradition contributes something; none subsumes the others. The convergence does not eliminate the distinctive contribution of each tradition.

Convergence does mean: the structural insight that several traditions independently arrived at, with no contact and different vocabularies, is evidence that the insight is tracking the structure of how language and thought relate. The position is committed to this as a substantive philosophical claim. The article is the evidentiary statement of the claim.

---

## Appendix F. A short statement of the position's place in the dialectic

A short statement is owed at the close. The position is the user's; the article is a statement of the position's methodology; the methodology is grounded in the convergence among the seven traditions read. The position is realist, plural, anti-Hegelian on totalization, anti-Derridean on anti-ontology, and committed to the Indian apparatus as the operative positive grammar with Wittgensteinian negative discipline as the operative methodological check. The substantive metaphysical commitments — what the Real is, what the alternative absolutes are, how *bhakti* and *jñāna* fit together, what *Saccidānanda* names — are defended elsewhere in the corpus. The present article is the methodological keystone; it identifies the convergence and articulates the discipline. The substantive metaphysics builds on the methodological discipline; the discipline does not by itself entail the metaphysics.

The position is therefore not a deductive system. It is a coordinated set of commitments: methodological at one level, metaphysical at another, soteriological at a third. The coordination is the position's contribution. The methodological commitments are exhibited in the present article; the metaphysical commitments are exhibited elsewhere; the soteriological commitments are exhibited at the boundary between the *jñāna*-articles and the *bhakti*-articles in the corpus. The coordination is the user's view that the three levels can be held together without internal contradiction, each constituting a piece of a larger philosophical articulation. The article does not defend the coordination at the level of the whole; the corpus does.

The methodological discipline of register is the operational backbone. Without it the substantive commitments at the higher levels are exposed to the standard objections (relativism, eclecticism, incompatibility-with-the-traditions). With it the substantive commitments have a frame within which they can be coherently maintained. The article's contribution is to give the discipline its full articulation and to exhibit the convergence among the traditions that grounds the discipline. The corpus's contribution is to apply the discipline to every load-bearing claim and to defend the substantive commitments on their own grounds.

This is, in the end, what the philosophy of language is for in the position's project. The philosophy of language is not a self-standing inquiry pursued for its own interest. It is the discipline that makes the larger philosophical project possible. The convergence among the traditions is the evidence; the discipline is the instrument; the project is what the discipline serves. The article is the articulation of all three.

---

## Appendix G. Glossary of technical terms used in the article

A short glossary of the technical terms used in the article, with primary-text loci where the term is articulated. The glossary is for the reader who has not yet read the primary texts and wants to follow the article's argument without disruption.

**adhikāra** — Pāṇinian technical term: a governing *sūtra* whose scope extends over a domain of subsequent *sūtras*; everything in the domain is read as scoped under the *adhikāra*. *Aṣṭādhyāyī* passim; the generic mechanism is established at the start of book 3 (*A.* 3.1.1, *pratyayaḥ*, is the most famous *adhikāra*).

**adhikārin** — Mīmāṃsā technical term: the addressee of a *vidhi*-utterance; the eligible recipient whose epistemic state and practical context determines what the utterance can do. *Mīmāṃsā-sūtra* I.2 (the *adhikāra-pāda*) develops the analysis.

**anubandha** (*catur-anubandha*) — Mīmāṃsā fourfold tuple specifying any authoritative discourse: *viṣaya* (subject-matter), *sambandha* (relation to authority), *adhikārin* (addressee), *prayojana* (purpose). The schema is operative throughout the *Mīmāṃsā-sūtra* and is summarized in later digests.

**arthavāda** — Mīmāṃsā technical term: explanatory accompaniment to a *vidhi*; descriptive-supportive rather than performative-constitutive. *Mīmāṃsā-sūtra* I.2.7 ff.

**autpattika-sambandha** — Mīmāṃsā doctrine: the relation between *śabda* and *artha* is intrinsic, not conventionally established. *Mīmāṃsā-sūtra* 1.1.5.

**codanā** — Mīmāṃsā technical term: Vedic injunction; the mark of *dharma*. *Mīmāṃsā-sūtra* 1.1.2.

**différance** — Derridean coinage: the combination of *differing* (spatial) and *deferring* (temporal); the constitutive structure of meaning at a level below any present speech-act. Derrida, "La différance," in *Marges* (Minuit 1972).

**form of life** (*Lebensform*) — Wittgensteinian technical term: the bedrock of agreement-in-practice that makes a language-game intelligible. PI §241–§242.

**hinges** (*Angeln*) — Wittgensteinian technical term in *On Certainty*: propositions exempt from doubt that frame the language-game of knowledge-claims. OC §341–§343.

**illocutionary act** — Austinian technical term: what the speaker is doing in producing an utterance (asserting, asking, promising, etc.); to be distinguished from the locutionary act (the saying) and the perlocutionary act (the effect of the saying). *How To Do Things with Words* Lectures VIII–IX.

**language-game** (*Sprachspiel*) — Wittgensteinian technical term: an activity within which signs, words, and sentences have determinate use, success-conditions, and grammar. PI §23.

**logos** — Greek philosophical term: speech, account, reason, sentence. In the *Sophist* at 261c–264b, the *logos* is the *sumplokē* of *onoma* and *rhēma*.

**mahāvākya** — Vedāntic technical term: a great utterance; canonically the four are *tat tvam asi*, *ahaṃ brahmāsmi*, *prajñānaṃ brahma*, *ayam ātmā brahma*. The *mahāvākya*s are the load-bearing scriptural utterances for the *jñāna-mārga*.

**naya** — Jain technical term: a standpoint; one of the seven *nayas* (*naigama*, *saṃgraha*, *vyavahāra*, *ṛjusūtra*, *śabda*, *samabhirūḍha*, *evambhūta*). Each *naya* gives a determinate but partial standpoint on the real.

**nirvacana** — Yāska's technical term for etymological derivation; the method of recovering a word's meaning from its root. *Nirukta* I.20.

**onoma** / **rhēma** — Greek philosophical terms: name and verb; the two constituents whose *sumplokē* makes a *logos*. *Sophist* 261d–262d.

**paśyantī** — Bhartṛharian level of *vāc*: visionary, undifferentiated pre-articulation. *Vākyapadīya* 1.131.

**prayojana** — Mīmāṃsā technical term: the purpose for which a discourse is conducted. Component of the *catur-anubandha*.

**pratyaya** — Patañjalian technical term: the apprehension or mental form that mediates between *śabda* and *artha*. *Mahābhāṣya* on *A.* 1.2.45 and passim. Distinct from the homonymous grammatical *pratyaya* (suffix).

**śabda** — Patañjalian technical term: word, sound, utterance. Distinguished from *dhvani* (mere noise) and from *artha* (the referent) and from *pratyaya* (the apprehension). *Mahābhāṣya* on *A.* 1.1.1 (the *Paspaśāhnika*) and passim.

**sambandha** — Mīmāṃsā / Vedāntic technical term: the relation of a discourse to its authoritative source. Component of the *catur-anubandha*.

**signifiant** / **signifié** — Saussurean technical terms: the sound-image and the concept that together constitute the linguistic sign. *Cours* Part I ch. I.

**sphoṭa** — Bhartṛharian technical term: the unitary meaning-bearing entity that "bursts forth" at the moment of recognition. *Vākyapadīya* 1.44–52.

**sumplokē** — Platonic technical term: the interweaving of *onoma* and *rhēma* that constitutes a *logos*. *Sophist* 262a.

**sūnyatā** — Buddhist (especially Madhyamaka) technical term: emptiness; the absence of *svabhāva* (own-being); usually paired with *pratītyasamutpāda* (dependent co-arising). Not the same as Derridean *différance*: *śūnyatā* preserves a positive ontological commitment to dependent co-arising; *différance* does not.

**svataḥ-prāmāṇya** — Mīmāṃsā doctrine: the self-validity of cognition; cognitive validity is presumed until specifically defeated. *Ślokavārttika* in the *Codanā-sūtra* exposition.

**syādvāda** — Jain technical doctrine: qualified assertion; the seven-fold predication *syāt asti, syāt nāsti, syāt asti-nāsti, syāt avaktavyam, syāt asti avaktavyam, syāt nāsti avaktavyam, syāt asti-nāsti avaktavyam*. *Sūtra-kṛtāṅga*; Akalaṅka *Aṣṭa-śatī*.

**trace** — Derridean technical term: the mark of constitutive *différance*; every sign is the trace of what it is not and of what it defers. *De la grammatologie* Part I.

**vāc** — Sanskrit term: speech, especially in the layered sense that the *vāc*-hierarchy articulates. *Ṛgveda* 1.164.45; *Vākyapadīya* 1.131.

**vaikharī** / **madhyamā** / **paśyantī** / **parā** — the four levels of *vāc* in the Bhartṛharian and Kashmir-Śaiva analyses. *VP* 1.131; *Īśvara-pratyabhijñā-kārikā* 1.5.13.

**valeur** — Saussurean technical term: the value of a sign as constituted by its differential position in the system. *Cours* Part II ch. IV.

**vidhi** — Mīmāṃsā technical term: the injunction; the performative-constitutive utterance whose mark is *codanā*. *Mīmāṃsā-sūtra* 1.1.2 and passim.

**viṣaya** — Mīmāṃsā / general Sanskrit technical term: subject-matter; what a discourse is about. Component of the *catur-anubandha*.

**Weltbild** (world-picture) — Wittgensteinian technical term in *On Certainty*: the inherited background against which true/false distinctions are made; not itself a true/false claim. OC §94.

The glossary is for orientation only; the primary-text citations point the reader to the works where each term is articulated. The article's argument depends on the texts, not on the glossary.

---

## Appendix H. Extended close reading of the *Paspaśāhnika*

The *Paspaśāhnika*'s technical density warrants further exposition than was given in Part 2.2. The section is the most concentrated piece of philosophical reflection on language in the early Indian literature; its argument-structure is non-linear and its citations of earlier authorities (especially the Vedic *vāc*-passages and the *padapāṭha* tradition) require unpacking. This appendix takes the section in sequence and exhibits the philosophical move at each step.

### H.1 The opening question — *atha śabdānuśāsanam*

The text opens *atha śabdānuśāsanam*: "Now, the discipline of words." The opening word *atha* — "now" — is, in the Sanskrit *śāstra*-tradition, a marker of beginning that carries the weight of authority. The text is announcing itself as a *śāstra*, a discipline; the *śāstra* is *śabdānuśāsana*, the discipline of words. The first question Patañjali asks is: of *which* words? The answer: *laukikānāṃ vaidikānāṃ ca* — of worldly and Vedic. The discipline covers both. This is a substantive claim. The grammatical tradition is not restricted to Vedic Sanskrit; it covers ordinary language as well.

The next question is the *prayojana*: why is the discipline pursued? Patañjali gives five reasons (the *pañca-prayojana*): *rakṣā* (preservation of Vedic *śabda*), *ūha* (modification of mantras for ritual application), *āgama* (scriptural authority for grammatical conclusions), *laghv* (compression, the value of a compact grammar), *asaṃdeha* (removal of doubt about correct usage). The five reasons together constitute the *prayojana*-tuple for the entire *Mahābhāṣya*; they are the *catur-anubandha*-like specification of what the work is for.

For the present argument the first reason — *rakṣā* of Vedic *śabda* — is central. The grammatical tradition exists, before anything else, to preserve the integrity of the Vedic utterance. The Vedic utterance carries *dharma*; the *dharma* depends on the utterance being preserved exactly; the grammar is in service to the preservation. This installs a hierarchy: the *śabda* is prior; the *śāstra* (grammar) is subordinate to the *śabda*. The Western tradition's default ordering — grammar as a tool for analyzing language, language as a tool for thought, thought as a tool for accessing the world — is reversed. In Patañjali's framing, *śabda* is the primary phenomenon; the *śāstra* serves *śabda*; *śabda* serves *dharma*; *dharma* is what is at stake.

The structural insight is that the *prayojana* of the discipline is constitutive of how it operates. A grammar pursued for *rakṣā* of a sacred utterance is different from a grammar pursued for descriptive purposes or for pedagogical purposes. The methodology takes this as paradigmatic: every discipline is shaped by the *prayojana* it serves. To read the *Mahābhāṣya* as if it were a descriptive linguistic treatise is to misread; the *prayojana* of the work is *rakṣā* of the Vedic *śabda*, and the technical apparatus is calibrated to that *prayojana*.
