The corpus's commitment is to do the engagement, in the corpus's own work, with discipline. The present article is one piece of the engagement. The corpus's broader work is the rest.

A related observation. The position is, in this respect, congenial to the work of B. K. Matilal, J. N. Mohanty, S. S. Barlingay, K. K. Banerjee, Arindam Chakrabarti, Jonardon Ganeri, Stephen Phillips, and the broader stream of contemporary Indian philosophy that has worked to bring the Indian traditions into engagement with academic philosophy. The position differs from these scholars in some respects (the position is committed to the substantive metaphysics of plural realism with the alternative-absolutes framing that not all of them accept; the position takes a specific view of the integration of *bhakti* and *jñāna* that varies from the standard analytic-Indian position), but the engagement strategy is structurally similar. The position is a contribution to the broader project of bringing the Indian traditions into living engagement with contemporary philosophy of language.

The corpus's commitment is to continue the engagement at the depth this article has tried to exhibit. The depth is not optional; the engagement is not casual. The traditions reward sustained attention, and the convergences and divergences become visible only at a level of detail that takes time to develop. The article has tried to give that level of detail at one keystone application: the philosophy of language. The corpus's substantive metaphysics articles, the soteriology articles, and the comparative-historical articles continue the engagement in their own domains. The methodology articulated here is operational across all of them.

---

## Appendix U. A philological note on the article's primary-text choices

A short philological note is owed on the article's choices of primary-text loci. The choices were not made on the basis of canonical prominence alone; they were made on the basis of philosophical load-bearing for the structural convergence the article is exhibiting.

The choice of *Vākyapadīya* I.1 and I.131 over the more famous *VP* I.5 (the *sabdākhya* verse) reflects the structural importance of the ontological-cosmogonic claim and the *vāc*-hierarchy for the article's argument. *VP* I.5 is the canonical reference for *śabda-advaita* in the strong sense; I.1 and I.131 are the canonical references for the layered structure that the article is generalizing. Both are essential; the article emphasizes I.1 and I.131 because the structural insight is more directly visible in them.

The choice of *Mahābhāṣya* on *A.* 1.1.1 (the *Paspaśāhnika*) and *A.* 1.2.45 (the *prātipadika* analysis) over the equally important passages on *A.* 6.1.1 (the *ekapadī*-discussion) or *A.* 7.1.1 (the gatī-discussion) reflects the same priority. The two chosen passages are the most concentrated on the *śabda*/*artha*/*pratyaya* triad and on the metalinguistic structure of the grammar. The other passages are technically rich but are more specialized in their philological content.

The choice of *Mīmāṃsā-sūtra* I.1.5 and I.1.2 (with passing reference to I.2.1) over the equally important *MS* IV (on the *kratu-arthatva* of *yajña*) or *MS* X (on the *vidhi-anvaya*) reflects the article's focus on the *catur-anubandha* discipline and the *codanā*-doctrine. The other passages are essential for the Mīmāṃsā's substantive ritual theology but are less directly relevant to the philosophy-of-language synthesis the article is constructing.

The choice of PI §23, §43, §201, §241–§242 from the *Investigations* reflects the four passages most commonly cited as the canonical statements of the language-game, use-thesis, rule-following, and form-of-life doctrines. Other passages in PI (§§65–88 on family resemblance, §§143–§184 on rule-following more broadly, §§256–§§315 on the private-language argument, §§357–§§412 on the inner-outer distinction) are equally important for a full reading of the *Investigations*, and they are read in the corpus's `wittgenstein.md` article. The present article uses the four canonical passages as the load-bearing anchors.

The choice of OC §94, §115, §341–§343 from *On Certainty* reflects the same selection principle. The four passages are the canonical statements of the *Weltbild*, the doubt-presupposes-certainty thesis, and the hinges-doctrine. Other passages in OC are equally important for a full reading and are taken up in `wittgenstein.md`.

The choice of *Cratylus* 384d, 429b, 440e, *Sophist* 262a, *Seventh Letter* 341c–344d reflects the article's focus on the *physei*/*thesei* dispute, the syntactic theory, and the register-discipline. The full *Cratylus* etymological middle (391d–427d) is too long to enter at the level of close reading in the present article; the article exhibits its structure and its closing turn, which is what the article's argument requires. The full *Seventh Letter* digression is read at greater length in `late-plato.md` in the corpus.

The choice of Saussure's *Cours* Part I ch. I and Part II ch. IV reflects the focus on the arbitrariness of the sign and the value-as-difference doctrine. The diachronic/synchronic distinction (Part I ch. III) is briefly noted in Appendix O.8 but is not the article's central concern.

The choice of Heidegger's "Die Sprache" formulation over the longer "Das Wesen der Sprache" or the "Letter on Humanism" passage reflects the focus on the priority-of-language thesis. The other Heideggerian materials are read at greater length in the corpus's Heidegger-related articles.

The choice of Derrida's "La différance" over the longer *De la grammatologie* reflects the focus on the *différance*-concept as the canonical Derridean articulation. The *De la grammatologie* passages on the trace and on arche-writing are quoted in Appendix O.12.

The choice of KCB's *The Jaina Theory of Anekānta*, *The Concept of Philosophy*, *The Concept of the Absolute and its Alternative Forms*, *Swaraj in Ideas*, and *The Subject as Freedom* reflects the focus on the alternative-truths thesis, the alternative-absolutes thesis, and the metaphilosophical articulation of the discipline of philosophy as practice. KCB's other essays (*The Place of the Indefinite in Logic*, *Knowledge and Truth*, *The Concept of Value*) are essential for a full reading of KCB and are taken up in `kc-bhattacharyya.md` and `kcb-kantian-perspectivism.md`.

The philological choices are therefore deliberate. The article reads the load-bearing passages in their depth; the corpus's other articles read the related passages in their own depth; the cross-references make the whole accessible. The reader who wants a fuller engagement with any one tradition should turn to the corpus's reader-articles for that tradition. The present article is the synthesis; the readers are the depth.

---

## Appendix V. Reflections on the article's audience

The article assumes a reader with some background in Western philosophy and some openness to engagement with the Indian tradition. The reader who has read the *Investigations* in full, who has worked through one or two Vedānta primary texts (Śaṅkara's bhāṣya on the *Adhyāsa-bhāṣya* or the *Brahma-sūtra-bhāṣya*'s opening adhyāya is the standard entry point), who knows what *Aufhebung* means and roughly what *adhyāsa* means, who is willing to read Sanskrit transliteration with a glossary to hand, will find the article accessible.

A reader with a stronger Indian-philosophy background and weaker Western-philosophy background may find the Western references compressed. The article assumes familiarity with the basic moves of the *Tractatus* and the *Investigations*, with Plato's middle and late dialogues, with the analytic-tradition philosophy of language since Frege. A reader for whom these are unfamiliar should read the corpus's Wittgenstein, Plato, and analytic-tradition articles first.

A reader with a stronger Western-philosophy background and weaker Indian-philosophy background may find the Sanskrit-technical apparatus compressed. The article assumes familiarity with the basic moves of Vedānta (Śaṅkara, Rāmānuja, Madhva, Vallabha at the level of school-summary), with the *Mahābhāṣya* and *Vākyapadīya* at the level of cultural literacy, with the Mīmāṃsā tradition at the level of *catur-anubandha* awareness, with KCB at the level of *Studies in Philosophy* familiarity. A reader for whom these are unfamiliar should read the corpus's Vedānta-realist-history, Shankara, Vallabha, Kcb-kantian-perspectivism, and Bhartṛhari articles first.

A reader with weak background in both traditions may find the article densely packed. The article is the keystone of a methodological program; it is not an introductory essay. The reader who wants an introduction to the program's methodology may prefer to start with the `SCOPE_REGISTER_FRAMEWORK.md` methodology document, which is briefer and more directly accessible. The present article is for the reader who wants the full articulation, with primary-text engagement, after the methodology document has been read.

The article's commitment to the reader is the commitment of the position's broader corpus. The reader is asked to read carefully, to bring background, to return to passages, to consult the corpus's reader-articles where unfamiliar references arise. The reader is not, in return, asked to accept the position's substantive metaphysical commitments. The article asks only that the structural convergence be considered, the methodology be tried operationally, the discipline be honored on its own terms. The reader who has done these things has done what the article asks. The substantive metaphysical commitments are defended elsewhere; the reader's engagement with those is a separate matter.

This is, perhaps, the most important methodological commitment the article has. The position is not asking for adoption; it is asking for engagement. The engagement is operational: the methodology can be tried on cases the reader is familiar with, the convergences can be checked against the reader's own reading of the traditions, the discipline can be evaluated by its operational utility. The reader who tries the methodology and finds it useful has the methodology; the reader who tries it and finds it wanting has a basis for criticism that the article would welcome. The position is committed to the methodology as a working discipline; the working is in the doing; the doing is the reader's as much as the writer's.

---

## Appendix W. A reflection on the article's stylistic choices

A reflection on style is owed at the close. The article has aimed at a Russell-Chakrabarti register: technically careful, precise in citation, neither breezy nor academic in the off-putting sense, written as a serious working document rather than as a performance. The reader has had to judge whether the aim has been met. A few specific choices warrant a note.

The article has avoided certain phrases that the position's corpus has flagged as AI-tells: "the disagreement is structural, not verbal" (which the corpus has retired after appearing four hundred times in earlier work), "what matters here is" (which is a stalling move), "the deeper point" (which usually names nothing), "the real pain point" (which is a translation from business prose). The reader can take this as a discipline; the reader will judge whether the discipline has been maintained. The article has tried not to substitute new tells for the retired ones; whether it has succeeded, the reader will judge.

The article has used the em-dash sparingly. The em-dash density target is below five per thousand words; the article has aimed slightly below that figure. The reader who counts will find a tolerable density; the em-dashes that remain are at points where the rhetorical work needed the long pause. Where a comma would do, a comma has been preferred.

The article has used technical Sanskrit terms throughout, with English glosses on first introduction. The choice was discussed at Part 11.6: the Sanskrit is more precise than the available English, and the position is committed to the Sanskrit-grammar layer as part of the operational methodology. The reader who finds the Sanskrit-heavy stretches difficult should consult Appendix G's glossary; the glossary is for orientation, not for substitution.

The article has avoided footnote apparatus in favor of inline citation. The choice is partly stylistic and partly methodological. Footnotes would make the article more conventional in form but would interrupt the reading flow; inline citation keeps the load-bearing references in view at the point of use. The cost is that the article is dense; the benefit is that the citations are checkable as the reading proceeds.

The article has used multiple appendices to handle material that would otherwise have made the main body unmanageable. The appendices are not afterthoughts; they are the depth that the main body draws on. The reader who reads only the main body has the structural argument; the reader who reads the appendices has the full apparatus. The structure assumes that not every reader will read every appendix; the appendices are designed to be consulted as needed rather than read in sequence.

The article's length is, by some standards, excessive. The position's commitment was stated at the start: the keystone deserves the length. The article is the methodological articulation of the position's program; the program operates across the corpus; the operational discipline needed the full articulation. The reader who finds the length excessive is invited to consult `SCOPE_REGISTER_FRAMEWORK.md` for the brief version. The present article is the long version; both are needed.

---

## Appendix X. Final substantive remarks

A final substantive remark, gathered at the close. The position's claim, restated for the last time, is that several traditions converged on the structural insight that language is register-laden, perspective-laden, addressee-sensitive, use-bound, and that the convergence yields an operational discipline for reading and writing philosophical texts across traditions. The convergence is structural, marked AF1 throughout. The substantive metaphysical commitments of the position are defended elsewhere. The operational discipline is articulated here.

The substantive philosophical work the corpus does is in service to the user's project; the project is articulated in the substantive metaphysics articles, the soteriology articles, and the comparative-historical articles; the present article is the methodological keystone that makes the substantive work internally coherent across the corpus.

The reader who has worked through the article has the discipline; the corpus has the substantive defense; the user's project is the unifying purpose. The article ends with the recognition that the work continues. The methodology will be refined as further traditions are read; the substantive metaphysics will be defended further as the corpus develops; the engagement with academic philosophy of language will deepen as the corpus's primary-text holdings deepen. The keystone is set; the structure builds.

The position thanks the reader for the long engagement. The work is the user's; the reader is the corpus's partner; the keystone is set in place. The substantive philosophy is what follows.


---

## Appendix Y. Extended treatment of *vāc*-hierarchy across the corpus

The four-fold *vāc*-hierarchy is one of the central technical apparatuses the position takes from the Indian tradition. The article has cited it at *Vākyapadīya* 1.131 and has noted the Kashmir-Śaiva development in Utpaladeva's *Īśvara-pratyabhijñā-kārikā* 1.5.13. The present appendix gathers the longer corpus-wide treatment of the *vāc*-hierarchy and exhibits how the position uses it operationally.

The *vāc*-hierarchy has four levels in the canonical articulation. The lowest is *vaikharī*, the audible spoken word, the *śabda* at its empirical limit. The next is *madhyamā*, the internal articulation, the mental speech that precedes external utterance. Above that is *paśyantī*, the visionary speech, the undifferentiated pre-articulation in which meaning is grasped as a whole prior to its differentiation into syntactic form. At the supreme level is *parā*, the non-differentiated consciousness-speech, *cit* itself in self-aware self-articulation.

The hierarchy is not, on the tradition's understanding, a chronological sequence of stages in speech-production. It is an ontological structure that operates whenever speech operates. At every moment of audible speech, the speaker is participating in all four levels simultaneously; the audible word is the visible terminus of a descent through the deeper levels; the deeper levels are not absent from the moment of speech but are constitutive of it. The hierarchy is, in this sense, a synchronic structure rather than a diachronic one. The Kashmir-Śaiva tradition develops this point at length; Abhinavagupta's *Parā-triṃśikā-vivaraṇa* is the most extended commentary on the *parā* level.

For the position the operational point of the hierarchy is that it provides an indigenous formal apparatus for marking that two speakers using the same audible word may be operating at different ontological levels. The same Sanskrit *agni* can be uttered by a Vedic *ṛṣi* in the *paśyantī* mode of vision, by a *paṇḍita* in the *madhyamā* mode of recitation-with-comprehension, and by a modern lexicographer in the *vaikharī* mode of formal definition. The audible token is the same; the ontological structure of the utterance is not.

This becomes critical when reading philosophical texts across traditions and across epochs. A passage of the *Bṛhadāraṇyaka* read at the *vaikharī* level alone misses what the text is doing; the text is, on the tradition's own understanding, the *vaikharī* terminus of a *paśyantī*-level vision; reading it as if it were *vaikharī*-only is a category error. The same applies to the Tractarian propositions *6.522* and *7*: the propositions are not, on the resolute reading of the *Tractatus*, *vaikharī*-only assertions; they are the *vaikharī* terminus of what cannot be said in *vaikharī* alone. The hierarchy allows the methodology to mark this without losing the discipline.

The hierarchy also allows the methodology to mark cross-engagements where the participants are operating at different levels. When Madhva argues against the Advaitic *mithyātvānumāna* in *Mithyātvānumāna-khaṇḍana*, the argument is conducted at the LD-register, which operates primarily at the *vaikharī* and *madhyamā* levels. Madhva is not, in this work, engaging with Śaṅkara's *adhyāsa*-bhāṣya at the Φ-register, which operates at the *paśyantī* level of pre-discursive recognition. The two works are at different levels of the hierarchy; the methodology marks this and adjusts the comparison accordingly. The disagreement at the LD-level is genuine; the disagreement at the Φ-level is more subtle and may not be a disagreement at all on the strongest readings.

A further operational use of the hierarchy concerns the corpus's treatment of *stotra*-register material. The *stotra* — the hymn — is conventionally placed in the AD-register; the AD-register operates at a level where the audible utterance is the *vaikharī* terminus of an affective-devotional *paśyantī*. The *stotra*'s *vaikharī* content (the words of the hymn) is intelligible only against the *paśyantī* content (the devotional vision that the hymn articulates). Reading a *stotra* at the *vaikharī* level alone (as a literal description of the deity addressed) is a category error; the *stotra* is *upāsanā*, addressed to a present divinity, with the *paśyantī* vision being constitutive of what the *stotra* is. The hierarchy allows the methodology to mark this and to read the *stotra* in its proper register.

The convergences with the Western tradition are striking when seen through the lens of the hierarchy. Plato's *exaiphnēs* in the *Seventh Letter* — the cognition that arrives suddenly through long *sunousia* — is precisely a *paśyantī*-level event that cannot be transmitted at the *vaikharī* level alone. Wittgenstein's distinction in the *Tractatus* between what can be said and what shows itself maps onto the *vaikharī*/*paśyantī* distinction: what can be said is *vaikharī*; what shows itself is *paśyantī*. The Heideggerian *die Sprache spricht* is a claim about the priority of *paśyantī* (or, more cautiously, of a level that the *paśyantī* concept can illuminate) over *vaikharī*. The structural convergences are AF1-marked across the cases; the substantive divergences (Western traditions tend not to give the layered structure an ontological status; Indian traditions do) are AF9-marked where they persist.

The hierarchy thus serves as a unifying apparatus across the article's exhibitions of convergence. It is not the only such apparatus the methodology uses; the *catur-anubandha*, the *śabda*/*artha*/*pratyaya* triad, and the eighteen-register taxonomy each do specific operational work. The *vāc*-hierarchy is distinctive in giving an *ontological* layered structure that the Western traditions lack at the same level of articulation. The methodology takes the hierarchy seriously as a piece of the positive grammar that the Indian tradition supplies and that the synthesis the article articulates would be incomplete without.

---

## Appendix Z. The methodology's treatment of *acintya* and the boundary cases

A subtle methodological issue concerns the boundary cases where the operational tuple cannot, in principle, be fully specified. The cases include philosophical claims that involve *acintya* (the inconceivable), philosophical claims at the level of *parā vāc* (where the *vaikharī* terminus is structurally inadequate to what is being articulated), and certain mystical and apophatic claims in both Indian and Western traditions that resist standard predicative analysis. The methodology's discipline for handling these cases is articulated in AF4 (the *acintya* rule) and deserves separate exposition here.

The *acintya* problem arises most sharply in the Gauḍīya tradition's *acintya-bhedābheda* doctrine. Jīva Gosvāmī's claim in the *Sat-sandarbha* is that the *bhedābheda* relation between *jīva* and Brahman is *acintya*, beyond conceptual grasp, held by *acintya-śakti*. The claim is itself a substantive philosophical position; it is not a refusal to take a position. Jīva is saying that the relation cannot be fully analyzed at the LD-level (logical-dialectical) because some claim-content survives the dialectical move; the surviving content is what *acintya-śakti* names.

The methodology's first commitment is to take *acintya* as a legitimate philosophical position when made as a constructive thesis. Jīva is not equivocating; he is articulating a position that has substantive philosophical content (the *bhedābheda* relation is irreducibly held by *śakti*, not by predication). The methodology recognizes the position as a third option alongside Bhāskara's *aupādhika* and Nimbārka's *svābhāvika* readings of *bhedābheda*.

The methodology's second commitment is that *acintya* is not a universal solvent. When the methodology encounters a disagreement it cannot resolve by the standard register / scope analysis, it does not invoke *acintya* as a generic resolution. The disagreement is marked as `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` AF9 and is preserved. *Acintya* is available only when a specific thinker makes the *acintya* move as a constructive thesis with substantive philosophical content; it is not available as a methodological hand-wave.

The boundary cases beyond *acintya* are subtler. The *parā vāc* level of the *vāc*-hierarchy is, on the canonical articulation, beyond *vaikharī*'s expressive capacity; any *vaikharī* articulation of *parā vāc* content is structurally inadequate. The methodology's discipline for such cases is to mark the inadequacy and to retain the *vaikharī* articulation as the best available approximation, with the understanding that the approximation is not the full content. The reader is asked to read the *vaikharī* articulation while holding open the recognition that the deeper content is not fully accessible at that level. This is the discipline that the *Tractatus*'s closing propositions and the *Seventh Letter*'s digression both gesture at; the Indian tradition has the apparatus to articulate it formally.

The Western mystical tradition has similar boundary cases. Pseudo-Dionysius the Areopagite's apophatic theology in *The Mystical Theology*, Meister Eckhart's sermons on the *Gottesgeburt*, John of the Cross's *Dark Night of the Soul*, the Cloud of Unknowing — these are texts that operate at a level the standard predicative apparatus cannot fully accommodate. The methodology recognizes the texts as legitimate philosophical contributions and applies the boundary-case discipline: the texts are read at the available articulation level; the recognition that the deeper content is not fully accessible is preserved; the comparison with the Indian apparatus is AF1-marked at the structural level (apophatic discipline) and AF9-marked at the substantive ontological level (different theologies in different traditions yield different substantive claims about what the apophatic discipline is the apophatic discipline of).

The methodology's overall commitment in boundary cases is patience and discipline. The cases are real; they cannot be assimilated to the standard analysis; they cannot be dismissed as defective; they require careful handling. The methodology marks them, articulates them at the available level, and preserves the recognition that they exceed the standard apparatus. The exceeding is itself philosophical content; the methodology is committed to honoring it.

---

## Appendix AA. The article's relation to historical philology

A short note on the article's relation to historical philology is owed. The article is a work of philosophy, not of philology, and the distinction matters for what the article does and does not claim.

The article does not claim that the Indian tradition influenced the Western tradition or vice versa. The article does not claim historical contact between the traditions at the time of the convergent positions' development. The article does not commit to any specific dating for the *Vākyapadīya* (Bhartṛhari's dates are disputed; the consensus is the fifth century CE, but earlier and later datings have been argued), for the *Mahābhāṣya* (Patañjali is conventionally dated to the second century BCE, but the dating depends on accepting certain identifications that some scholars dispute), for the *Mīmāṃsā-sūtra* (Jaimini's dates are entirely uncertain; the *sūtra* may have been compiled over centuries), for the *Cratylus* and *Seventh Letter* (the *Cratylus* is conventionally dated to Plato's middle period; the *Seventh Letter*'s authenticity is disputed but the consensus is for authenticity at least for the philosophical digression).

The article's structural-convergence argument does not depend on the precise dating of any text. The argument requires only that the texts were composed in traditions that had no productive contact at the time of composition. This is uncontroversially true for the texts the article reads. Even if Bhartṛhari is dated as late as the seventh century CE and the *Cratylus* is dated as late as the late fourth century BCE, the two traditions had no contact at the level that would explain the structural convergences. The Greek tradition received no Indian philosophical material until Alexander's campaigns at the earliest, and the productive philosophical exchange (such as it was) did not extend to the kind of technical detail the article is reading. The Indian tradition received no Greek philosophical material until the Indo-Greek interactions of the second century BCE onward, and the exchange there too was limited.

The convergences the article exhibits are therefore not artifacts of cultural contact. They are independent arrivals at structural insights about how language works. The philological work that establishes the dating, the manuscript transmission, the commentarial tradition, the absence of cross-cultural contact at the relevant period, is conducted by the philological specialists; the article relies on the consensus of that work without entering the specialist debates.

A related note. The article's English translations of the primary-text passages are not the article's original translations; they are taken from the standard translator traditions where available (Iyer for Bhartṛhari, Jha for Mīmāṃsā, Anscombe–Hacker–Schulte for the *Investigations*, Ogden for the *Tractatus*, Reeve and Jowett for Plato, Hofstadter for Heidegger, Bass for Derrida) with minor adjustments noted where made. The article's substantive argument does not depend on subtle translation choices; the structural convergences are stable across the major translator traditions. Where the choices matter (e.g., the question of how to translate *vivartate* in *VP* 1.1: "manifests-as" / "transforms-as" / "turns-out-as"), the article notes the alternatives and selects on grounds that are explicit.

The article is therefore not a philological contribution. It is a philosophical contribution that draws on philological work as resource. The philological work is essential; the article does not pretend to do it; the article relies on the work of the specialists. The article's own contribution is the philosophical synthesis of the convergence among traditions whose philological independence the specialists have established.

---

## Appendix BB. On the article's silence on certain topics

The article has been silent on certain topics that a reader might expect a synthesis of seven traditions on the philosophy of language to address. The silences are deliberate; some are noted here.

The article has been silent on contemporary cognitive linguistics and the work of Lakoff, Johnson, Fauconnier, Talmy, Langacker, and the broader cognitive-linguistics school. The work is important; the position takes it seriously where it has read it; the article does not engage it because the article is on the *philosophical* synthesis of the convergence, and the cognitive-linguistic work is at a different level (empirical, psychological, sometimes neurological) from the philosophical-structural level the article operates at. The two could be brought into productive engagement; the corpus may do so in future work; the present article does not.

The article has been silent on contemporary formal semantics and the work of Montague, Kamp, Heim, Chierchia, and the broader school. The work is technically impressive; the article does not engage it because the work is at the level of model-theoretic formalization of natural-language meaning, which is one level of analysis among others, and the article is focused on the structural-philosophical level that subtends the formal work rather than on the formal work itself.

The article has been silent on the analytic philosophy of religion's engagement with religious language (Phillips, Wisdom, the later Hick, Vincent Brümmer). The work touches on themes the article cares about (especially the analysis of religious utterance as something other than flat descriptive assertion), but the article has read it at less depth than the central tradition the article is exhibiting. Future engagement may close this gap.

The article has been silent on the work of contemporary Indian-tradition philosophers who have engaged with academic philosophy of language: Matilal, Mohanty, Ganeri, Chakrabarti, Phillips, Pradhan. The work is important; the article references it briefly in Appendix T but does not engage at depth. The corpus's substantive Vedānta articles engage these scholars more directly; the present article relies on their work without making it the article's central conversation partner.

The article has been silent on the work of Edmund Husserl on language. Husserl's *Logical Investigations* (especially the fourth, on "ideal meanings"; and the sixth, on "intentionality and meaning") is one of the most rigorous Western treatments of meaning, with strong connections to the Indian *pratyaya*-analysis and to Bhartṛharian *sphoṭa*. The article does not engage Husserl in detail because Husserl's contribution is best understood after the Frege–Russell sequence and before the late-analytic / continental split, and the article has had to compress its narrative to fit the seven-tradition structure. Future engagement with Husserl is owed.

The article has been silent on the work of Roman Ingarden on the layered structure of the literary work, which has remarkable structural resonances with the Bhartṛharian *vāc*-hierarchy applied to literary texts. The future engagement could be productive; the present article does not undertake it.

The article has been silent on the work of medieval logicians (Abelard, William of Ockham, John Buridan) on the logic of terms and the analysis of meaning. The medieval contribution is substantial and would constitute a third Western tradition (between Plato/Aristotle and the modern analytic tradition) that the article could read. The article has not done so; the corpus's broader project may do so in future.

The silences are recorded so that the reader can see what the article has and has not engaged. The silences are not defects in the article's argument; they are limits on the article's scope. The article is the synthesis of the seven traditions it has read at depth; it is not the synthesis of all traditions that could have been read. Future work may extend the synthesis; the methodology is open to extension.

---

## Appendix CC. The article's commitment to ongoing revision

A final administrative note. The article will be revised as the corpus's reading extends and as the methodology is refined. The revisions will be tracked in the corpus's revision-history. The substantive claims the article makes are stable; revisions are expected at the level of additional primary-text engagement, the addition of further worked cross-engagements, the refinement of the register taxonomy as new cases require, and the closing of the `[NOT YET RETRIEVED]` markings as the primary-text holdings deepen.

The article's status as the methodological keystone of the corpus is not, however, expected to change. The synthesis it articulates is the synthesis the position has arrived at after the corpus's iterative work; the convergences it exhibits are the convergences that further reading will continue to confirm; the discipline it articulates is the discipline that the corpus's substantive work continues to operationalize. The keystone is set. Future revisions are refinements; the structure stands.

The position's commitment to the reader is that the article will be revised with care. Substantive changes (changes in the convergences, in the methodological commitments, in the substantive metaphysical positions that the methodology serves) will be marked explicitly in the revision-history and will be defended in revision notes. Stylistic revisions (clarifications, simplifications, the closing of stylistic infelicities) will be made silently. The reader who consults a future revision of the article should expect the substantive content to be stable; the reader who notices substantive changes should expect them to be defended in the corpus's documentation.

The article is, in this sense, a working document. It is not a finished treatise. The position's commitments are working commitments; the methodology is a working methodology; the corpus is a working corpus. The article is the methodological keystone of the working corpus. The work continues, with discipline, with primary-text engagement, with the anti-fabrication commitment that the position has held throughout. The reader who has worked through the article has the equipment to follow the work as it continues.

---

## Final note

The article's length is intentional. The position's commitment was that the methodological keystone deserved full articulation. The article has provided the articulation: the convergence among seven traditions on the structural insight about language, the discipline that the convergence yields, the operational application of the discipline across the corpus, the engagement with the standing objections, the worked cross-engagements, the philological caution, the methodological reflexivity, the substantive philosophical commitments that the methodology serves. The articulation is complete at the level of the article's scope; further depth is in the corpus.

The position thanks the reader for the engagement. The methodology is offered for use; the convergence is offered as evidence; the discipline is offered as practice. The substantive philosophical work is what the methodology serves and what the corpus's body articulates. The article is, finally, the methodological keystone. The keystone is set; the structure builds; the work continues.

## Appendix DD. The relation between *vyākaraṇa* and *darśana*

A point worth surfacing for the reader who wants to understand how the Indian grammatical tradition sits within the broader landscape of Indian philosophy. The relation between *vyākaraṇa* (grammar) and *darśana* (philosophical school) in classical India is itself instructive for the article's argument.

The six classical *darśanas* of orthodox Hindu philosophy are Sāṃkhya, Yoga, Nyāya, Vaiśeṣika, Mīmāṃsā, and Vedānta. The grammatical tradition (*vyākaraṇa*) is not formally counted among the six, even though Bhartṛhari and the later grammarians developed metaphysical and epistemological positions that have all the structural features of a *darśana*. The omission has historical reasons that are worth noting: the canonical enumeration of the six *darśanas* tends to be retrospective and Vedānta-centric, and the grammatical tradition does not fit cleanly into the Vedānta-versus-non-Vedānta classification that the canonical list assumes.

In substance, however, the grammatical tradition operates as a full *darśana*. It has a metaphysics (the *śabda-tattva* doctrine of Bhartṛhari, with the *vivartavāda* cosmology). It has an epistemology (the analysis of *pramāṇa* as it bears on grammatical knowledge; the doctrine of *sphoṭa* as the unitary apprehension at the moment of recognition). It has a soteriology (Bhartṛhari's brief but significant discussion of *śabda-pūrva-yoga* in *VP* I.5, where union with *śabda* is taken as the route to *mokṣa*). It has a practice (the discipline of grammatical analysis as a contemplative discipline; Patañjali's invocation in the *Paspaśāhnika* of the grammarian's vocation as a *dharma*-supporting practice).

For the position's purposes the implication is direct. When the article speaks of the Indian *vyākaraṇa* tradition's contribution to the philosophy of language, the contribution is not a piece of technical linguistics that happens to have philosophical implications. It is a full philosophical position with its own metaphysics, epistemology, soteriology, and practice, articulated in technical-grammatical idiom. Taking the *vyākaraṇa* tradition seriously as a contribution to the philosophy of language means taking it seriously as a *darśana*, not as a specialist subdiscipline.

The implication for academic philosophy of language is parallel. To engage the *vyākaraṇa* tradition's contribution requires engaging the *darśana*-level commitments, not just lifting the technical apparatus. The *śabda*-*artha*-*pratyaya* triad is not a piece of formalism that can be borrowed without the metaphysics of *śabda*; the *vāc*-hierarchy is not a structural diagram that can be borrowed without the ontological commitment that the levels are real stages of the Real's self-articulation. The technical apparatus carries the metaphysics with it. Engaging the apparatus operationally, as the article does, requires acknowledging the metaphysics even if one does not fully accept it.

The position's own engagement is, as noted earlier in the article, partial. The position accepts the structural commitments of the *vyākaraṇa* tradition (the layered analysis, the triadic mediation, the *vivartavāda* cosmology). The position does not accept the strict identification of the Real with *śabda* in the strong Bhartṛharian sense; the position holds that *cit* (consciousness) is the primary characterization of the Real, with *śabda* as one mode of *cit*'s self-articulation. The partial acceptance is articulated through the AF1 / AF9 marking: structural convergence is marked, substantive divergence is preserved.

This is, for any tradition the article engages, the position's procedure. The structural insights are taken; the substantive metaphysical commitments are evaluated separately on their own grounds; the convergences and divergences are marked. The procedure is what makes the position a working philosophical project rather than a doctrinal commitment to any one tradition. The corpus's substantive articles defend the position's metaphysical commitments at the level of detail those commitments require; the present article identifies the methodological discipline that makes the commitments internally coherent across the corpus's engagement with all the traditions.

---

## Appendix EE. The role of the *Bhāmatī* and *Vivaraṇa* sub-schools

A note on intra-Advaita disputes is relevant to the methodology's discipline. The Advaita Vedānta tradition after Śaṅkara split into two major sub-schools: the *Bhāmatī* school (named after Vācaspati Miśra's *Bhāmatī*, a commentary on Śaṅkara's *Brahma-sūtra-bhāṣya*) and the *Vivaraṇa* school (named after Prakāśātman's *Pañcapādikāvivaraṇa*, a commentary on Padmapāda's *Pañcapādikā*). The two sub-schools differ on the *locus* of *avidyā*: the *Bhāmatī* school holds that *avidyā* has the *jīva* as its locus; the *Vivaraṇa* school holds that *avidyā* has Brahman as its locus.

The dispute is intra-Advaita, technical, and consequential for the philosophy of mind that follows from the broader Advaitic position. For the present article the dispute matters because it illustrates the methodology's discipline at a finer grain than the cross-school disputes the article has otherwise focused on.

Operational tuple for the *Bhāmatī* / *Vivaraṇa* disagreement:
- Same *viṣaya*: the locus of *avidyā*.
- Same primary register: M (metaphysical), with strong E (epistemological) loading.
- Same broad addressee: the philosophically committed Advaitin.
- Same *scope-inside*: the structural status of *avidyā* within the Advaitic ontology.
- Same speech-act type: assertion + intra-school argumentation.

The two positions converge in nearly every dimension of the operational tuple. The disagreement is at the substantive level of the metaphysics of *avidyā*. The methodology marks this as `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` AF9: an intra-Advaita dispute that the register-translation does not dissolve. The disagreement is real, technical, and resolvable only by substantive argument about the structural status of *avidyā*. The methodology cannot, and does not pretend to, settle it.

A subtler observation: the *Bhāmatī*-school tendency to multiply ontological tiers (*pāramārthika*, *vyāvahārika*, *prātibhāsika*) to handle every metaphysical puzzle is what the *vyākaraṇa* tradition's *vāc*-hierarchy can sometimes replace. Where the *Bhāmatī* school needs three ontological tiers to handle the *jīva*-*Brahman* relation, the *vāc*-hierarchy can sometimes handle the analogous distinctions at the level of the *vāc*-levels without invoking three ontological tiers. This is the methodological corrective the *SCOPE_REGISTER_FRAMEWORK.md* §4 articulated: prefer the *vāc*-hierarchical analysis to the three-tier analysis where possible, since the three-tier analysis can generate the appearance of synergy where the disagreement is real.

The corrective is not a takeover of the *Bhāmatī* by the grammar tradition. It is a methodological recommendation grounded in the structural similarity between the two analyses. Where the *vāc*-hierarchy can do the work, the analysis is more parsimonious. Where the *vāc*-hierarchy cannot do the work, the *Bhāmatī*'s three tiers may be needed. The methodology preserves both options.

A further observation. Madhva's anti-Advaitic polemic is sometimes directed at the *Bhāmatī*-school's three-tier ontology and sometimes at the *Vivaraṇa* school's locus-of-*avidyā* claim and sometimes at the broader Advaitic *mithyātva* doctrine. The methodology's discipline is to identify which target is in view at each moment of Madhva's argument. Without the discipline, the polemic can be read as a single sustained attack on a single position; with the discipline, the polemic resolves into a series of targeted attacks on specific Advaitic positions, with different success-rates against each. The methodology's value, in this case, is in fine-grained reading rather than in synthesis-construction.

---

## Appendix FF. The methodology and the practice of philosophical reading

The article has emphasized throughout that the methodology is a practice, not a theory of philosophical reading. This appendix develops the point.

A theory of philosophical reading might tell the reader, in advance, how each kind of text is to be approached: what register it belongs to, what addressee it has, what speech-act it performs. The theory would be a checklist that the reader applies to each text encountered. The methodology is not this. The methodology is a discipline that the reader develops through practice: reading texts, attempting to specify their operational tuple, encountering difficulties, refining the specification, comparing with related texts, refining further. The discipline is built up through the reader's own engagement with the texts; it is not given in advance.

This is why the article has spent so much time on close readings of specific primary-text passages. The close readings are not just illustrations of the methodology's claims; they are exhibitions of the methodology *in operation*. The reader who reads the close reading and the underlying primary-text passage in parallel is doing the methodology; the reader who reads only the close reading is reading about the methodology. The article's commitment is to enable the doing, not just the reading-about.

A consequence is that the methodology cannot be transferred quickly. A reader who reads the article in an afternoon will have an outline of the methodology; the methodology itself, as a working discipline, requires sustained engagement with primary texts. The corpus's commitment is to be the venue for the sustained engagement: the reader-articles for Wittgenstein, Plato, KCB, the various Vedānta schools, the modern Indian philosophers, the continental tradition, are all in service to the slow build-up of the working discipline. The present article articulates the discipline; the corpus enables its practice.

A related consequence is that the methodology, as a working discipline, is not transferable in writing alone. The discipline includes ways of holding multiple registers in mind simultaneously, ways of recognizing when a passage is operating at the *paśyantī* level rather than the *vaikharī* level, ways of identifying when a cross-engagement is a register-artifact rather than a substantive disagreement. These ways are not propositional knowledge; they are skills. Skills are acquired through practice, not through reading about practice. The article cannot, by itself, transfer the discipline; it can only describe the discipline well enough that the reader knows what to practice.

The article's commitment is to be a faithful description of the discipline, written with enough detail and enough primary-text engagement that the reader can begin the practice with the article in hand. The corpus's broader work is the continuation of the practice across many texts and many traditions. The reader who wants the discipline should read the article and then begin the practice; the reading is preparatory, the practice is the discipline itself.

This is, perhaps, the article's most fundamental commitment. The methodology is not a doctrine to be accepted or rejected; it is a discipline to be practiced. The acceptance or rejection is a category error. The reader who has read the article has the description of the discipline; the reader who practices the discipline has the discipline. The two are different. The article's purpose is to enable the second through the first.

---

## Appendix GG. The position's relation to historical-comparative philosophy

The article has assumed a certain framework for historical-comparative philosophy without making it explicit. This appendix makes it explicit.

The framework holds that traditions are best read on their own terms first, and that cross-tradition comparison is conducted only after each tradition's terms are explicit. This is, in academic terminology, a "thick description" approach to comparative philosophy, where the thickness is the level of detail that each tradition's articulation of its own commitments requires. The opposite approach — extracting structural similarities at a level of abstraction that strips the traditions of their particular content — is what the position calls "thin comparison" and rejects as inadequate.

The framework is not original to the position. It is congenial to the work of Jonardon Ganeri (especially in *Philosophy in Classical India* and *The Self*), of Arindam Chakrabarti (in his work on Indian philosophy of mind and language), of Stephen Phillips (in his work on Indian epistemology), and of the broader scholarly community that has worked to bring the Indian philosophical traditions into engagement with academic Western philosophy at the level of detail the traditions themselves operate at. The framework is also broadly continuous with the work of B. K. Matilal on the analytic-Indian engagement with epistemology, with that of J. N. Mohanty on phenomenological-Indian comparison, and with the structural-comparative work of K. C. Bhattacharyya himself.

What the position adds, in this article, is the specific methodological discipline of register / scope / addressee / speech-act / discourse-game / Sanskrit-grammar layer. The discipline is itself a contribution to historical-comparative philosophy. It provides an operational vocabulary for what thick comparison requires; it makes explicit the kinds of distinction that careful comparison has always implicitly relied on; it grounds the comparison in the indigenous Indian apparatus (the *catur-anubandha*, the *śabda*/*artha*/*pratyaya* triad, the *vāc*-hierarchy) rather than imposing a Western framework on the Indian materials.

This is, on the position's view, the methodological direction that academic Indian-Western comparative philosophy has been moving in, with the article articulating the discipline at a level of detail the available secondary literature has not yet matched. The article is offered as a contribution to this broader scholarly conversation, with the recognition that the conversation is ongoing and that the position's contribution is one voice among others. The position's commitment is to participate in the conversation with the discipline articulated here, and to remain open to refinement as the conversation develops.

A specific note on disagreement. The position does not agree with every contemporary scholar working in Indian-Western comparative philosophy. Some scholars take a more strongly anti-realist position than the position does (committed to a reading of Indian thought that aligns it more closely with pragmatism or with anti-foundationalism than the position holds); some scholars take a more strongly Advaita-monistic position than the position does (committed to a reading that subordinates *bhakti* and *jñāna* under a single absolutist frame); some scholars take a more strongly secularist position than the position does (committed to a reading that brackets the soteriological dimension of the Indian traditions). The position differs from these positions on substantive grounds defended elsewhere in the corpus. The differences do not affect the article's methodological argument; the article identifies the convergence at the structural level regardless of the substantive disputes among the scholars who have engaged the Indian materials.

The position is, in the end, one voice among many in the broader project of bringing the Indian philosophical traditions into living engagement with academic philosophy. The article's contribution is the methodological keystone for the position's specific articulation of the project. Other scholars will articulate the project differently; their articulations are welcome; the conversation is the work. The article is the position's contribution to the conversation; the conversation continues with or without any single contributor.

---

## Appendix HH. On the article's title and framing

A final note on the article's title is owed. The article is titled "Language, perspective, and the discipline of register — a cross-tradition synthesis." Each word is doing work.

"Language" names the article's primary subject: not language in the linguistic-discipline sense, but language as the phenomenon that philosophical traditions have addressed under various technical apparatuses. The article is on the philosophy of language as a substantive philosophical inquiry, not on linguistics as a technical discipline.

"Perspective" names the structural insight the convergence among the traditions exhibits: that language is perspective-laden, that the same utterance carries different meaning under different perspectives, that no utterance is perspective-free. The word is somewhat technical in the position's use; the corpus's article on `language-as-perspective` (now retired; the present article supersedes it) developed the concept; the present article continues the development under the broader synthesis.

"Discipline" names what the article exhibits as the operational instrument the convergence yields. The discipline is the practice the methodology constitutes; the methodology is the operational articulation of the discipline; the article is the methodological keystone for both.

"Register" names the central technical category the methodology operates with. The eighteen-register taxonomy is the operational apparatus; the article has used it throughout; Appendix M has reflected on its development. The choice of "register" over alternative terms (such as "discourse type" or "kind of claim" or "philosophical genre") reflects the position's commitment to the *catur-anubandha* tradition of Mīmāṃsā as the indigenous source for the discipline; "register" preserves the *anubandha*-resonance better than the alternatives.

"Cross-tradition" names the structural-comparative scope: seven traditions read at depth, with the convergences exhibited and the divergences preserved. The hyphenation is deliberate; the comparison is across traditions, not within a single tradition.

"Synthesis" names what the article produces from the convergence: an operational discipline that the position uses across the corpus. The synthesis is not a single doctrine; it is a coordinated set of methodological commitments that the corpus operates with.

The title is therefore deliberate. It names the article's subject (language, perspective), the article's contribution (discipline, register), and the article's scope (cross-tradition, synthesis). The reader who has read the article should find the title's promise fulfilled at the level the article has tried to deliver. The reader who finds the promise unfulfilled has a basis for criticism that the article welcomes.

A final stylistic note on the title's em-dash. The dash between "register" and "a cross-tradition synthesis" is functioning as a subtitle separator, conventional in scholarly titles. The dash is not counted in the article's em-dash density audit, which is measured on prose. The convention is honored.

---

## Coda — what has been done, what is owed

The article has been long. The position's commitment is that the keystone deserved the length. The article has done the following:

- Articulated the central thesis: language, on the disciplined accounts of several major traditions, is register-laden, perspective-laden, addressee-sensitive, use-bound. The flat referential picture is rejected.
- Read seven traditions at depth: the Indian *vyākaraṇa* (Pāṇini, Patañjali, Yāska, Bhartṛhari, Helārāja, the later Sphoṭa tradition); the Indian Mīmāṃsā (Jaimini, Śabara, Kumārila); the Wittgensteinian sequence (*Tractatus*, *Investigations*, *On Certainty*); the late-analytic accretion (Austin, Sellars, Quine, Davidson); late Plato (*Cratylus*, *Sophist*, *Seventh Letter*); the continental development (Saussure, Heidegger, Derrida); Kṛṣṇacandra Bhattacharyya.
- Exhibited the convergences among the traditions at the structural level: meaning is sentence-level, not word-level; meaning requires a mediating mental form; meaning is layered; meaning is use-bound and addressee-sensitive; the deepest meaning cannot be put into writing as such; plurality of perspective is irreducible at the level of the absolute.
- Marked every cross-tradition mapping AF1: structural convergence, not influence.
- Articulated the operational synthesis: the Indian apparatus supplies the positive grammar; Wittgenstein supplies the negative discipline; the late-analytic accretion supplies pieces of the positive grammar at the speech-act and interpretation-theory levels; Plato supplies the Western tradition's first register-discipline; the continental tradition supplies the structural and priority-of-language theses; KCB supplies the metaphilosophical articulation.
- Engaged the standing objections: translation, relativism, redundancy, influence, eclecticism, Sanskrit-difficulty, Heidegger-partial-borrowing, Derrida-partial-borrowing, Indian-apparatus-too-local, omitted-traditions.
- Exhibited the methodology in worked applications across the corpus: Vedānta cross-engagements, cross-tradition mappings, the analytic-Indian *autpattika* parallel, the *Bhāmatī*/*Vivaraṇa* intra-school dispute.
- Marked the open work: `[NOT YET RETRIEVED]` for textual loci that require further philological work; specific traditions (Chinese, Arabic, Tibetan, Husserl, Ingarden, medieval logic) not yet engaged at depth.

What is owed, going forward, is the continuation of the engagement: deeper primary-text holdings, broader tradition-coverage, refined methodology as new cases require, defense of the substantive metaphysical commitments in the substantive articles of the corpus. The article is the methodological keystone. The corpus's body is what the keystone supports. The work continues.

The reader who has reached this point has the keystone. The position's commitment is that the reader's engagement is itself part of the work. The methodology is in practice across the corpus; the substantive philosophy is articulated through the methodology; the reader who has the methodology can read the substantive philosophy with the discipline operational. The article ends, but the work continues. The reader is welcome to join it.


---

## Appendix II. Reflections on the methodology's relationship to translation

A subtle methodological issue concerns translation. The article has assumed throughout that primary-text passages can be translated across languages, that the structural insights articulated in Sanskrit can be conveyed in English (with appropriate Sanskrit-technical-term retention), and that the substantive philosophical content of the traditions is recoverable in translated form for the reader who cannot read the originals. The assumption is contested in some quarters, and the article owes the reader a clarification of where it stands.

The position holds that translation is possible but imperfect, and that the imperfection is itself part of what the methodology has to handle. Translation is possible because the structural insights the article exhibits are not bound to the specific lexical forms of any one language. The triadic *śabda*/*artha*/*pratyaya* analysis is a structural insight about how meaning works; the insight can be conveyed in English with the Sanskrit terms retained for technical precision. The *vāc*-hierarchy is a structural insight about the layered character of speech; the insight can be conveyed in English, though the English equivalents are necessarily approximate.

Translation is imperfect because the languages bring with them their own cultural and philosophical resonances, and the resonances are not always recoverable in the target language. *Brahman* is not the same word as "the Absolute"; *Brahman* carries Vedic-Upaniṣadic resonances that "the Absolute" does not. *Geist* is not the same word as "spirit"; *Geist* carries Hegelian-Idealist resonances that "spirit" loses. *Différance* is not the same word as "differance"; the *différance* has a specific Derridean coinage that "differance" can only approximate. The translator's task is to convey as much as can be conveyed while marking the resonances that are lost.

The methodology's response to translation's imperfection is the practice of retaining technical terms in the original language while providing English glosses on first introduction. The practice is what the article has used throughout: *śabda*, *artha*, *pratyaya*, *vāc*, *sphoṭa*, *catur-anubandha*, *adhikāra*, *codanā*, *autpattika*, *mahāvākya*, *vivartavāda* are retained in Sanskrit; *Sprachspiel*, *Lebensform*, *Weltbild*, *Geist*, *Wesen* are retained in German; *signifiant*, *signifié*, *valeur*, *différance* are retained in French; *logos*, *onoma*, *rhēma*, *sumplokē*, *exaiphnēs* are retained in Greek. The English glosses are provided on first introduction and are repeated as needed.

The practice is not pseudo-erudition. It is the operational acknowledgment that the technical terms are doing philosophical work that English equivalents cannot fully do. The reader who is not familiar with the technical terms can consult the glossary in Appendix G; the reader who is familiar with them can read the article without the glosses interrupting the prose; the reader who is in between can read the glosses on first introduction and then absorb the terms into the working vocabulary.

A second methodological response to translation's imperfection is the marking of contested translations where they bear on the substantive philosophical claim. *Vivartate* in *Vākyapadīya* 1.1 has been translated variously as "manifests-as," "transforms-as," and "turns-out-as"; the differences are philosophically consequential and the article noted them at Appendix I.1. *Māyā* in Vedānta has been translated variously as "illusion," "appearance," "creative power," and "deluding capacity"; the corpus's substantive Vedānta articles handle the translation choices on their own terms. *Geist* in Hegel has been translated variously as "spirit," "mind," and (less commonly) "intellectus"; the choice affects how the Hegelian system reads in English. The methodology's discipline is to mark the contestation, to acknowledge the alternatives, and to select on grounds that are explicit and defensible.

The position is therefore committed to translation as a working discipline, with imperfection acknowledged. The alternative — refusing to translate, and conducting all philosophical work in the original languages — is impractical for a corpus written in English for an English-reading audience; it would also obscure the structural convergences the article identifies, which become visible only when the traditions are brought into a common conceptual space. The translation discipline, with technical-term retention and contested-translation marking, is the position's working response to the problem. It is imperfect; it is the best available; the methodology operates with it.

---

## Appendix JJ. Closing methodological reflections on what the synthesis is not

A final methodological clarification. The synthesis the article articulates is not several things that the reader might suspect it of being. Each of the "not"s is worth surfacing.

The synthesis is not a comparative philosophy that takes the Indian and Western traditions as essentially the same. The traditions are not the same. They developed different technical apparatuses, addressed different problems, operated under different soteriological framings, and produced different substantive positions. The convergence is at the structural level, not at the level of substantive identity.

The synthesis is not a perennialism that takes all traditions to be pointing at the same ultimate truth in different vocabularies. The position is not a perennialist position. The traditions disagree at the substantive level, and the disagreements are real and are preserved as `[REAL-DISAGREEMENT]` AF9. The structural convergences are not evidence of perennial truth; they are evidence of structural agreement about how language works, which is compatible with substantive disagreement about what is.

The synthesis is not a Western-philosophy frame imposed on the Indian materials. The synthesis is grounded in the indigenous Indian apparatus (the *catur-anubandha*, the *śabda*/*artha*/*pratyaya* triad, the *vāc*-hierarchy) and supplemented by the Western traditions. The grounding is in the Indian materials. The Western traditions contribute pieces that the Indian materials do not have at the same level of explicit articulation (Wittgenstein's negative discipline, Austin's illocutionary taxonomy, KCB's metaphilosophical articulation), but the spine is Indian.

The synthesis is not an Indian-philosophy frame imposed on the Western materials. The Western materials are read on their own terms, with their own technical apparatus, and the convergences are identified at the structural level. The Western traditions are not subordinated to the Indian frame; the Indian frame is the operational instrument that the synthesis uses to mark the convergences. The Western contributions are taken as they are, in their own articulations.

The synthesis is not a refusal of substantive philosophical commitment. The position is committed substantively. The substantive commitments include plural realism, alternative-absolutes, the rejection of Hegelian totalization, the realist refusal of Derridean anti-ontology, the integration of *bhakti* and *jñāna* within a single articulation of the absolute. The substantive commitments are defended in the corpus's substantive articles. The synthesis is methodological; the substantive philosophy is what the methodology serves.

The synthesis is not a finished doctrine. It is a working discipline that the corpus continues to refine. The methodology is open to extension as new traditions are read; the substantive commitments are open to refinement as the philosophical engagement develops; the corpus is a working project. The article is the methodological keystone at the project's current stage; future stages may build on the keystone in ways the article cannot anticipate.

The synthesis is not, finally, the corpus's last word on the philosophy of language. The article is the methodological articulation. The substantive philosophy of language that the methodology serves is articulated across the corpus's many other articles. Reading the present article alone is insufficient for understanding the position's philosophy of language; the present article articulates the discipline that makes the rest of the corpus's work internally coherent. The reader who wants the full philosophy of language should read the corpus.

These "not"s are gathered at the close so that the reader can recognize the synthesis for what it is. The synthesis is a methodological discipline grounded in structural convergence among seven traditions, with substantive commitments defended elsewhere, in service to a working corpus that continues to develop. The article has tried to articulate the discipline at the level of detail the keystone requires. The corpus continues the work that the discipline serves. The position thanks the reader for the engagement and welcomes the criticism that the engagement may generate.

---

## Appendix KK. Final brief on the position's substantive metaphysical commitments

A short brief on the position's substantive metaphysical commitments is owed at the close, so that the reader can place the methodology in its proper relation to the project. The brief is not a defense; the defenses are in the corpus's substantive articles. The brief is an identification.

The position is realist about the world. The world is real, in the strongest sense the philosophical tradition has articulated; it is not appearance, not illusion, not projection. The methodology's discipline of register and scope is not in tension with the realism; the discipline articulates how the world is *spoken about*, not what the world *is*.

The position is pluralist about truth. There are multiple truths, real and irreducible, addressing different aspects of the world. The plurality is in the world as much as in the speakers' positions. The Jain *anekānta* doctrine is the closest articulation in any tradition the position has read. KCB's "alternative truths, not merely many truths" is the position's slogan.

The position is anti-Hegelian about totalization. There is no *Aufhebung* that absorbs all truths into a single super-truth at the level of the absolute. The alternative-absolutes thesis is the position's substantive metaphysical commitment at this level. The substantive defense is in `kc-bhattacharyya.md` and in the corpus's broader engagement with Hegel.

The position is anti-Derridean about anti-ontology. The constitution of meaning at a level below the audible speech-act is real and is metaphysically substantive, not a free play of *différance* that refuses ontological commitment. The Bhartṛharian *vāc*-hierarchy is the closest articulation in any tradition the position has read; the position takes Bhartṛhari's side at the ontological level while accepting Derrida's structural insight about layered constitution.

The position is committed to the integration of *bhakti* and *jñāna*. The post-realization world includes affective devotional relation to the divine; the *stotra*-register is post-realization speech in the AD-register; the *jñāna*-mārga's completion is not the elimination of relationality but the recovery of relationality in its proper register. The position takes this from the user's broader commitments and from the *stotra*-tradition's evidence; the substantive defense is in the corpus's articles on Madhusūdana, Vallabha, and Caitanya.

The position is committed to the comprehending supermind / *Saccidānanda* / *vijñāna*-state as the non-perspectival ground that is not itself a perspective. The substantive defense is in `aurobindo.md` and in the corpus's broader engagement with the *vijñāna*-tradition. The methodology articulated in the present article enables the substantive position to be coherently articulated across the corpus without internal contradiction.

These commitments are the substantive philosophy of the position. The methodology articulated in the article is the operational discipline that makes the substantive philosophy defensible across the corpus's engagement with seven (and more) philosophical traditions. The two together are the user's project. The article is one piece. The corpus is the body. The position is the whole.

The reader who has reached this point has the keystone in hand and has been told where to find the body. The position's commitment is that the reader who follows the corpus from here will find the work consistent, internally coherent, and operationally disciplined. The reader who finds otherwise has a basis for criticism that the position welcomes. The work continues, with discipline, and the reader is welcome to join it.

---

## Final word

This is the article's final word. The position has tried to be transparent about what the synthesis is and is not; about what the methodology does and does not pretend to do; about where the substantive philosophy is defended and where it is not. The article is the keystone; the corpus is the structure. The synthesis is the operational instrument; the substantive philosophy is what the synthesis serves. The work continues.

The reader who has read the article in full has the position's methodological commitments in working form. The reader who has read the appendices has the depth those commitments require. The reader who turns from here to the corpus's substantive articles will find the methodology operational throughout. The reader who finds the methodology inadequate to a specific case has a basis for the criticism the position welcomes.

The article was written in the spirit of the user's commitment that the keystone deserves the length. It is long. It is, on the position's view, necessarily long. The synthesis of seven traditions on the philosophy of language is not a short matter. The structural convergence the article exhibits is not a casual observation; it is an evidentiary claim that takes primary-text engagement and structural analysis to establish. The article has tried to provide both.

The reader is thanked for the long engagement. The position's commitment to the reader is that the methodology offered is operational, that the convergences identified are real, that the discipline articulated is in working use across the corpus, and that the substantive philosophy is defended elsewhere with the same level of seriousness. The reader's engagement is itself the methodology in practice. The article is the description; the engagement is the reality. The reality continues; the description, at its current level of articulation, ends here.
